THE ROUND TABLE
A Quarterly Review of the politics of the British Empire.
The March number contains many excellent articles.
Of special interest are those entitled The Victory that
Will End War; Three Doctrines in Conflict; Pales-
tine and Jewish Nationalism; America’s War Aims:
and the usual useful summaries and criticisms ot
political events in the United Kingdom and the
Dominions. Of note amongst the latter is one on the
Great Strike in Australia.
Price 2/6 per copy. 10/- per annum.
Macmillan & Co.. Ltd., Russell Street, Melbourne.

unnecessary. Hudson’s Eumenthol Jujubes
are most useful to those who suffer from

The Red Page
Georgian Poetry
“They.”
The Bishop tells us: “When the hoys come
hack
They ivill not he the same; for they'll hare
fought
In a just cause: they lead the last attack
On Anti-Christ; their comrades’ hlood has
bought
New right to breed an honorable race.
They have challenged Death and dared him
face to face.”
‘‘Wie’re none of us the same!” the hoys reply.
“For George lost both his legs; and Bill’s
stone blind;
Poor Jim’s shot through the lungs and like
to die:
And Bert’s gone syphilitic; you’ll not find
A chap who’s served that hasn’t found some
change.”
And the Bishop said: “The ways of God are
strange!”
, Siegfried Sassoon.
The Volunteer.
Here lies the clerk who half his life had
spent
Toiling at ledgers in a city grey.
Thinking that so his days would drift away
With no lance broken in life’s tournament:
Yet ever ’twixt the hooks and his bright eyes
The gleaming eagles of the legions came,
And horsemen, charging under phantopi skies,
Went thundering past beneath the oriflamme.
And now those waiting dreams are satisfied!;
From twilight to the halls of dawn he went;
His lance is broken; but he lies content
With that high hour, in which he lived and
died.
And falling thus, he wants no recompense,
Who found his battle in the last resort;
Nor needs he any hearse to bear him hence,
Who goes to join the men of Agincourt.
Herbert Asquitix.
It’s a Queer Time.
It’s hard to know if you’re alive or dead
When steel and fire go roaring through your
head.
One moment you’ll be crouching at your gun
Traversing, mowing heaps down half in fun.
The next, you choke and clutch at your right
breast —•
No time to think —leave all—and off you
g 0... . .
To Treasure Islands where the Spice ivmds
blow', ,
To lovely groves of mango, quince and lime —
Breathe no good-bye, but ho, for the Red
West!
It’s a queer time.
You’re charging madly at them yelling '‘Fag!’’
When somehow something gives and your feet
drag.
You fall and strike your head; yet feel no
pain
And find... .you’re digging tunnels through
the hay
In the Big Barn, ’cause it’s a rainy day.
Oh, springy hay, and lovely beams to climb!
You’re back in the old sailor suit again.
It’s a queer time.
OY you’ll be dozing safe in your dug-out —
A great roar —the trench shakes and falls
about —
You’re struggling, gasping, struggling, then
.... hullo!
Elsie comes tripping gaily down the trench,
Hanky to nose —that lyddite makes a stench —
Getting her pinafore all over grime.
Funny! because she died ten years ago!
It’s a queer time.
The trouble is, things happen much too
quick;
Up jump the Bodies, rifles thump and click,
You stagger, and the whole scene fades
away:
Even good Christians don’t like passing
straight
From Tipperary or their Hymn of Hate
To Alleluiah-chanting, and the chime
O* golden harps... .and... .I’m not well
to-day
It’s a queer time.
Robert Graves.
Lament.
We who are left, lioiv shall we look again
Happily on the sun or feel the rain
Without remembering how they who went
Ungrudgingly and spent
Their lives for us, loved, too, the sun and
rain f
A bird among the rain-wet lilac sings—
But we, how shall we turn to little things
And listen to the birds and winds and
streams
Made holy by their dreams,
Not feel the heart-break in the heart of
thin [is? ■
Wilfrid Wilson Gibson.
From “Georgian Poetry. 1916-1917.” (The
Poetry Bookshop, Gondon.)
The third volume of Georgian Poetry
(1916-7) (The Poetry Bookshop. Devon-
shire-strect, Theobalds-road, Ix>ndon) in-
cludes selections from IS writers, nine ot
whom appear in this series for the first time.
But several of these poems have appeared
in one or more of the other similar antho-
logies. Georgian Poetry . however, includes
many poems that do not treat of the war,
though none, it seems to this page, quite
so suitable for quotation. All lovers ot
dogs will admire a poem, too long to quote,
by J. G. Squire, entitled “To a Bulldog,’
btginning thus: —
We shan’t see Willy any more, Mamie,
He won’t be coming any more:
He came back once and again and again,
But he won’t get leave any more.
We looked from the window, and there was liis cab ;
And we ran downstairs, like a streak,
And he said: “Hullo, you bad dog,’ and you
crouched to the floor,
Paralysed to hear him speak... .
The same poet is responsible for a cubist
sort of thing, beginning:—
The*lily of Malud is born in secret mud.
It is breathed like a word in a little dark ravine
Where no bird was ever heard and no beast was ever
seen,
And the leaves are never stirred by the panther s
velvet sheen....
Ho hurtles on like that for pages. Harold
Munro agreeably works the vein of The,
Blue Bird in “Every Thing.” Man, he says,
Has failed to hear the sympathetic cadi
Of Crockery and Cutlery,
and he makes vocal his Bed, “the old im-
petuous Gas," even the Soot and the Putty,
and concludes that there is not much to
choose
Between the purposes of you and me,
And your eventual Rubbish Heap, and mine.
Ralph Hodgson is represented by three
delightful poems, one on “Babylon" begin-
ning : —•
If you could bring her glories back !
You gentle sirs who sift the dust
And bm row in the mould and must
Of Babylon for bric-rl-brac;
Who catalogue and pigeon-hole
The faded splendors of her soul
And put her greatness under glass—
If you could bring her past to pass !
There is good poetry a-plenty in England
now.
Chesterton, Historian
The popular histories of England, G.-Iv.
Chesterton explains in A Short History of
England (Chatto and Windus, per Melville
and Mullen, Melb.), are written against the
people: in them the populace is either
ignored or elaborately proved to have been
wrong. Even Green’s Short History of the
English People is too short for the people
to be properly mentioned. Especially is
Chesterton concerned _ with the neglect of
medieval civilisation in these popular his-
tories. We hear about the Great Charter,
but we are not taught that “the whole stuff
of the Middle Ages was stiff with the’ parch-
ment of charters. The carpenter heard of
one charter given to the barons, and mostly
in the interest of the barons; the carpenter
did not hear of any of the charters given to
carpenters The boy and girl reading the
stock,..simplified histories of the schools prac-
tically never heard of such a thing as a
burgher, until he appears in a shirt with a
nobse round his neck.”
Chesterton’s qualifications as a historian
need some searching for; but his attempt,
which abounds in wild guesswork and is
not free from errors, is interesting and, be-
cause he gives the ordinary bloke a show,
is valuable. For Australian children still
have to memorise the important fact that
some monarch or other died from a surfeit
of lampreys.
Chesterton emphasies the' fact that France
and England are Roman remains. Britain
was directly Roman for 400 years—“longer
than she has been Protestant, and very
much longer than she has been industrial.”
And he insists that popular fable is always
nearer the historical truth than the edu-
cated opinion of to-day. So that the place
of Cceur de Lion in fable is far more like
his place in true history. “Richard, as the
typical Crusader, did make a momentous
difference to England by gaining glory in
the East, instead of devoting himself con-
scientiously t domestic politics in the ex-
emplary manner of King John. The acci-
dent of his military genius and prestige gave
England something which it kept for 400
years—the reputation of being in the very
vanguard of chivalry.”
This medieval modern Chesterton asks us
nor to look at the shadows but at the common
daylight of the Middle Ages. ‘ln the arts
of peace, and in the type of production, the
Middle Ages stand singular and unique....
The dynasties and the Parliament pass like
a changing cloud and across a stable and
fruitful landscape The medievals not
onlv had self-government, but their selt-
tovernment was self-made. Modern local
government always comes from above: it is
granted or imposed. “Men banded together
in guilds and parishes long before Coca!
Government Acts were dreamed of in
modern constitutional countries there are
practically no political institutions thus
given by the people; all are received by the
people. There is only one thing that stands
in our midst, attenuated and threatened, but
enthroned in some power like a ghost or the
Middle Ages: the Trades Unions.”
So .Chesterton traverses the long course
of English history, and gives lus own inter-
pretation of its events. It may not be the
true interpretation, hut, at all events, it is
a bright one. Thus, “We talk of the disso-
lution of the monastries, hut what occurred
was the dissolution of the whole of the old
civilisation. Lawyers and lackeys and
money-lenders, the meanest of lucky men,
looted the art and economies of the Middle
Ages like thieves robbing a church. Their
names (when they did not change them)
became the names of the great dukes and
marquises of our own day.”
Inevitably “the spacious days of Queen
Elizabeth” were.' to Chesterton, not spacious
at all. England did not then realise that she
was groat; she first realised that she was
small. “England achieved, at the beginning
of her modern history, that one thing human
imagination will always find heroic —the
story of a small nationality. The business of
the Armada was to her what Bannockburn
was to the Scots, or Mnjuba to the Boers —a
victory that astonished even the victors....
It was the greatness of Spain that was the
glory of England. It is only when we realise
that the English were, by comparison, as
dingy, as undeveloped, as petty and provin-
cial as'the Boers, that we can appreciate the
height of their defiance or the splendor of
their escape.” And in a noble passage Ches-
terton tells that story. “The uncanny com-
pleteness and abrupt silence that swallowed
that prodigy touched a nerve that has never
ceased to vibrate.”
Of the Irish question Chesterton wisely
says that it is a tragic necessity that the
Irish should remember England’s treatment
of Ireland ; but that it is far more tragic that
the English forgot it. Me speaks of the
accession of George of Hanover as the
entrance into England of “something hardly
mentioned in medieval or Renascence writ-
ing, except as one mentions a Hottentot —the
barbarian from behind the Rhine.” In the
American rebellion Chesterton finds that
America forced the quarrel. “She was not
thinking of her wrongs as a colony, but
already of her rights as a republic We
did not really drive away the American
colonists, nqr were they driven. They were
led on by a light that went before. That
light came from France, like the armies of
Lafayette that came to the help of Washing-
ton.”
In England’s historical rivalry with
France, Chesterton points out one of his-
tory’s ironies. This rivalry was due chiefly
to England’s desire to keep any foreigner
from menacing her from the Flemish coast;
a secondary cause was the two nations’
colonial rivalry. Now “in order to keep the
French out of Flanders we flung ourselves
with increasing enthusiasm into a fraternity
with' the Germans. We purposely fed and
pampered the power which was destined' in
the future to devour Belgium as France
would never have devoured it. and threaten
us across the sea with terrors of which no
Frenchman would ever dream.”
Lighting up this chronicle of Chesterton’s
ideas are glowing English. That
describing the defeat of the Armada is
equalled only by this of Nelson, who “died
with his stars on his bosom and his heart
upon his sleeve” :
“There is no word for the memory of
Nelson, except to call him mythical. The
very hour of his death, the very name of
his ship, are touched with that epic com-
pleteness which critics call the long arm of
coincidence and prophets the hand, of God.
.... And he remains the incarnation of a
spirit in the English that is purely poetic;
so poetic that it fancies itself a thousand
things, and sometimes even fancies itself
prosaic. At a recent date, in an age of
reason, in a country already calling itself
dull and business-like, with top-hats and
factory chimneys already beginning to rise
like towers of funereal efficiency, this
country clergyman’s son moved to the last
in a luminous cloud and acted a fairy tale.
... .In outward action he led his ships to
victory and died upon a foreign sea ; but
symbolically he established something inde-
scribable and intimate, something that
sounds like a native proverb; he was the
man who burnt his ships, and who for ever
set the Thames on fire.”
In discussing modern English politics
Chesterton is at his best. He suggests that
the working-class was allowed votes only
when it had been rendered too w r eak to use
them. “The old parliamentary oligarchy*
abandoned their first line of trenches be-
cause they had by that time constructed a
second line of defence. It consisted in the
concentration of colossal political funds in
the private and irresponsible power of the
politicians, collected by the sale of peerages
and more important tilings, and expended on
the jerrymandering of the enormously
expensive elections. In the presence of
this inner obstacle a vote became about as
valuable as a railway ticket when there is
a permanent block oh the line. The facade
and outward form* of this new secret gov-
ernment is the merely mechanical applica-
tion of what is called the Party System.
The Party System does not consist, as some
suppose, of two parties, but of one. If
there were two real parties, there could be
no system.” .
Concluding his tale, which, despite Ches-
terton’s tendency to fireworks and his slap-
dash methods, has an inherent coherency and
a moral, he comes to the war. “The English
poor, broken in every revolt, bullied in every
fashion, long despoiled of property, and now
being despoiled of liberty, entered history
with a noise of trumpets, and turned them-
selves in two years into one of the iron armies
of the world.” But he fears the spread of
Prussian regimentation into England: he
sees the Belloc bunyip at the end of the
passage—the Servile State. _ “And there are
moods in which a man, considering that con-
clusion of our story, is half inclined to wish
that the wave of Teutonic barbarism had
washed out us and our armies together; and
that the world should never know anything
more of the last of the English, except that
they died for liberty.” . • , ,
Chesterton needn’t fear: Labor in England
-—and elsewhereI—will 1 —will be able to look alter
itself when the war is won.
The industrious Arthur Wright obliges
with another Bookstall racing yarn. Over
tlu Odds. A freak will necessitating the
marriage of an unpleasant bookie before he is
40, a missing husband, and a racehorse which
the reader instinctively knows will win that
race in the last chapter, lead up to a com-
plicated plot full of surprises. The author
does this sort of thing deftly and with verve.
Australian
Grace Ethel Martyr, author of After-
words and 0.1 . (Aust. Authors Agency,
Melb., Is.), writes careful verse on war
themes; but there seems to this page too
great an insistence upon the reception in
Heaven awaiting most of our Billjims. We
are shown him “pressing up the Great
White Way,” received by The Christ with
welcoming'hands; or entering Heaven, and
“smiling to find it home, a dear familiar
place of friends and light”; or led by an
angel over “cloth of gold,” with crimson and
royal purple burning and glowing over Ins
happy head, and blinding light so strong
that it is “like a shout of triumph” ; or joy-
ously taking his place “in that White Com-
pany” ; or held by Christ in His heart; or
standing before the throne waiting for One
to call his name. This familiarity with
Heaven, this definiteness about the after-
wards, lowers the poetic worth of her verse.
But Grace Martyr can do better than that.
There is a poetic thought in “Westward :
The mellow moonbeams lend their loveliness
And lay their limpid light across the sea—
A path of silver stretching to the West,
A shining highway to the veiled unknown,
Across the shimmering ocean’s gentle breast
Is it the night wind’s silken-soft caress
That stirs the murmuring waters dreamily?
Or has some happy soul in passing, flown
Across the waves? Perchance his eager feet
With swift and fleeting touch have lightly pressed
The silver sea —the wrinkling ripples seem
Like shadowy footprints ! Surely, it were meet
That such a burnished beckoning path should
gleam
To lead the hero homewards, to his rest.
And in “Rain” the author has very cun-
ningly caught the monotony of her desola-
tion by a repetition of rime and phrase:
I hear the sound of falling, falling rain,
Like dead hands beating on the window-pane,
And still it sobs and sobs the same refrain —
That he is gone nor will he come again.
Perhaps on such a drear night he has lain
Out in the darkness there, a'mong the slain
Uncovered to the pitiless, falling rain.
I hear the moaning, crying wihd complain,
The sound of pattering drops beats on my brain,
That I shall never see his face again,
But lonelv wait and wait for him in vain,
And, weeping with the longing and the pain,
My tears are falling, falling with the rain.
He has preserved his honor without stain,
But all I hear is sobbing, driving rain,
That beats and taps upon the window-pane.
I only know he will not come again.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Magic Fiddler.
Oh, where are you leading me, fiddler of
mine,
With the tink and the clink of your little
gold strings?
My heart is adream on a bubble of wine,
And the lip of my cup has been kissed by a
king’s.
My senses are singing a fairyland song
To your music of mirth, and I cannot keep
still.
Oh, my feet beat a dance as you lead me
along
With your “Follow me, follow me over the
hill.”
What light on the water! What joy in the
air!
The stars are all nodding and keeping in
time;
And slim little evening with one shoulder
bare
Is running with bracelets and anklets
achime.
How glad are the glow-worms that light
the green wood!
And see what a feast at the tree’s mossy
root!
White nuts and brown spices and cinnamon
good
And blossom bells brimming with pink
pixie fruit!
On, on ! I will follow you over the way
By red rosy walls and the old rustic mill;
For my heart knows all tunes that your
fiddle can play,
As it knows all the laughter that twilight
can spill.
I cannot come back, for those little tunes
press
As close as the grass and the star-song
above —
Oh, it all comes of wearing a fairy-green
dress
And drinking sweet wine from a white cup
of love!
N.s.w.
ZORA CROSS.
In Green and Blue.
Green is for tired eyes,
Blue for a long thought;
Old and very wise
Are the lessons the sky taught.
But folly lives in the green;
And the green for me
Under the rustling sheen
Of a laughing tree.
Sea that is very blue
As I look from the hill,
Old and wise are you
When the winds are still;
Old and wise am I
When my heart is slow;
I have looked at the old sky,
And I know—l know.
Sea that is very
In the lapping pool,
Close have I looked and seen,
Where the weeds are cool,
How the dimpled folly goes
In laughter and sighs,
Rippling the water that knows
More than the wise.
Sky that is very blue,
if my heart could stand
And look at the heart of you
From the highest land,
Would I find green folly there
Laughingly rise
Through the pools of the dimpled air
That seem too wise?
Green is for merry heart,
Blue for a long thought;
Would ye tear with a word apart
The lesson the world taught?
Blue and green of fine weather!
God bless the laughter and sighs
That go always together
In the heart of the wise!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Our Jim.
The lean years pinched ; the lean years went,
' An’ fat" years came on laggard wing.
Misfortunes saw their savin’s spent,
Yet dad an’ mum knew how to sing
Of golden hopes that loomed ahead,
But now —but n0w....0ur Jim is dead!
Mum braves the shafts of Sorrow well,
But dad is droopin’ like a flower.
Big Jim—his pride! Hot tears trill well
Into his eyes.. . .to see him cower
Makes cry in’ catchin’. My! we dread
To talk aloud since Big Jim’s dead !
When our Jim went, dad worked the farm,
Though he’d retired a goodly span.
“Hard graft,” he said, “will never harm
A willin’ an’ a healthy man.”
Of course we humored this kind whim —
For dad was doin’ it for Jim!
Dad has lost heart —he’s quite convinced
That all he’s done is labor lost;
Though hard the graft, he never winced
About the worry or the cost.
’Cos Jim was fightin’ o’er the foam,
An’ he mus’ see the harvest home!
Mum braves the shafts from Sorrow’s bow,
But dad is wiltin’ like a tree
That’s ringbarked. . . .Nearby days may show
That it’s within Fate’s grim decree
To send ol’ dad —a whilom whim —
Out west, a wingin’ after Jim!
VICTORIA.
RITA SUNYASEE.

THURSDAY, MAY 16, 1918.
TRUTH AND DEMOCRACY.
The foundation of democracy is truth. The
theory that the mass of individual citizens
working together at the problems of State
will do the right thing and the wise thing is
dependent upon each individual citizen know-
ing the whole truth about the matters with
which he has to deal. The present war is,
in the minds of the citizens of the Allied
nations engaged in it, a struggle between
democracy and military autocracy; but, in
waging it, democracy has been so ill-advised
that it has for the greater part failed to use
its most important weapon. In a word,
fighting for democracy, it has allowed those
in temporary authority to become autocratic,
and, in doing so, has failed to come to the
real test as between system and system.
■* * * *
Never since the days of the Saxon kings
has England been, democratic. From age to
age despotic sovereigns permitted small sec-
tions of the people to have a say in the con-
trol of public affairs. At the close of the
seventeenth century —after one king had lost
his head and another had been compelled to
sprint from his crown —Parliament in Eng-
land represented only a small minority of
the people; and by the middle of the
eighteenth century, owing to the movements
of population, which created “rotten
boroughs,” half the seats of the Commons
were in the pockets of a few wealthy men.
Then the franchise began to be organised
and extended on a more decent plan; but
meantime a system of party government had
come into existence which destroyed all the
democratic effect of the organisation and
extension. Never at any time have the
people of England had a real say in the
government of England. Always that gov-
ernment has been in the hands of clique
and class; and while some theory of democ-
racy was always preached by the crowd-
flattering aspirant to Parliament, the sub-
stance was never there, in that the people
were never told the whole truth or allowed
to exercise their alleged powers beyond a
mere choice between those in power and
those who wanted to be in power. Once in
seven years or less John Hodge was taken
to the poll to vote for one party or the other
—that was the sum total of the exercise of
John’s alleged democratic rights. When
his vote was cast Parliament took no
more notice of him than it did of the turnips
in the field; and there Was never any idea
that he should be told about anything that
was going on. But party politics blinded
not only John Hodge; it kept the bandage
tight over the eyes of most of the big players
of the game itself. Something appeared in
print, something was reported in Parliament;
but the main art of statecraft lay in getting
at a fragment of the truth lying behind all
the semi-truth which was, in effect, false-
hood. The system was not democratic, nor
open, nor fair to the people or their leaders;
but always there remained the hope that by
the slow Brjtish mode of reform real democ-
racy based on truth and knowledge would
grow out of it.
* * ■* *
When the great war arrived, Britain
entered it with a whoop for democracy.
Another nation which had got 'into much
closer grips with the realities of popular
government was there standing on the defen-
sive against autocratic power. Democracy
was the winning word, just as it had been
at the hustings; and it was used for all it
was worth in urging the essential rightness
of the British cause. Nobody need doubt
that that cause was right, and that the Ger-
man force compelled the Allies to take the
stand they did ; but democracy became more
and more a sham as the war rolled on its
calamitous way. To the statesman of the
hour it seemed out of the question to light
autocracy with the weapons of democracy.
With the cry of democracy on their lips
they proceeded to create the stiffest sort of
despotism and to wage war in the enemy’s
way, while the people who did the bleeding
and put up the cash were kept in practical
darkness as to the course of events. It was,
of course, expected that this doing of evil
that good might come of it would be crowned
with success; and in politics success is held
to justify everything. But the success has
not come; and, on latest information, a kind
of fury has taken possession of that portion
of the alleged democracy which in the ranks
of the army is face to face with the real, red,
ruthless foe, that facts should have been so
perverted or obscured by the people in
power. Whatever political upheavals may
result, the crisis of British history seems to
have arrived; and, win or lose, a real democ-
racy may emerge from the war-smoke. The
German guns began in 1914 to batter roads
to the east and west on which Imperialism
might make its triumphant march. The
effect on the east was to break Imperialism
into little pieces; and now in the west a.
similar result is being arrived at. In Berlin-
itself democracy is yowling between hunger-
pangs for recognition; and it is pretty clear
that, in London, truth, which is the sole
foundation on which a true democracy can
be built, is going to be insisted upon with
some violence. Only in Australia is the
autocracy created by the war in the name of
democracy tolerated with complete meek-
ness ; which means, in the plainest of terms,
that only in Australia is the Prussian idea
holding its own. Our alleged Win-the-War
policy brushes the people aside as an utterly
negligible quantity.
* •* * *
Yet the chances are that true democracy
could have thumped the Kaiser to a stand-
still before this. A democratic army is not
by any means the unheard-of thing that a
few big-hats suppose. Such an army was
seen in England 270 years ago. It had little
discipline except on the field; it chose its
own leaders; it discussed with its officers
the conduct of campaigns, and did all sorts
of military abominations; but it never lost a
battle , and it set every throne in Europe
trembling in fear lest it should cross Dover
Strait. Similarly, the first armies of the
Involution, though small, badly munitioned
and often very much put of hand, kept the
game going in a fashion that a few years
later made the achievements of Napoleon
possible. That army had only a. rude hint
of democracy about it—a lot of the old ser-
vility remaining as an aftermath of the
Bourbon serfdom —but such democracy as it
had, with the enthusiasm which democracy
roused, made it a solid fighting institution.
At any rate, whatever may be the needs of
the army in the way of order and discipline,
and whatever the chances of our present
armies winning, the thing behind the armies
must revert to plain democracy if victory is
to be real and lasting. On present appear-
ances, there is a fair chance of Germany
emerging from the struggle with a large
measure of political freedom, while the Allied
nations win through to success and find
their former freedom gone. That result, in
all but name, would be a Prussian war-lord’s
victory.
* * * *
Let us get down to plain bedrock in con-
sidering this matter. Truth is the only
foundation on which a democracy can stand;
therefore any person who obscures or per-
verts truth is opposed to the cause for which
the Allies are fighting. People begin to real-
ise that in London; and the realisation is
going to make a lot of difference to the big-
hats who stand in the road of war-efficiency.
Let us realise it here in Australia. It is the
Australian people who are in this war. who
do the paying and the bleeding and the
weeping. Our armies are under an outside
control, and must take the place dictated in
the great fighting machine; but the people at
home have a right to their democratic free-
dom resting on democratic truth. We do
not believe that the Kaiser can beat our
democracy; why should a lot of much
smaller people be allowed to tread on us? If
that question is pushed outside practical
politics now. we may reach a crisis before
many years in which it will arise with jagged
force to hurt somebody.
RECONSTRUCTION.
The war has hurried Australia on to a
point that might have taken half a century
to reach under the conditions of peace. The
Federal system as we know it has been a
failure. It is not a question now whether it
should be changed, but what the nature of
the change shall be. The load of debt on the
nation’s shoulders and the immense new
expenditure the people will have to provide
for apart altogether from the debt, make it
impossible to continue the Fourteen Houses
of Parliament lunacy and a duplication of
responsibilities and effort in almost every
direction. Either there must be a national
Parliament, with a reconstruction of the
States for purposes of local government and
administration, or Australia must go back
to the old divisions or something like them
and abandon the idea of becoming a united
nation. The latter choice would be a
calamity. But the prevailing waste and
muddle means calling tenders for the same
thing.
* * * *
In the days before Federation the idea of
unification got a poor hearing, partly
because many people believed that it was put
forward with the intention of hamstringing
the Federal movement and for the rest
because it looked impossible to get
agreement on the subject. But the
arguments that carried force then are
not the arguments that come . forward
now. In those times the controversy
was in the main speculative and dealt with
what might be. In these times there is the
experience gained in 18 years of a hybrid
system, which has worked out in a way
almost wholly different from what was ex-
pected. The results are to be seen in a
growth of the cost of government to a figure
which turns the hopes of an older time into
ridicule; in debts that are mountainous; in
extravagances almost unbelievable; in taxes
imposed by rival authorities without regard
for one another or the taxpayer; in an over-
lapping of legislation and administration; in
squabbling over this, that and the other; in
dodge, confusion and waste of effort, time,
opportunity and money. National policies
are neglected. The Federal Parliament works
with one eye on the State Parliaments and
the other on the State ballot-boxes —even in
carrying on the war. For their part when
the State Premiers met in Sydney this week
the first thing they talked about was the
intrusion of Federal legislation and expendi-
ture upon the States. Most of what they had
to say was perfectly right. The absurdity of
it all can be seen by anyone, just as a blind
man can see the folly of an arrangement
which to achieve this end maintains numer-
ous Parliaments, gives eacli of them unlimited
power to waste the public assets and makes
the wretched citizen pay two bills instead
of one.
* * * *
It had for a long time been fairly clear that
under any conditions there was only a remote
hope of the people and politicians thinking
in terms of Australia so long as the" States
and Commonwealth remained in the rela-
tionship established by the Federal Consti-
tution. The enthusiasms generated by the
establishment of the Commonwealth were
beginning to evaporate under the influence
of a parochialism even more sinister than
prevailed originally. Political parties in the
Federal Legislature were becoming in a.
sense mere branches of the State Parlia-
ments, and the hope that the abler minds
would be drawn into the Federal arena had
ceased to be seriously held anywhere outside
of Melbourne. The double-barrelled system
had commenced to react upon itself with con-
sequences disastrous to the political thought
and national ambitions of the people. And
if the war has done nothing else for Aus-
tralia it has at least shown the community
the futility of the plan the nation has been
working on. But in showing this the war
has done something else. It has laid the
foundation of economic conditions which will
compel a change of method. Australia will
soon be faced with the naked realities of a
struggle for existence. Waste and shirk and
extravagance will be crimes against the
people, if they be then possible, which is
doubtful, for at rock-bottom these disasters
in the past have only been the results of the
casualness and demoralisation caused by
flinging unearned millions about for the
people to scramble for. Unification offers
a way out; and the sooner the States begin
to find where they stand on this matter the
better weather they will make in the rough
times to come. The war cannot go on
indefinitely. "When it ends all the old
problems of production and population and
trade and labor and taxation will be on the
table, each bigger and more urgent than
before. The prevailing system failed _us
yesterday. It would land us in the ditch
to-morrow. It is a painful truth that a
State could do better for itself working lone-
handed than tangled up with five others in
the prevailing confusion. But to allow
them all to work lone-handed would
mean national suicide. The. real job before
Australia is to make Australia a reality, and
the only way to start will be to cut out all
the lumber that was left in by mistake when
the contract started.
THE IRISH ANSWER.
Dear Bulletin, —The Irish Question has
long been an exasperation and a thorn in
the flesh, just like the Eastern Question, and
the Balkan Question, and the Chinese Ques-
tion (if it is still alive), and the Woman
Question (whatever that may be). It is
large, long, persistent and not easy to define,
and the Irish Answer, which is always com-
ing to hand, never proves satisfactory. Now
I beg respectfully to submit my theory. It
consists in the withdrawal of the British
garrison—not so much the armed and uni-
formed garrison, which doesn’t matter to
any extent, but the other one The trouble,
it seems to me, only began to look incurable
when the other ope came to Erin to stay.
It is an irreconcilable element, and even if
it has the best intentions, which it probably
hasn’t, for hardly anybody has, that doesn’t
improve the case. A great deal _of the
world’s damage is done by people with good
intentions. I allude of course to the Presby-
terian and in a small degree Methodist
colony, which dwells so loudly in a corner of
Ulster. The Anglican settlers, though they
date back further, have made far less dis-
turbance, perhaps because they take their
creed less seriously, while the other folk are
in deadly earnest, and are a cause of earnest-
ness among their neighbors.
Britain has had bigger complications, but
none that could make more row for its size.
Consider a few examples:—
When the Romans came to Britain they were
people of a new race, a new language and a new
religion. Also, what was a great drawback, they
o>ved allegiance to an outside Power. But the
language difficulty and the racial difficulty were got
over. As for the religious discrepancy, the Roman
went on the simple principle that any old faith was
much the same as any other old creed. Provided it
didn’t include human sacrifices or treason against the
emperor, he was willing to give it house-room among
his own gods. He ran a boarding-house for gods.
He held it as the respectable thing for each man to
belong to the catechism of his ancestors, and believed
that no temple, provided it was any sort of a gentle-
man, should throw bricks at any other temple. His
greatest Objection to the Early Christians was that
they were savagely disrespectful to Jupiter, and that
they invented a hot place for G'.esar to go to unless
he became one of them. As regards the domination
of Rome, a time came when a Briton could become
C,esar just like anybody else provided there was a
vacancy and he got there first. That alleviated the
position. The Roman was a Superior Person, but he
wasn’t a bad specimen as the times went.
The Saxon was a beastly Superior Person. He
didn’t make England part of a foreign empire, for he
brought his throne —a whole lot of thrones —with
him, so he lacked one bad quality. But he stood for
an imported race, an imported language and an
imported worship. He got over the dislike of the
Roman-British population by practically wiping it
out.
The Norman was the most Superior Person ever
born. His religion was all right, but he had every
other disappointing feature —difference of race, differ-
ence of speech, and his foreign connection with
Normandy, and later also with Anjou, Maine, Poitou,
Aquitaine, etc. But above and beyond all other
things he was the most Bumptious Party ever born.
In the end, however, he lost his great continental
possessions, and with them the support of his con-
tinental army, and became merely a small alien
garrison in a large infuriated land.. So, perforce, he
climbed down from his pedestal by instalments and
pretended to be a human being, and became more or
less English. So the Norman Question may be said
to have settled itself.
But the Irish Question, which is now the
Corner-o£-TJlster Question, takes an awful
amount of settlement. Home reasons :
(1) The Ulster Presbyterian is a Superior Person
of the sour and unpicturesque sort. He is the Toro's
anointed, and he doesn’t forget it.
(2) He represents an intruding race.
(3) He also represents an exotic religion.
(4) At first he represented in some measure a
foreign speech.
(5) 1-Ie was, and is, backed up by a Power outside
Ireland, so that in addition to being himself, which
is bad, he is another person’s small brother and
hanger-on, which is worse. This arrangement shows
no tendency to die out as was the case with the
Normans, for the Government at London is quite
healthy. Nor is it smoothed over as was the way
with the happy-go-lucky Romans; who would have
regarded it as quite in order for an Irish officer to
assume the purple in a palace beside the Thames if
the Irish army acclaimed him and the other armies
didn’t put up more than six rival candidates.
(6) He got his footing by dispossessing native pro-
prietors, which was the orthodox proceeding. But
he lacked the halo of conquest, for his bit of land
wasn’t the prize of his own how and spear. He was
sent over as a sort of bailiff in possession; also to
some extent as an individual whom Great Britain
could do without; also as a protest against the Irish
national idea and the Irish Church.
(7) And for most of his term of residence his
privileges, as against the Catholic voter, who had no
vote; and the Catholic member of Parliament, who
wasn’t allowed in Parliament or anywhere near it;
and the Catholic landowner, who wasn’t permitted
to own any land ; and the Catholic religionist, who
was liable to penal servitude if he was found having
his own religion, were as large as those of any self-
important invader of the lot. It was self-importance
without the dignity of complete self-reliance.
The invasion was large enough to be
annoying and not large enough to be decisive.
If the new people had been like the Franks
and Goths, who did their own conquering,
set up their own metropolis in the stolen
land, and took their religion very much as
it came, the country might have felt that it
had only experienced a casual change of
figureheads. But all the conditions were
unfavorable. Most people wear their creeds
loosely buttone'd, or not buttoned at all, in
times of peace. But Catholic Ireland was
worried first by Catholic England on the
groilnd that its church was untidy and not
up to sample. Then Reformed England,
under the Tudors, started to teach it the
only true faith all of a sudden —a new true
faith which the Tudor crowd had devised
and on which the varnish was still damp.
About this time Ireland sat up and took
notice. On the heels of this visitation came,
in breathless haste, still another only true
faith, and its forerunner was a dour person
who brought his synod and his presbytery
and his catechism and later on his Covenant
in his carpet-bag. (This person even man-
aged to make his creed the established church
of England for a few years, but that is a side
issue.) He didn’t attempt to diffuse him-
self in a friendly way, but took up a strong
but aloof position in a corner of the island
as if he were a Pharisee or a fortification.
Everybody sat up in real earnest. The
bailiff grew so violently Protestant that he
couldn’t sleep at night for thinking of his
Protestantism. The bailiffed was filled with
a fervor that he had never known before.
They remain very much like that to this
day. The one can’t forget that his people
once promised to be about the greatest of
the second-class nations —lack of territory
forbade any greater ambition—and that now
they are only the remnant of a race which
has been scattered to the four winds. The
other hardly disguises the sentiment that if
the scattering could' be continued it would be
a good thing, as making more room for a
much more desirable person, in fact, the
most desirable person—to wit, himself. And
some unfortunate remark about the walls of
Derry, which have made almost as much
commotion in their time as the walls of
Babylon, happens now and then to aggravate
the case.
The matter might surely be settled if the
Presbyterians were removed to Scotland,
whence most of their ancestors came. The
community that was sent to Ireland could
be sent away again, especially considering


the improved means of transit. There are
only about 450,000 persons involved, mostly
women and children, and in these days when
Britain shifts 10 times that number of armed
men around the map the figure is relatively
unimportant. As for the cost of removal
and re-settlement and compensation for dis-
turbance. it would look a trifle to a genera-
tion which has grown accustomed to shovel-
ling out £100,000,000 of war expenses before
breakfast. A good part of Scotland is thinly
peopled and is losing population. The cli-
mate is alleged to be healthy; there are many
fine and almost untenanted harbors; the
water power is past enumeration ; and the
Solemn League and Covenant grows luxuri-
antly. With space and time and money and
more water power than anybody knows what
to do with there are the best of possibilities.
—Tours, etc-., Henry llorsecollar.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Peace “by Negotiation.”
The futile voice of Queensland Labor poli-
tician Higgs was heard last Friday
demanding peacfe by “negotiation.” The
same day it transpired that Roumania has
at last got the article that Higgs wants. It
is to abolish its army—all but 30,000 men.
It is to pay all the expenses of a large Ger-
man force which will be permanently sta-
tioned in the country to supervise the collec-
tion of the output of wheat, meat, oil and
sundries and their export to Germany and
Austria, the miserable country receiving no
assurance of payment. It is to freely admit
German and Austrian products; to admit
Hun warships to its ports and rivers ; to
give up part of its territory, while the rest
is governed by German, Austrian, Bul-
garian and Turkish officials. Also it is to
pay an impossible indemnity and obey any
further orders which it may receive. That
is the fate of the country which got the Hun
peace that Higgs recommends so cordially.
Yet the utterly crushed little nation which
has got Higgs’s tranquillity didn’t come into
the war voluntarily. It simply received a
postcard from the Hun telling it that neu-
trality wasn’t allowed; that it must put its
army under Hun control as an instrument
for the butchery of Serbs and other Allies
and lend the Hun all its resources—or take
the consequences. There is also for the en-
couragement of Higgs the case of the new
republic of Finland. Like Roumania it has
bought peace by negotiation. The worst that
has happened to it is a Hun occupation, the
annexation of everything that is useful to
the Hun, the seizure by the Hun of its Cus-
toms revenue, the enrolment of its male
population in the Hun army, and the appoint-
ment of a Hun ruler.
The Lloyd George Ministry.
A letter written by General Maurice,
until lately Chief Director of Military
Operations, stirred up something like a poli-
tical earthquake in London last week.
Apparently it was a brief letter and the
point of it simple. In effect Maurice
announced that replies given by Lloyd
George and Bonar Law to recent questions
relating to (1) Haig’s fighting strength.
(2) the extension of the British line before
St. Quentin, and (3) the number of troops
in Egypt and Mesopotamia had been con-
trary to fact. The first Ministerial answer
was a statement that the Government pro-
posed to set up a judicial tribunal to in-
quire secretly into the allegations. Asquith
suggested that the matter was entirely one
for the Commons and should be investigated
by a select committee. The Ministry,
after thinking for 24 hours, abandoned the
idea of a judicial tribunal and decided to
state its defence directly to the House and
take a division. Meanwhile Asquith was
violently assailed by the Lloyd George
newspapers for conspiring with the military
to overthrow the Government, and Lloyd
George flamboyantly claimed that the
motion to appoint a committee of inquiry
was equivalent to a vote of censure. The
end of it all w r as that the vote w 7 as hurried
on before the Irish members who were over
in the distressful isle could get back to
Westminster, and the Government found
itself held up by a huge majority—293 to
106. But judging from the tone of debates
in the House of Commons during the last
few months, the enthusiasm with which
some of the newspapers turned to assail
Asquith instead of proving Lloyd George
to be beyond suspicion, and the fact that
the Commons voted in the dark, this paper
would judge that the majority is a long way
from being a true indication of wdiere the
Ministry stands. The Government is a
stop-gap, and it can be taken as certain
that unless its leader soon begins to win
the Avar he will go out. The reason the
Commons have not struck him down already
is that there is no workable alternative to
a Ministry composed of similar elements
and no other man than the vigorous little
Welshman coukl be expected to do some of
the things Avliich had to be done in the past.
But the war has not been going according
to time-table. The old Lloyd George tone
suits neither the hour nor the surrounding
circumstances, and the new tone is not
reassuring. No Government such as this
one, holding office on sufferance amid such
conditions, could possibly hope to be popular
unless it could point to unmistakable suc-
cesses in the field and in domestic adminis-
tration. This the Lloyd George Govern-
ment cannot do. It is retained, therefore,
not for its own merits, but because of the
demerits of Asquith and his gang of mili-
tary muddlers —many of them now displaced.
Asquith as leader of the Opposition is the
mainstay of Lloyd George as Prime Minis-
ter. So it comes down to this. The Com-
mons didn’t answer the question whether
George and Law are what Maurice said
they are. It simply said that it shuddered
at the very thought of Asquith back in
office.
Currency and Other Matters.
Treasurer and Acting Prime Minister
Watt threatens strong measures against
people Avho hint that the Federal currency
isn’t safe, and therein he has some justifica-
tion. The< face value of the currency is pro-
tected by a gold reserve which, on all ex-
perience, is more than ample. And so long
as a crowd which has, so far, shown neither
foresight nor serious regard for sol-
vency doesn’t get a chance to tamper with
that reserve, the paper money can’t deterio-
rate. The only disquieting feature is that
the people wdio manufacture and tolerate the
State Parliaments and Ministries do the
same for the Australian Parliament and
Ministry, and some of those State Parlia-
ments and Governments seem to skate
towards insolvency with a light heart, as
these figures show :—•

These are not Federal war expenses, but

much worse if the Commonwealth hadn’t
relieved the State Governments of old-age
pensions and other unproductive expendi-
tures. The figures represent the multiplica-
tion of jobs which don’t produce any corres-
ponding revenue, but are a dead weight on
the Treasury; the lavish increase of pay
wdiere there is a political pull (teachers, for
instance, being the most scattered tribe of

the lavish investment of 5 p.c. oi’ p.c.

votes. (By increases of railway and tram-
way rates the growth of taxation has been
made rather less than it would otherwise
have been.) The last three lines of figures
show where the States may expect to get
unless they reform, Especially in the loan
business. But they can’t reform without
admitting that the policy of the past has
been hideously foolish and corrupt, which
their leaders show no sign of doing. Nor
can they reform without breaking the news
to a large and already dissatisfied crowd of
voters, some of whom already talk repudia-
tion, that they have been living in a fool’s
paradise largely of their own creation, and
that the real causes of dissatisfaction haven’t
quite commenced yet. As the possibilities of
taxation aren’t unlimited and the willing-
ness to pay taxes is far from unlimited, it
may be assumed that some at least of the
States are skidding cheerfully towards repu-
diation or default or some sort of breakage.
The moral is that as the State electors are
the same as the Federal electors, and their
policies and ideas can’t be dissociated, this
isn’t a time to be bumptious about the secur-
ity of anything—except perhaps the note
issue. That stands in a category by itself,
the only risk being that the Heaven-born
muddlers xvho have deteriorated so many
other things may find a chance to try ex-
periments with the currency also. Against
the Heaven-born muddler in powrnr there is
no guarantee.
It Must Come Out of Loans.
Mr. James (-N. S. Wales Minister for Education)
urges the finding of £500,000 to catch up arrears of
work in providing necessary school accommodation.
....This money, of course, would have to covie Out
of loans. —Sydney Telegraph.
Yes, of course, it must come out of loans.
Yet schools are not a permanent and repro-
ductive work, for nothing is permanent, and
since the abolition of fees State schools pro-
duce no income. Possibly the theory is that the
State is too lazy and shiftless and extrava-
gant and too much diseased by the long-
established borrowing habit to want to pay
its ordinary expenses out of its own ordinary
pocket. Possibly the theory is that it can’t
pay, even now when wool and meat and wheat
and minerals command almost record prices
in the world’s markets, and when paper money
is abundant, and though the past six years’
revenue has been the biggest on record —that
is to say, the revenue for the year to June,
1912, was the largest up to date, and that
for 1913 was larger, and for 1914 larger still,
and 1915 beat all its predecessors, and 1916
beat that in turn, and 1917 topped them all.
Also it is a time when the war, in a financial
sense and so far as the State Governments
are concerned, is still more of an asset than
a liability, for the States don’t pay war
expenses and they profit indirectly by 1 the
expenditure of Federal war loans. If the
Ma community under these conditions can’t
meet the cost of buildings in which to teach
the rising generation its ABC, then there isn’t
the slightest visible hope that it can meet
the school loan when it falls due, and the
poor, misgoverned, disheartened place might
as well call itself bankrupt at once. A school
loan is especially infamous, for a school, so
far from yielding revenue, is a constant
source of expenditure. It involves equip-
ment, repairs, cleaning, teachers’ salaries
and sundries. The Minister who proposes
charging items of that sort to posterity should
be disqualified for life, and the same should
happen to the members who support him;
and the papers which openly or tacitly sup-
port the proceeding should be proceeded
against under the War Precautions Act for
discouraging honesty and self-reliance, and
promoting a belief in the insolvency of the
Treasury. Still it is too much to hope that
anything of the sort will happen. The nation
has been so rotted and corrupted by the easy
habit of crawling to foreign capitalists for
the loan of a crust that probably only one
person in a hundred notices the shameful-
ness and cowardice of the business. New'
South Wales agitated most strenuously in
bygone years for the abolition of the
kid’s school fees, and when the old
man and the old woman got rid of
that trifling liability they smote their
chests and reckoned that a great thing
had been done in the cause of Liberty. In
the matter of selling the kid’s liberty to the
hungry financier neither the wretched old
man nor the unthinking old woman seems to
be in the least agitated. Perhaps Minister
James and his colleagues might consider this
question: Seeing that the revenue of their
State rose from £13,839,139 (£8 Bs. sd. per
inhabitant) in 1911 to £20,495,996 (£ll Os.
7d. per inhabitant) in 1917, and seeing that
the Government with £6,600,000 more to play'
with in the last year than the first, is less
able to find the price of a common ordinary
school now’ than it was then, how much
money per annum is needed to keep these
political Jugginses out of the ditch? So
far the rule has been that the more money
they have the more they are ditched.
Pay for the Dog and Bark Yourself.
A railway is wanted from Coonabarabran
to Burren Junction, and Ball, N.S.W.
Minister for Works, wags his coat-tails in
blessing the project, but admits that he is
helpless to carry it out. The only people
who can do it are the people who live in
the district; and if they will raise the money
the work will be pushed ahead. Again,
Webster, P.M.G., calls upon us to look
after the mail bags and see that nobody
steals them. This is getting right back to
the simplicities of primitive life. It is
putting public fueling and the public’s desire
to protect itself in place of a police system
controlled by Governments. Every man his
own policeman if you will; but every man
his own policeman while a police force is
maintained at great expense to do the work
for him is another matter. All along the
line there are indications that -our central-
ised Governments are failing. The Federal
Government advocated conscription and had
the pow'er to put it into force, yet threw the
onus of decision on the electors; and when
defeated it clung on to office and attempted
to prosecute the war without the powers
which it had declared were necessary. If
the people must do the work of Governments,
why go to the needless trouble and expense
of having the Governments at all? Every-
thing points to the conclusion that the great,
efficient democracies of the future will handle
their affairs in small sections. Centralised
government is merely a hurdle which democ-
racy must jump every time it requires to
take a step forward. After all, Attica —a
parish on the seaward spur of a Greek moun-
tain —was and is a bigger thing for all time
than that vast Imperial Russia that recently
fell in a sprawling heap. Given a thousand
new Atticas, and Australia has a chance of
greatness that, will never come through
clumsy centralisation.

The Mania for “Boards.”
It is a cold day and a long wet night when
some Ministry doesn’t appoint a Board of
four or more paid members, with a salaried
president and a remunerated secretary and
some stipendiary expert assessors. The
Board inquires into and watches over the
interests of Something; and is in constant
touch with another hired Board which is
devoted to the promotion and advancement
of Almost Anything; and a third wage-earn-
ing Board which keeps an unsleeping eye on
Dashed Near Everything. These report to
a Centra] Council with a reasonable hono-
rarium, which Council collates and revises
their conclusions, and sends them to the
Government Printer to be laid before the
public annually, bound in cloth, and quite
unsaleable at 7s. 6d., though they cost
a great deal more. Information for the
enlightenment of these Boards is supplied to
a great extent by local bodies, whose special
knowledge enables them to advise bn all
matters connected with pastoral, agricul-
tural and ux-ban districts, also irrigation
areas, drainage areas, cactus-infested areas,
rabbit-haunted areas, sludge-channels and
abandoned selections. The ideal institution
of this sort has a wide range. For instance,
if its business is Spirits it naturally deals
with disembodied spirits ; bottled spirits ; and
spirits that gibber in the night, with a tele-
scopic department to see the spirit coming,
and a telephonic department to hear it gib-
ber, and a meteorological department to
ascertain what kind of night it mostly gib-
bers on, and whether it gibbers more on
Friday than any other day, and at what
stage of the moon it is most active. The
Chief of Police arrests the spirit if it has
drunk too much of itself. The Government
Architect is a paid member of the Board, his
business being to decide what kind of build-
ing is most patronised by spirits, with a
special eye to the construction of wine vaults.
The Superintendent of the Zoo and the Chief
Medical Officer are also paid members, their
business being to decide whether dogs can
see ghosts and the effect of absinthe on the
kidneys. The Chief Lunacy Officer reports
monthly on high spirits, low spirits, methy-
lated spirits (with the assistance of a house-
painter at union rates) and elementary spirits.
A special clerical, literary and political depart-
ment spreads itself on evil spirits, imported
spirits, unclean spirits, adulterated spirits
and the Spirit of Freedom; the Historical
and Pilotage Society, assisted by a ship-
captain and a diver, considers, without fee
and from purely patriotic motives, those
spirits which Owen Glen dower called from
the vasty deep; likewise the Flying Dutch-
man and Hosier’s Ghost; and the Fire
Brigade writes exhaustively on the alleged
spontaneous combustion of old people who
have been large consumers of rum. And that
is about all, except that the N. S. Wales
Cabinet, in its haste to disseminate the sur-
plus, has appointed a Board of Trade which
looks neither better nor worse than the rest.
Cocky and His Wheat Bags.
When the farmers of Australia read what
Mnjof Purcell has said about cornsacks
they will discover another reason for bless-
ing the dominating personality that has so
carefully guarded their interests. Purcell,
on remount business in India, was asked to
investigate the matter of woolpacks and
cornsacks and the steep prices thereof. Ip'
saw many jute manufacturers, who were
quite candid. There was no increase in the
cost of producing sacks, they said ; but when
the war restricted freightage the merchants
—to whom the manufacturers sold—cornered
the stocks and raked in coin. The manu-
facturers then reckoned that they might as
well have a bit too, so naturally prices
jumped still further. Major Purcell had
observed that Argentina was being supplied
with sacks at about half the rates charged
to Australia, and naturally he asked why.
The manufacturers were frank, about that,
too —Argentina got its goods at bedrock
prices because its Government dealt with
the manufacturers direct. Australia could
have done the same and might'still do so,
but its Government preferred to deal with
the merchants —and Australia had to pay for
it. These manufacturers told Major Purcell
that for two or three seasons they have not
only been willing but anxious to save the
Commonwealth over £1,000,000 by supplying
the Government direct; but the Government
decided “not to interfere with the usual
course” —the “usual course” being to put
the farmer at the mercy of all the rooks in
the world. Incidentally there were no
scruples about departing from “the usual
course” when the farmer’s wheat was com-
mandeered and put in unprotected stacks to
be the prey of mice and weevil. The scruples
only began to work when it became a ques-
tion of interfering with the other parasites.

Time to Look Ahead.
About the great Sea Trek: —
There is a problem waiting for the pacifists, and
the party which professes that any really serious
attempt at Defence is an outrage on “liberty,” and
those who say that all men are brothers, and that
the Devil isn’t as bad as he is painted, and for
the T.W.W. and Bolshevik folk who write so many
of the Labor manifestoes these days, and those who
say they will begin to consider the security of the
country when the capitalist or the wage system or
drink or gambling or the present artificial basis of
society is abolished, and “class-consciousness” is fully
established, and “victimisation” is a thing of the
past, and the public debt is repudiated, and every
Saturday is made a holiday. The problem is this:
What is the best place to go to if the Hun wins the
war, and takes Australia as a natural consequence?
The choice will lie between (1) staying here and losing
everything, including the. last shred of liberty and
probably life itself, and (2) getting away in a penni-
less condition and making a fresh start. Leaving out of
account the migrations of barbarous races in the long
ago, civilised peoples have done or attempted the big
trek before now. The Dutch prepared to move bodily
by sea in the worst days of the Spanish war, but the
luck turned, and that trek didn’t come off. The Boer
States were founded by a little community taking up
its bed and walking. Venice was created by refugees,
and they built so well that their republic lasted 1000
years. Three-fourths or more of the Irish nation left
home because home wasn’t a good place to live in,
and Erin became, like Jewry, a scattered people
under many alien flags. But these happenings were
in days when the world had lots of breathing space,
and some of them were in days when No Man’s Land
could be found round the next corner. The Boers
had a handy land refuge, which we haven’t. The Irish,
the Venetians and the Dutch could find abundant
shipping, which we can’t. Yet there will be the neces-
sity to adjourn somewhere somehow if things go
wrong. The Hun knows by his small experiments in
Schleswig-Holstein, Poland and Alsace-Lorraine that
he isn’t the kind of ruffian that subject populations
take kindly to, and that they are a constant worry and
danger. He makes no disguise of his intention to
prevent the danger growing to overwhelming dimen-
sions, and the only way is by thinning out future
annexed races and keeping them thinned out. There-
fore, if they want to live, the pro-Huns who oppose
Australian defence, openly or otherwise, should look
ahead for once and think about their future place
of residence.

The People’s Offensive.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Labor leaders of Britain are not for a
separate peace. Their whole procedure is to organise
a common front; and to do it, not as they believe
the Governments had done prior to President Wil-
son's initiative, by arriving at the least common
multiple of their several ambitions, but by cleaving
through to what are the great common denominators
of democratic purpose.— Paul Kellogg on ‘‘The
Uritish Labor Offensive.’’
Kings had . their small ambitions. These
are gone.
Seldom their dreams soared higher than the
sweep
01 some large sword on which the white sun
shone
Gilding its bloodied edge. But we shall reap
In broader harvest-fields where faith makes
good
Above the shock of battle red and rude.
War is upon us as a rolling flood;
But not as sheep for slaughter are we sent.
Ours the chief price to pay in tears, and
blood—
Our graves fill a tormented continent—
But for the pure tears wept, the true blood
shed
We shall take vengeance worthy of the dead.
This is our Revolution. In the storm
Shall all thrones shake to their last tinsel
shine.
The Song of Hate is prelude to reform.
The guns’ loud blasphemy a voice divine;
This is the People’s war, and through the
smoke
Of battle-torment breaks the age-old yoke.
Not an imperial triumph, not the lean,
Poor promise of a victory in trade
Shall satisfy us when our eyes have seen
The new sun rise, the old lamps break and
fade;
But through the rolling clouds of dust and
fire
We march to touch the goal of Man’s desire.
Bet the wheels turn ! Stand to your post of
toil!
Give of your [jest! The long dark breaks in
light.
The feet of Labor stand on ransomed soil;
‘Tis Labor that shall triumph in the fight.
Above the wreck of Thtngs-tha t-Are we see
The flaming banner of the days to be.

give a damn about, there may be a means of
arriving at a fair guess as to the probable
result in the event of things going wrong
with the Allied arms. In 1169 there was a
Teuton invasion of Ireland as an aftermath
of the conquest of England by the Boche of
those days. That Boche in the matter of
arms, equipment and organisation had a
greater advantage over the Irish native sol-
diery than the German of to-day could have
over any voluntary armies raised in Ireland.
So terrific was the invader in his heavy
armor that the Irishman, accustomed to
another mode of fighting, was absolutely
unable to hurt him in any way at nil, while
he could butcher the peasantry and lay waste
the country to his heart’s content. He did
butcher and lay waste; but the Irishman in
spite of his sufferings and agonies still kept
standing erect. The conquest of Ireland
was regarded as complete on the occasion
of Prince John’s visit in 1185; but in 1885,
exactly 700 years later, there was only one
small corner in the north-east of the country
where outside an armed fort the English flag
could be raised or the English national
anthem sung. If Irishmen under the fright-
ful handicap of lack of arms stood upright
against the first Teuton invader for 700
years, “Plain Australian” needn’t worry
too much about how erect they would stand
in the event of a second invasion. As to the
statement that plain Australians don’t give
a damn about Ireland and its affairs, it is
worth noting that one-third of the plain
Australians in this Commonwealth are of
Irish descent; and if “Plain Australian”
happens to belong to the Teuton element,
that is hardly a reason for assuming that
the people round him don’t care. The
real tragedy, in which all the thinking world
is interested, is that when a world-war is
being waged for the principles for which
Ireland has stood through all the centuries,
the bitter mismanagement of English rulers
has prevented the weight of a nation that
never shirked the test of a fight being thrown
into the great struggle.

Official Dope—and a Protest.
A long rigmarole from the Secretary of
State for the Colonies, introduced with the
explanation that it “gives official news of the
operations on the West Front,” was printed
in Australian newspapers on Tuesday.
Allegedly the despatch was transmitted to
the Governor-General for the information of
the Federal Government. Actually—or, at
any rate, this paper assumes it to be the
true explanation —the despatch was sent out
for propaganda purposes, though what the
propagandist had in his mind Heaven alone
knows. The net effect of the farrago is to
make the Minister responsible for it. seem
like nothing so much as a rooster trying to
outcrow the poultry at Berlin. The story
is barren of anything dimly resembling infor-
mation. Boiled down, it is that the Allies
are giving the enemy beans, that the Iluns
are on the verge of quarrelling among them-
selves, that Ludendorff’s reserves are near-
ing complete exhaustion, that the time is
drawing closer when his defeat is inevitable,
that he is working “feverishly,” that the
Allies have complete confidence, that Focii
is using the French reserves with masterly
skill and Germany is drained of re-
sources. Turned upside down this is exactly
what the German is also saying. Further, it
is what some of the British newspapers and
most of the “experts” have been saying every
day for over three years. Some of it may
be true, if heavily discounted. But it is all
as stale as week-old mutton, and the general
effect of it a gross perversion of the situa-
tion it pretends to deal with. So far from
giving a picture of the position on the
Western Front capable of reconciliation with
recent events and current happenings, it is a
mere exercise' in the gentle art of camouflage
and an insult to the intelligence of the public.
When Australian citizens are told one moment
that their duty is to secure the enlistment of
every man capable of bearing arms and are
“officially” doped the next with bombastic
certitude about victory, the time is ripe for
someone or other to knock off. In most earnest
protestation The Bulletin puts it to the
Governor-General that further instalments of
this mischievous trash should be pushed back
to where they come from, with an intimation
that Australians are neither fools nor chil-
dren. The German may fail in his offensive.
This paper fervently hopes that he. will.
But nobody with the brains of a rabbit can
fail to see that he is making a stupendous
effort and that the British and French
armies are facing terrible trials. Nonsense
about the German being exhausted and all
the rest of it long ago became a sickening
substitute for the cold truth that the war is
shaking the Allies to the boots, and that all
hands are wanted on the job. The exploits
of the German armies, the fate of Poland,
Russia, Roumania and Servia, the tone
of the Reichstag and the attitude of
the Radical press behind the Rhine are all
against, the theory that the German yet feels
himself to be on the last lap. The best that
can be said is that the Allies in the West
have fought with superb courage against
an enemy still powerful and resolute, and
that the longer they can continue to hold
him down the better will be the chances of
resisting a made-in-Germany peace. Until
he is convinced that the Channel ports and
Paris are beyond his grasp he will keep on
fighting; and after everything has been said
he is certainly not convinced on that point
yet. Meanwhile recent activities by the
Navy at Ostend and Zeebrugge have been
cheerful to hear about. Over in Russia the
crop of chaos appears to be giving a record
yield. Apparently the Germans are simply
seizing one place after another as quickly as
they can shift around the landscape. So far
the only thing the Bear seems to have got
out of the revolution is a kick at either end.
If the German could find a third extremity
the poor beast would get it there, too.
Always the Nark.
A big thing in its way is the musical part-
nership which is being finalised by the Con-
ference in Sydney this week. When the
Universities got together in Melbourne in
1006 for the local jubilee, they discussed
joining to combat the English bodies which
examine here in music, and take thousands
of pounds out of the country every year. It
was arranged that Melbourne and Adelaide,
the only two with Chairs of Music, should
do the examining. Hobart supported the
scheme warmly from the first, and Brisbane
and Perth have done so since they came into
existence. Sydney was the Nark. Sydney
Uni. did not and does not teach music, and
was not proposing to play second fiddle to
the others which did and do. So the Uni-
versity scheme has all along had to battle
without the support of one of Australia’s
chief universities, and this has naturally
barred its claim to be a thoroughly Feder-
ated affair. Now that N. S. Wales has a
Conservatorium of Music, that body repre-
sents the Ma State, and the whole six are
at last federated for musical purposes. It
is still not quite a University federation,
Sydney Uni. being outside, but at any rate
it represents all Australia. The English
examining bodies can now fairly be asked
to withdraw, just as Oxford and Cambridge
withdrew from examining in ordinary sub-
jects when satisfied that Australia’s stan-
dard was high enough for educational pur-
poses. The standard here in music is now
high also, so there can be no valid reason
why they should remain. If they do we
shall have to educate Australia tQ view them
as mere fee-grabbers.
—
An important advance in Australian steel-
manufacture was assured by the Tramways
Conference held the other week, when the
engineers of various trusts from all the
States met to agree on the adoption of a
standard Australian rail. Up to now all
the tramway rails have been imported in
various sizes and styles. There was the
girder rail, with a narrow groove for the
wheel flange, used in N. S. Wales. The
Adelaide and Melbourne rails differed from
one another, and both were declared to be
better than the Sydney importation. Bris-
bane has the American standard rail. The
Conference decided on a uniform pattern
for all the States, so that Australian manu-
facturers can specialise on the one type
instead of dissipating on various types all in
limited demands. The object of the Confer-
ence was, practically, to establish a new
Australian industry, and one weeps over the
natural pathos of the Melbourne Argus re-
port of the gathering. Said that organ of
foreign-trade:—•
Another aspect related to the difficulty that exists,
owing to the war, in obtaining steel rails from
abroad. It was thought that for some time in the
future, at any rate, tramway rails would have to be
procured in Australia.
One can hear a wail of sorrow in those words
“for some time.” In the depths of its soul the
Argus is asking “How long, O Lord, how
long?”
“Two Seas” : Consider the business methods
of the Wheat Pool. Last November I was
encouraged by reports that the business was
“under entirely new management” to write
to the State Wheat Office in Sydney asking
for a quotation for a truck-load of damaged
wheat for pig feed. Most firms, run by
plain business men and not fool politicians,
will send me a reply in a day or two. They
want to do business—their tucker depends
on it. But I waited over a month before I
got a reply from Sydney informing me that
if I wrote to the Wheat Inspector at Goul-
burn I would be attended to. I wrote to
that Wheat Inspector the same day (January
31), and am still awaiting an answer to
my application to purchase some of the
wheat that is being allowed to get into such
a rotten state that even pigs won’t look at
it. Still, these hod-carriers and muck-
shifters who mag their way into Parliament
are looking around for fresh fields in which
to expose their ignorance, in spite of the fact
that they have wrecked every business they
have put their hoofs into. When will the
country kick them out of the road?
“Mamba”: Somebody’s bright idea of
starting returned officers again from scratch
makes me blink. An artillery officer pro-
moted from the field must undergo (Army
Orders) a course of five months’ training
at the school in England ; which means that
to make an officer re-enlist and regain pro-
motion involves a sinful waste of money,
besides being a queer way of recognising
long and (as proved by promotion) success-
ful service. Incidentally a quaint sort of
unit may be expected at the Front shortly:
ex-Colonels, ex-Majors and ex-Captains
shouldering rifles under their one-time cor-
porals and sergeants! What a chance for
someone to get a little of his own back!
From the Gallery:—
Watt’s troubles accumulate. The job of soothing
his wrangling majority whilst prodding his Cabinet
to greater energy is taking all the velvet out of his
voice and knocking the kewpie out of his smile.
One pressing worry is the clamor over the enlistment
of minors without their parents’ consent. The dis-
senting voices of parents have converged and united
in a wail of protest from Watt’s side of the House.
But Watt’s greatest nightmares are Pearce and the
Censorship. To be constantly sniped in the Chamber
over the futilities and follies of Pearce’s department
makes him yearn for more enlightened direction at
the Victoria Barracks. Then there is the Censorship
still guilty of unimaginable idiocies of suppression in
the case -of news of no military value. The other
night Tudor submitted to Watt a couple of galleys
of local comment from the Labor Caul, which the
Censor had practically blue-pencilled in globo. Even
a quoted par from the Patman’s organ, Liberty and
Progress, had been cut out entirely, and Tudor natu-
rally asked “Whaffor?” Watt looked at the copy in
blank amazement. The absurdity of the case made a
spoken apology seem superfluous.
Dr. Mannix pleads the resolutions of the
Paris Conference as an excuse for his alle-
gation that Australia is engaged in a “sor-
did trade war.” This speciously ignores
the fact that Germany began the war, and
that the avowed purpose of the Paris Con-
ference was to devise means to prevent Ger-
many from getting into a position to ever
wage such another. Why not also accuse
the seamen of Britain of sordid trade motives
in resolving that they will not work on
ships trading with Germany for a certain
period ?
Higgs’s demand for a Protectionist tariff,
the same to be handed out promptly, roused
the Shoddy papers to a fine exhibition of
astonished hypocrisy. They wanted to know
if Higgs wasn’t aware that the war had ob-
structed imports to an extent which made it
a very big tariff in itself, and they told Higgs
to go away and get information. Truly the
Pecksniff press, which has so long been selling
Australia to German importers, is an insti-
tution that never alters. The war is a tariff,
but it is a tariff that may stop at any
moment. No manufacturer can raise money
for any big enterprise if he is merely to be
a sort of warming-pan for the Shoddy paper’s
pet Plun, and is to have all the world’s cheap
labor turned loose on his high-wage industry
when he is no longer essential. “Come and
work for us till we can get our cheap alien
[Hun or otherwise! back again, and after
that you can go on the land or to the Devil,”
is the cry of the Freetrade paper.
Victoria has had little luck with its labor
leaders since the days of John Hancock,
the sterling soul who horrified Duncan Gillies
by thanking God in the Assembly for the
sacred right of Revolution. Now Elmslie,
the pleasant, patient, painstaking man who
was Labor Premier for 13 days in 1913, has
come to the end of a ] long, weary illness.
By trade a stonemason he got into promi-
nence first as a union secretary, and, winning
Albert Park in 1902, had very little difficulty
in holding it till his death.
Coneybeer, who has been Speaker of the
S. Aus. Assembly for the past three years,
is threatened with serious opposition from
George Laffer.
