Home and Territories Minister Glynn,
wffio doesn’t look a humorist, says he has
consulted three experts about the afforesta-
tion of Canberra, the capital of Australia.
Probably it is the only thing to be done
with it. No Ministry is worried over the
afforestation of London or Madrid, or even
of Melbourne or Sydney, but ’Berra is in a
category by itself.
An auction-sale of Melbourne city property
last week was a painfully earnest plea for
land-value taxation. Just 79 years ago,
when surveyor Robert Hoddle planned the
Melbourne of the future, he snapped up a
central block for £5B. Half of it remained
in possession of the Hoddle family until this
irunth. when it was “sacrificed.” A bid of
£70,000 for the larger portion was below the
reserve, and the rest was sold for £15,100.
So Hoddle’s investment of half of £5B in the
year 1839 is worth more than £85,000 to-dav
on the open market, though what the
Hoddles have done to make the increased
value is hardly worth mentioning. The
State which doesn’t recover the value it
creates throws away the property of the
people.
Blundell, being out of the S. Aus. Parlia-
ment, and therefore necessarily out of the
Ministry, has been given a billet as organis-
ing secretary to the “National” party in that
State. He says he is going to fight the Go-
Slow policy, “a hellish doctrine,” in the in-
terests not only of the nation, but of trade-
unionism, which, he declares, it threatens to
kill. He is quite right ; but if it isn’t
scotched it will probably kill the nation first.
After a long, long sleep on the proposal,
the Defence Department has almost decided
to do its own butchering for the troops in
camp and transport, instead of buying the
great masses of meat through contractors.
There never was any reason why the military
shouldn’t have been purchasers and slaugh-
terers of cattle on the hoof, nor was there
ever an excuse for the costly system of get-
ting the bread supply through contractors,
instead of establishing camp bakeries. The
public would be staggered to hear how much
money has been paid to certain bread-and-
meat contractors during the past 45 months.
The Westralian Government has closed its
State meat shops because they show a loss
and the Queensland Government is opening
more State meat shops because, even after
reducing prices, they show a profit. . The
chances are ten to one that the Westralian
man is right. Any public enterprise can be
made to look better than it is by plausibly
charging a portion of the expenses to capital
(which is loans), and most of them are
bolstered up that way; but the habit of mak-
ing anything look worse than it is by charg-
ing capital expenditure against revenue has
never taken root in Australia. Ryan’s
State has far more loans to play with now
than Lefroy’s, wherefore Ryan’s steak and
pig’s-trotter stall is likely to pay, while
Lefroy’s isn’t.
It’s a queer world. There are white men
all over the globe who would be proud to be
mistaken for Australians, yet there are people
in Australia who would like to see their
sons going to fight disguised as Harry
Lauder. And it is one of the penalties
we have to pay for the voluntary system that
we must humor them.
Captain S. M. Bruce, who won Flinders
(Vic.) for the Phewsion, got his wounds and
decorations as an officer of an English regi-
ment. The M.C. came to him for a game bit
of work at Suvla Bay, whilst the Croix de
Guerre was France’s reward for another
operation. Bruce was fighting beside the
French in the first days of the Great Adven-
ture, and was one of a number of special
performers who were asked to elect a man
to wear the Cross. The Victorian got the
decoration by the unanimous decision of the
others.
Clarence Wells Didier Daly, the fearless
young Tasmanian who led the Sixth in its
attack on a German officer’s trencli in Gal-
lipoli in August, 1915, has died of wounds
in France. Joining as a captain, he was
lieut.-colonel and D.S.O. when the end came
—and he was then only 28. He had been
in all the big stunts of the Sixth, and for a
time commanded the Second Brigade, which
he rallied at Menin-road last October, and
won a special mention for it. He was thrice
wounded, and was temporarily blinded by
shell-shock in Gallipoli, where he was buried
by an explosion.
“Peter”: The military police are getting
rather rude in the execution of their duty.
In an alleged hunt for an Italian reservist
they broke into a shop at Richmond (Vic.)
the other night, with distressing results to
two unfortunate infants within. A while
back a search for an Australian soldier led
some more of these untrained police to
burgle their way into a house at three in
the morning only to discover two or three
women in a state of collapse. If the diplo-
matists must make domiciliary visits at un-
godly hours, the Government might at least
provide them with a burglar’s kit or get
them instruction in expert entry from one
or two professors out at the “Farm.” Their
present methods are conducive to ill feeling
and cause damage to propputy. If Pearce
doesn’t mind the ill feeling, he will surely
bo touched by the damage to propputy.
Sydney daily paper item: —
Though a substantial deficit is anticipated Minis-
ters are not alarmed about the general position.
Notable characters who weren’t alarmed
about their own position were Harold
Skimpole, Wilkins Mic-awber (save for
casual spells of depression), Charles Surface,
the Jubilee Juggins, the Prodigal Son (till
he was driven to fall back on the natural
resources of the country in the shape of
husks and hogs), and perhaps Sydney’s own
Dan O’Connor. As for the people who aren’t
alarmed about the country’s position, they
include not only the above-mentioned Minis-
ters but almost everybody. There is heaps
of company on that road.

“Time, Please !” —the new featherweight
show put on by Espinosa—should keep the
wolf and the borehound from Sydney Tivoli
for weeks. With it the house makes a good
recovery from “The Million Dollar Girl” and
gets back to the cheerful level of “Samples.”
Barry Lupino and Espinosa are the prin-
cipals who do most of the lifting. They
are a bright, agile and busy pair, and Lupino
hangs up in the theatrical gallery a cari-
cature of an old head-waiter that ranks
with Field Fisher’s Alexis in “The' Girl in
the Taxi.” As he flaps about drearily in
bedroom slippers he moans at the humilia-
tion of having to prepare a private room
for two, and repents of having left his former
holy situation at the Wowsers’ Club. (The
Wowsers’ Club is one of the joyous inven-
tions of recent times.) In other sketches
he burlesques without effort a foreman, odd
jobber and vaudevillain, as well as sharing
an eccentric dance with Espinosa. A
Bairnsfather turn, with Barry as Old Bill,
armed with many famous jokes, reaches Aus-
tralia too late. Espinosa bursts into view
first as a staring “Buttons”; later he
is a stage Frenchman who fumes at the
ex-wowser waiter. But he makes his big
show as Midday in the Ballet of the Hours,
in which he shows his quality and gets
pretty close to flying. Beatrice Holloway
as Louise Svdney scores a pass in revue, and
young Noel Geddes is such a winsome young
woman that the smallness of her voice
almost escapes critical notice. Marie Eaton
attends to the songs that really have to be
sung and earns everyone’s thanks except
when she goes after a final top-note without
a step-ladder. Hugh Steyne is best as a
straw-haired idiot from Eton; Billy Itego
gets a few chances of speechless comedy;
Ethel Bennetto hovers about pleasantlv and
has a song to herself; and Zola Terrell
keeps her feet and dances weirdly with her
white arms. The Clints and Harry Whaite
are responsible for one or two striking
scenes, and there are some costume surprises.
Of a quiet show in a back street: —
Color at its most glorious ami design at its best
are to be seen at the exhibition of Modern Posters at
the Education Department’s gallery in Sydney. C.
Lloyd Jones collected an amazing variety of overseas
posters ; and, though the best of the French and the
Italian were submarined by the inartistic Hun, the
available English and American examples fill the
gallery with more genuine art than is usually sup-
plied by the annual exhibitions of art societies. A
special feature is the use the United States have made
of the poster to key up the community to prevent
waste of food in war-time. The London-Underground
Railways show a remarkable series, in which the
advertisement is subordinated to artistic and quaintly
humorous qualities. The exhibition is, paradoxically,
designed to show bill-poster firms how to make more
money for themselves by spending more on the artist.
The size of a poster, this exhibition shouts from its
dazzling walls, is of infinitely less importance to the
advertiser than good art. Also, it hints that the
way our men jumble posters together on hideous
hoardings, instead of displaying them symmetrically
and spacing them properly, lowers the advertising
value of each one. The show closes on May 28. All
profits go to the War Chest.
“8.5.” at another silent show: —
The Loan Collection of Australian pictures at the
Sydney G'allery deserves greater publicity than it has
received. Unless the fact of its existence is thrust
under the public eye every day, a great many people
will not know that it is there; hut the Government
does not approve of spending money on advertising
an exhibition of pictures. Much time and trouble
were spent by the director, G. Y. F. Mann, in get-
ting this collection together, from private owners and
the Melbourne Gallery. So thorough was his search
that he got far more pictures than he could or should
hang, and doubtless some hostile remarks have been
made about his rejections. Still the opinion of those
who know anything about the subject is that the
collection does represent the best art-work of the
last 25 years in Australia —except that Heysen does
not appear at all, for a reason which is unfortunate
but understandable. And it is believed too that the
collection shows better than anything else could that
a creditably high standard has been attained by
the Australians in their first quarter of a century.
Australian art virtually began in the ’nineties with
Conder, Roberts and Streeton. Before them were
immigrant artists who painted Australia as if it were
a part of Europe.
The small collection of Conder's decorative land-
scapes is rightly placed next to the Streetons. The
eccentric genius of Conder developed in a different
direction on his return to England, but he left his
influence upon Streeton, who was also affected by
Tom Roberts’s impressionistic methods (gained in
Europe, certainly), and carried them to greater
heights than either of his colleagues. The Streeton
panel is a magnificent one, its rich blues dominating
the room. Add the Streetons belonging to the Sydney
Gallery—“ Fire’s On,” “Still Glides the Stream” and
“A Cremorne Pastoral” —and one has an achievement
in landscape by a painter born and trained in Aus-
tralia of which any country might be proud. In
water-color Hilder is our top-notcher, and though
this collection of his work is small compared with
the memorial exhibition of 1916, it shows him at his
best, particularly in the big “Dora Creek.” Norman
Lindsay’s pen drawings put him in a class apart—a
class composed of such artists as Keene, Menzel,
Vierge and Phil May. There are also his remarkable
adventures in water-color, in which medium he may
go very far if etching does not entirely capture his
interest.
With the work of Streeton, Hilder and Lindsay
Australia can claim a respectable place in the art
world, and probably a higher one than any other
country has attained in such a short period. Lambert
might be added if it were not for the fact that his
technical mastery has been attained since he left
Australia. Gruner’s treatment of diffused light
through trees and on broken ground is another
triumph for the locally trained artist, and Long-
staff's portraits (though far from being a complete
collection) display a technical accomplishment of high
order.
The best paintings of David Davies, Tom Roberts,
Bunny, Phillips Fox, Julian Ashton, Bernard Hall
and some others are in this collection, together with
representative selections from Minns and a score or
more of other good men. Charles Wheeler, vyho has
scored a D.C.M. on active service, is a young painter
of the nude whose work has not been seen in Sydney
before, and is well worth seeing.
The sculpture is negligible—Australia does not
encourage the sculptor—but in pen and wash draw-
ings arid in etchings the exhibition shows what a
fine successsion of original talent we have had, from
“Hop” and Phil May to David Low, Percy Leason
and Ure Smith.
Allowing for the inevitable omissions, the Sydney
public is not likely to have again the chance of
seeing under one roof such a complete and satisfactory
collection of Australian art work as this, and anyone
who is interested in the finest expression of the
peaceful arts of this country will not miss it.

For her last concert of the present series
in Sydney Dolores had a huge audience. The
plump little lady keeps her voice and com-
plexion* free from any hint of Time the
Destroyer and turns the other peachy cheek
to volleys of applause. Ernest Truman let
the organ go at the right moment as the
lady’s flute-like voice warbled round “The
Bright Seraphim.” Staell’s fiddle assisted
when de Georgio’s “Ave Maria” was on the
menu. Pena repeated his popular “hit” on
the piano. And, throughout, the audience,
true to tradition, wanted more.
Though “De Luxe Annie,” at Sydney
Falace, is designed to mystify the audience,
the workmanship of the psychological comedy
is so clever that the spectator who sees it
a second time, though missing the delight of
the surprise denouement, gains as keen a

** * *
Winston Chufbhill’s “Crisis” at the Strand
in Sydney goes off in a great burst of fire-
works. the Battle of Vicksburg having been
specially designed to suit the movies. S.
Diane s impersonation of Lincoln is carried
out with striking fidelity, but the Virginia
Carvel fails to convey the charm of the
novelist s fair rebel. The same is to be said
of the heroine of “The Right of Way.”
Rosalie drawn by Gilbert Parker’s pen is
much more appealing flesh and blood than
her shadowy portrayer at the Globe. Mary
Pickford tells the story of “Rebecca of
bunnybrook Farm” in the charming Pick-
l'd s and the Haymarket, and
‘The Silent Master” holds his tribunal for
harsh landlords at the Crystal Palace. If
he had only hung out his sign a few years
back, what lie could have done to lessen the
sorrows of Ireland! Even Mannix might
have been different.

* * * *
Kathleen Macdonell is artistic in the
lighter passages of this unlawfully-domestic
drama, and convincing in her outbreaks of
violence, where her harshest notes are the
most natural under the circs. Her threat to
make things hot for the other girl seems to
harmonise with the passionate tint of her
hair, yet her frequent indications of good
nature seem genuine. Waldron, on ' the
other hand, is never quite natural save when
the dialogue breaks into heated altercation.
When be is exchanging helpings of melted
butter with his dear old friend and coun-
sellor Roy Redgrave, the effect is somewhat
mawkish on both sides, though Redgrave
carries rather more stage weight than did
Lewis Willoughby in the same part. Beryl
Bryant shows increased confidence and apti-
tude in the part of the cold, fond matron
who gets shocked back to propriety. Be-
sides, she has two valuable assets in her
voice and her face, which is said to be beauti-
ful —and looks it. Plumpton Wilson and
Brampton are well placed, and the staging
is good. “Daddy Long Legs” again on
Saturday.
* * * .1:
“The Masquerader,” at Melbourne King’s,
continues to utter its warning against the
use of morphia in immoderation. Guy Chil-
cote Post, in his hour of death, babbling
scornful remarks to his shadow on the wall,
in the vague delusion that he is addressing
Guy Loder Post, will point a moral and
appal the public until further notice. People
get hearty laughs as well as some good
shudders at the King’s these nights. Also
they remark favorably on the briefness of
the intervals. The stage is often in dark-
ness for a. couple of minutes, but the periods
of utter silence between acts are short, and
the audience’s interest in G. B. Post’s dual-
impersonation gets no time to cool. A Red
Cross matinge happens at the King's on
Tuesday next.

This is the fifth week of “The Bing Boys”
at Melbourne Majesty’s. There is no differ-
ence to record in the order of bings, except
that the “Doll Girl” song has been cut out,
and another encore is usually demanded from
the small kids in big belltoppers who pay
their terpsichorean addresses to Nellie
Pa.vne. One night last week the girlie in
red who dances a duet with Clyde Cook
appeared not so petite as Maie Baird, and
probably she wasn’t. Maie is a mere packet
oi sweets, and Cook holds her on high or
turns her over in his arms as though he were
Samson before he got a hair-cut. But on
this occasion the sprightly little lady seemed
a lump of delight that weighed heavier than
was expected, and Cook’s effort to make her
look like 3oz. was a comparative failure.

# ** * *
Alexander Watson’s first season at Mel-
bourne Athenaeum Hall is to close on Satur-
day in order that he may tour around, but
the strong demand for his recitals is bound
to bring him back to business in Melbourne
shortly. W atson is all things to all mono-
logues. Melbourne can’t remember another
reciter who rivalled him.
Saturday-night concerts at Melbourne
Auditorium help to keep the local popular
taste in music from dropping to the coon-
song level. The ballad concerts that
Chappell and Co. have arranged on a 2s. and
Is. basis strike the happy medium between
too classy and too commonplace. The best
of available voices get there in turn, and
the instrumental soloists are not too proud
to meet a respectable monologuist on the
programme. Blascheck had a say in the
proceedings last Saturday, and Cacialli, of
the late Italian Opera Co., was among the
singers.
At Melbourne Bijou Phrozo is still affect-
ing mechanical movement. A wonderful
engine hanging on her back is supposed to
give her motive force, though The Bulletin
suspects that she wouldn’t need the
machinery if there was a sudden shriek of
“Mouse!” Lew .Tames, a new comedian,
attempts to embellish a raw voice with a
Hebrew accent, whilst Con Moreni camou-
flages a naughty joke with an innocent man-
ner. Bertram and Raymond continue to
reiterate their mutual devotion, and a new
contortionist named Bonham is giving a life-
like imitation of a Phewsion politician.
Hoyt’s Melbourne house is showing Mary
Pickford as the sunshine of “Sunnybrook
Farm,” and her smile and her curls main-
tain an enchantment that seems to grow
richer with repetition. Mary, however, is
only a ray of innocence in the pervading
gloom of sex-drama this week. All those
who love the fair, frail lady and the glad
Vermilion Moment will get all they want in
the Pallas feature, “As Men Love!” House
Peters and Myrtle Stedman furnish some
clinging profiles for this murky production.
Melbourne Britannia began the week with
an exposition in Low Finance from the
“Shirley Kaye” heroine, Clara Kimball
Yeung. Clara comes to light again to-day
in a more feminine offering, “The Mario-
nettes,” with a new man, Corless Giles,
wearing the principal’s pair of trousers. At
the Melba the frank and flapperish June
Caprice led a three days’ spy-hunt in a Fox
comedy-drama, “Miss U.S.A.” For the
latter half of the week the star is Norma
Talmadge in “The Devil’s Needle.” This
film is a demonstration of drug-soaking,
beside which the performance of Guy Bates
Chilcote is a curate lapping milk.
** * *
“Grandad Rudd,” at Adelaide Royal, is a
better play than the old “Selection” so far
as it avoids melodrama on the one hand and
horse-play on the other. The talk is natural,
yet funny, and Bert Bailey gets every ounce
out of his part. That he once or twice
recalls the other veteran “Grumpy” is a side-
issue. Hero, heroine and villain are, as
before, slightly out of the picture through
not being up-country people like the others.
Several players are painstakingly funny.- but
for being natural the prize goes to Fred
MacDonald and Lilias Adeson. At the Tiv-
oli the old “Cabin” has been well staged.
Stanford is a dignified Uncle Tom, and little
Gladys Gaul a welcome change from some
too-mature Evas one lias known, and the
robust humor of Maud Goldspink at last gets a
chance in the part of Topsy. At the Majestic,
Mabel Morgan has a turn which recalls some
of her pantomime numbers, and Fred Bluett
has elaborated his old Boy Scout song. £oli
and Cornock have also dropped in out of
the panto.; but the recent programme has
needed a little more relief on the athletic side.
Wondergraph shows a strong Farnum film
in ‘‘The Conqueror,” which represents Texas
at real war with the Mexicans early last
century. Fighting is all over the screens
these times; even Olga Petrova at West’s,
though an American girl, gets into a Ruri-
tanian riot, in which her no-good husband,
who had skilfully pretended to be dead, gets
finished off in earnest to make room for the
local prince. The play is “A Daughter of
Destiny”—first time in Australia.

ANOTHER UNDISCOVERED CRIME.

‘Bully-t.”: On patrol in No Man’s
Land, our party called at an advanced
■outpost where a platoon was quartered to
see that Fritz didn’t come sneaking over.
The password was “Marjory,” and the night
very dark Presently a noise was heard of
someone feeling his way through broken wire
and shell-holes, and the sentry, waiting with
rifle ready, called : “Halt! Who goes there?”
Came a voice from the darkness: “I’ve for-
gotten her —— name, digger!” A little later
another noise and a challenge. Instead of
the password the reply came back: “I’m the
bloke with yer puddin’. Can’t yer see
the —— dixie on me block?” Of course
there’s an awful lot of bunkum about pass-
words; and Bill gets on to bunkum every
time.
“Rarawai” : The American guy remarked
that there was apparently no difference
between our Aussy headgear and that worn
by the Sammies. “No-o-o,” Billjim drawled.
“On’y ours is three years older.”
“Mimosa”: Under the new Repatriation
Scheme- an incapacitated soldier can have
his pension supplemented to reach £2 2s. per
week. And so it should be; but the trouble
at present in Queensland is over the matter
of deciding whether the man is fitted for
work or otherwise. Previous to this scheme
being introduced, the Officer in Charge of
the "Returned Soldiers’ Employment Office,
Department of Labor, had no difficulty in
obtaining employment for men willing and
able to work, and Queensland was without
unemployed soldiers; but since the Repatria-
tion Scheme has been in operation—that is
in regard to allowing men out of employment
£2 2s. per week —there are hundreds of un-
employed soldiers drawing sustenance, but
very few making application for work. This
obviously is a matter demanding strict in-
quiry, not because it means a waste of money
(I’m not worrying about that), but because
it is doing a lot of soldiers a lot of harm.
“Hamer”: This military habit of making
the best of "successful retreats,” and
“entirely satisfactory readjustments of our
line” may remain with us after the war; and
if we become thoroughly well trained in the
trick of rejoicing upon what seem very like
losses, life will become a more cheerful busi-
ness. In those days we shall be brightened
by items like this in the daily papers:—
The confidence of the creditors of the Gobung
Trading Co. is in no way impaired by recent proceed-
ings in the Bankruptcy Court, where it was ascer-
tained that a dividend of as much as eight pence in
the £ is likely to be paid. They point out that
they receive much more than 232 penee-in-the-
pound’s worth of experience, which will probably
save them thousands of pounds. The general manager
of the Gobung Co. has been relieved of that position.
He will be given an important post in the Com-
monwealth Bank.
With nothing but joyfulness and satisfaction
in all the affairs of life, the world will pro-
gress cheerfully backward until it arrives at
the asylum gates.

“Kopai” : These patriots! My Victorian
village owns, for its sins, two rival patriotic
societies which sell buttons and similar
emblems once a month and wrangle together
all the days between. _ Their chief bone ot
contention is as to which of the pair is the
official and duly accredited society, and
therefore the one to boss the sale of em-
blems. So far the
pack at the north
end has won, on
points. Anzac Day
was planned to put
up a record, and both
sets of patriots
sapped feverishly for
button - precedence.
On the Day the
North-enders sent
down to the South-
enders to know how
many emblems they
would like. The South
smiled back: “Thank
you. Wehevours!”
They had got in
the day before, and
were selling merrily
before the North
awoke! Upon which
the out-manceuvred
North jumped on its
hat and sent back its
own buttons unsold!
A Button Day with
us is worth about
£2OO, so this being an
extra-special effort
the boys probably lost
about £250 in the
scuffle. After three years of war we entrust
our amelioration fund-raising to the petty
jealousies of a crowd of snaggling women.
* * * *
“Centurion” : The Turk and Arab run every-
where to dog in large, mangy and utterly
useless quantities. I was quartered for some
time at Berassa, on the edge of the Libyan
Desert; it occupies part of the site of
the great Graeco-Egyptian city of Oxyrhyn-
chus. Here there is a vast chamel-heap in
which the inhabitants burrow to obtain the
rich dust of long-dead generations for top-
dressing their cotton fields. There is a
great open cut, something like the one at
Mount Morgan. The rich compost is sieved
and the bones thrown in heaps, the dust
being carried on donkeys and camels to the
canal, where it is dumped into barges. The
town, the mine and even the desert round
about are infested with a moving cloud of
dogs as smellful as the fine dust caused by
the mining operations. These dogs are big,
ferocious brutes which put in the day chew-
ing the more modern bones in the mine-
dump and by night howl and fight in the in-
tervals of stealing anything accessible.
Saddles, bandoliers, etc., were raided as fre-
quently as food stores; but any injury to

“Fitz”: It happened on a transport.
Somebody had forgotten to lock the door of
the crockery cupboard in the men’s quarters.
During the night a heavy sea worked up, and
when the ship gave a big lurch the unfastened
door flew open. With a noise like a simul-
taneous offensive on two Fronts a few thou-
sand plates and dishes smashed on the floor,
waking everyone but the night-watchman.
Then came a moment’s silence and a sleepy
voice; “The old man’s ’ome!”

‘'Mirrool”: My experience with regard to
the deduction of Is. all round for. “damages”
on troopships does not agree with that of
“Pills” (B. 9/5/T8). On our transport coming
home —over 1000 sick and maimed aboard —
the usual charge was made. The soldiers asked
the O.C. troops for a receipt of some sort;.
In fact, they went a bit farther than that.
Kesult: on payday the bob was entered, in
our paybook. The feeling on the subject
was so hot that the ship’s adjutant was
counted out when he was put up to address
the men on the subject of this wholly unfair
charge. By the way, and merely as illus-
trating how the soldiers’ pay is pecked at,
we had rather a peculiar charge made on
our way to Egypt in 1915. Two nights
before sailing our C.O. told us on parade
that our column had been invited to a per-
formance at a Melbourne theatre and that
he wanted all men to avail themselves of
the invitation. We were marched to the
theatre, saw the show and marched back to
camp again. The first pay 'On the boat was
3s. a man short. We were told that it was
to pay for the theatre party w r e were “in-
vited” to.

One day early this month the Devil bor-
rowed a 400-ton coal train coming up to
Sydney from the South Coast and used it as
a thunderbolt, but failed to register a hit.
The experiment began when a long string ot
coal-hoppers was stopped at Sutherland so
that the fireman and guard could see to the
brakes before the engine started the long
skate down a steep grade. While they were
on the line the train moved off, and when
the driver in the cab found it was out of con-
trol he jumped clear. The train roared on
and was three and a half miles away in four
minutes. Then an up-grade stopped it, and
it was running back when a truck got off
the line and ordered a halt. Had it switch-
backed over the second hill it would have
tried another swoop and a speed-burst ot
miles.
They don’t seem to take much notice 0!'
serious allegations at the Sydney Town Hall
these days. Recently there was a ballot for
the City Council’s representative on the
Water and Sewerage Board. As it turned
out, there was a tie, and the Mayor, who is
Joynton Smith, gave a casting vote which
put the old representative out. So far,
though this may not have been the usual
thing for a presiding officer to do, the Mayor
did what he at any rate had a right to do.
But when the defeated alderman-candidate
wrote to the Council declaring that the
other candidate and his chief supporter were
permitted, to .bring voters up to the ballot-
box and scrutinise their voting papers, a
question was raised which might reasonably
have been expected to lead to, something
violent. Yet the charge wasn’t even investi-
gated—the letter of complaint was merely
“received.”

THE LAY OF THE LAST RESERVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“This is a war of exhaustion.”
Klinger to hunger the grim nations stand
Above tiie countless graves of all the slain,
The guns outworn, the rifles in each hand
For lack of cartridges grown weak and vain.
Above them sing’s the lark. No aei’oplane
I-las means to keep its engine wheels at speed ;
And skeletons of armies in the land
Break into tears at mention of a feed.
Who shall be winner of this fearful fray
That makes no noise by day or sleepless night?
The beef is gone, the biscuit passed away;
This is a deadlier and a sharper fight—
Pull tight your belt; and God defend the right!
Nay, give us but one turnip large and fine,
One oyster in its shell of shining grey—
Sustained with these a man might break the line!
Lo, the munitions ! Through the moonless dark
One staggers with a pot of Irish stew.
Draw near and feast, ye valiant 1 No remark—
There is enough and just enough for two!
Nat heartily. Here is great work to do
All in grim silence knife and fork make play....
Kager they rise; and through the darkness—Hark !
Their wild offensive takes its headlong way.
Upon their stomachs all true armies fight.
Now up and at ’em. ye who have been fed I
The tumult sways across the startled night;
The last fierce word of war is swiftly said.
The leagues of dark are cumbered with the dead ;
And on one glorious plate of conquering stew
They turn the Teuton’s flank and break his right
And show the world what spuds and meat can do!
N.S.W.

“Shivers”: Another ghastly Sydney fire-
risk not named by the architects’ president
in his recent address is the horse-punt
crowded with motor-cars. An occasional
driver as he crosses the harbor will refill
his petrol-tank while his kerosene tail-light
is at work, or even with a lighted pipe or
cigarette in his mouth! Imagine what it
would be like if a well-saturated car burst
into flame in midstream! It takes eight or
10 minutes for a big punt to moor and clear
its load, and a lot of people can be grilled
even in eight minutes. Smoking should not
be allowed—it is prohibited on Government
punts—and all oil lamps should be put out
before cars are allowed aboard. Present
regulations are framed as if theatres
were the only combustible things around
town. The fire chiefs must have some bad
dreams when they look about and note the
possibilities.

“Phil,” somewhere in France, has a scrap
with his military conscience:—
Two of my sappers, dinkum Billjims, were missing’
when my party boarded the motor-lorry for home
after the day’s mix-up. A little later we passed a
Froggie’s cart on the road, and on the front seat,
driving the weary nag, were the missing ones! I
halted to investigate, and ordered them to dismount
and deliver. Whereupon one of them, with a mys-
terious, semi-tragic look, and with his finger on his
lip, said “Sh-ur, Cap.! Pork!” at the same time
lifting a corner of the canvas covering the cart and
revealing to my gloating eyes 10 succulent little
grunters. For one brief moment I faltered. The
temptation all but overpowered me. But just then
I remembered my early moral training (and also
the activity of the local gendarmerie), and sternly
putting aside the tantalising picture of real meat for
dinner I ordered them to let the live stock go.
While rejoicing in the knowledge that Billjim’s honor
was thus saved I am still wondering how it came
about that we had fresh roast pork for supper!

“Lhama”: Talking about the long limb of
coincidence. Winding through a defile in
the Judaean Hills to the east of Jerusalem
we came across a Taube that had been
wrecked in a duel months previously when
we were fighting for the foothills. It was
embedded in the sand of a wady, nose on,
balanced perpendicularly. The date of its
outgoing test was 30/10/T7: and it was the
afternoon of 30/11/T7 that it had its last
fight and its final plunge. Incidentally, it
suggests that the Germans haven’t many
aeroplanes to spare when a machine tested
in Germany bn October 30 is destroyed
in Palestine only a month later.

Freda Sternberg sees Uncle Sam at war:
Flags flew, bells tolled, the whole country talked
hard on Friday, April 5, the first anniversary of
America’s entry into the war. A few pessimists
dared to ask if the country had done all it might in
the year, but mostly it was a Hallelujah Chorus.
Taking into consideration the fact that not one
stroke was done before Wilson gave the word to
start, a vast amount certainly has been accom-
plished. The publicity work has been rn object-
lesson for the world. The Yank has b.een taught to
take his food very seriously. He has not been
brought up on ascetic lines, so it is not easy to
bring home the necessity for conservation. Still
the publicity is beginning to bear fruit, and quite a
number now take only three meals a day.
The third Liberty Loan is being filled at an amaz-
ing rate. All sorts of speakers are called into this
service. Billy Sunday has orated from many pulpits,
and Douglas Fairbanks, Mary Pickford and Charlie
Chaplin have deserted their studios to preach the
gospel of w'ar-saving stamps.
“War is a business” —that is Sam’s motto. And
as he has proved that success in business means
specialisation, every job he has to do in connection
with the war is done by a specialist. Thousands
of the best of them are “dollar a year men.” To be
a dollar a year man is to carry the stamp of super-
patriotism. It is these men’s wits against the wits
of the sharks who want here as elsewhere to make
money out of the war. In this connection one can’t
but observe the enormous value of the President’s
unselfishness. The money-maker in this crisis is
“no class.” There is no doubt about that.
WELL TESTED.
DOURTFUL CUSTOMER: “But are you sure it

MISTHER IZYMOSTHES : “ Shrink? Mine

There are 16 national camps. I have seen only
one. Several thousand seamen are in training at
San Diego. It's a wonderful sight, these men being
drilled amidst the beautiful buildings that were
erected for the great Exposition. One sometimes
wonders if the Yank will ever be able to get down
to real war conditions. At all the camps the men’s
huts are heated with steam and most of them have
flower gardens. Tulips, daffodils and hyacinths are
more beautiful than ever when they grow in Cali-
fornia, but—in a camp! “Why not?” asks Uncle
Sam.
His own objective is clear and splendid. “War,”
said one of the nation’s leaders to our visiting party
—“war has in the past spoiled men. ‘An old sol-
dier’—everybody knows what that meant. We aim
to make this war improve our men. We want to
make stronger men physically of the city weeds and
brighter men mentally of the boys from the country.”
There may be method in the daffodils, after all!

“Billy”: We had a new charwoman in the
other day, a simple soul with a firm chin and
a stout arm. While she was doing her job I
was busy on the pretties, using an electric
iron. This implement the scrubber regarded
with great interest. Then all of a sudden
the light broke in upon her. “ ’Tis the first
time I seen a flatiron tied up,” said she.
“Argh! Thim husbantls grow cunnin’er
ev’ry day, the brutes!”
“Country Ped”: The most worrying ques-
tion among the best settlers in isolated parts
is: “How can we get the kids educated?”
Victoria’s Education Department has made
an honest effort to answer it. In districts
where the number of .children available does
not justify a school, the Department has
itinerant teachers who travel from house to
house, gathering as many children as they
can at each stop. But that does not reach
all these isolated youngsters; so in 1914 the
Department, following a suggestion made by
an anxious mother in the Otway Forest,
began teaching children by correspondence.
To-day 70 boys and girls are being educated
in this way. The special staff formed for
this work operates as far as possible through
the parents: special time-tables are even
prepared to help them. Some children thus
taught have already surpassed expectations
by gaining qualifying certificates at the usual
age.

“F.A.”: Fritz’s airmen don’t always
trouble to mark the positions of the “spotted’*
guns on a map for the benefit of their artil
lery, One night after our battery had been
snugly fixed in the same position for a week
or more, four lights dropped from the sky-
one light in front of each of the four guns.
Of course a wise O.C. who was free to act
would have taken the hint. For some reason
(probably quite a good one) ours didn’t, and
about 2.30 the goods were delivered. The
first salvo got two of the guns and the other
pair went within a minute or so.

There’s always someone picking at the paths we walk
upon,
There’s always someone digging up the drain.
There’s always someone mourning- for the giants who
are gone,
And talking of the pigmies that remain.
There’s always some “I told-you-so” who knew but
couldn’t speak— S
In fact, there’s every sort of nark and bore.
But I’d rather have the lot of them and board them
for a week.
Than meefr the dreary citizen whose heart has
sprung a leak—
The busted undertaker type who tells you with a
squeak,
“I don’t see how we’re going to win this war!”

Captain Murray G. Patten, M.C., who
was killed in action on April 4, had a distin-
guished career at the Melbourne ’Varsity.
He graduated M.A. with a first-class, and
after lecturing in logic and philosophy at
Ormond College for three years, went to
Europe and studied philosophy at Edin-
burgh, London and Freiburg. When the call
came, the brilliant student gained a commis-
sion in the corps raised at Edinburgh ’Var-
sity, and later was attached to the Northum-
berland Fusiliers, with whom he died.
Heitmann, M.H.R., has volunteered for
military service. Price, M.L.A. for Albany,
was the first Westralian legislator to go : he
is still at the Front. Cornell, a goldfields
M.L.C., also got into khaki ; but he is back
as sergeant seeking re-election. Heitmann
is a man of some quality. A Day Dawn
miner originally, he won into the State Legis-

Jerry McDougall, a Recherche Bay
(Tas.) blacksmith and sawmill manager—
officially 4061 Sergeant Stanley Robert
McDougall—has won the V.C. A fine, bullet-
headed, thick-set hero he is—as cool as an
anvil on a Sunday evening. His chance
came in the Ancre line, near Albert,
where a few weeks later his younger bro-
ther Wallace, 22, was blown to bits between
machine-gun fire and a German barrage. As
the grey waves loomed through the mist,
Jerry the blacksmith dashed out and killed
seven of the Huns with his bayonet. Then
he seized a machine-gun, and, firing it from
his hip, routed the remainder of the second
wave, which was joining the first. Ammuni-
tion ran out, and he charged anew with the
bayonet, killing three Fritzies and saving a
Billjim officer’s life. At this stage he
spotted another Lewis gun, dropped by the

Years ago, in Wollongong (N.S.W.), two
printers tossed up to see which of them
would take a job as newspaper manager at
Mess Vale. The man who won and made a
moderate fortune was the late Speaker
McCourt. of N.S.W. Assembly. The loser,
Spencer Cornford, stayed on the Inky Way’s
sporting track, and as “Early Bird” wrote
sport till the end, which came last week in
Sydney. He was 68.
Dr. Walter Norman, dead in Adelaide at
67, had lived in retirement for years, owing
to failing eyesight, but he was a well-known
man in the old immigration days. He was
doctor in charge of immigrant ships for
years, acting in turn for three of the States
and Maoriland.
* William English must about take the
damper for the longest continuous service in
a bush school. lie will this year complete
his 40th year at Bandon, near Dungog
(N.S.W.). Three generations have seen
him chalk figures on the blackboard.
Major Charles Johnson, of Langwarrin
V.D. camp, is to be the pioneer Director-
General of V.D. establishments for the Vic.
Government, taking charge as soon as the
military can find the right man to fill his
place. Johnson is an Australian and one
of the Commonwealth’s front-rank bacterio-
logists. In his research years at Edinburgh
he was the right-hand man of the eminent
Prof. Greenfield ; and in this country, before
the war started, he gained considerable pres-
tige in the treatment of phthisis. As for his
work at Langwarrin, the latest records show
an amazing 300 per cent. of.success.
Passed out at Sydney, in the 71st lap,
Joseph Brooks, who was in charge of the
Ma State’s trigonometrical survey from its
start till 3907. Brooks came from the Cold
Country to South Aus. as a nine-year-old,
and was schooled in Adelaide, where he also
learnt his job as surveyor. In 3877 he got
a billet in the N.S.W. Lands Department,
and shortly afterwards began wandering
around the State with a portable observa-
tory to fix positions by astronomical
methods. From the base line at Lake George
(N.S.W.) he then started a series of trig,
surveys not even yet fully completed, as the
war has hung them up for the past three
years. When the new job of Director of
Surveys was created .11 years ago, Brooks
reckoned that his field work gave him a
mortgage on it; but the title went to Furber,
the computer at head office, and Brooks re-
signed in disgust. Since then he had amused
himself at astronomy, and was selected as
chief observer on three scientific expeditions
to Tasmania and Tonga for solar eclipse
purposes. For many years he raised a melo-
dious tenor voice with Sydney Liedertafel,
of which he was one of the founders.
One Reuben Iveirl, mostly of Melbourne,
has been before a London police-court earn-
ing three months on a charge of masquerad-,
ing as an officer of the Sydney. Reuben has
had a mottled career. During his earlier
operations in Victoria as Several sorts of
showman and lecturer, his success in evading
the notice of the authorities during his more
dubious enterprises was generally ascribed to
his being “a pal of Tommy Bent’s.” One of
his more notable cash-raising schemes was
the exhibition of a two-headed infant,
pickled in spirit. The State impounded that
trophy. Very much later he was up on a
charge of masquerading in uniform, but he
slipped out from beneath the hoof of the
Law with his usual dexterity. And even on
this occasion in London it was admitted that
the High Commissioner was very much in-
terested in him. There seems to be a string
somewhere.
When that most interesting Chinaman in
Australia, Louis Ah Mouy, just dead, was
engaged for Australia, 07 years ago, he was
a ship’s carpenter in the British mercantile
marine, reading English easily and speaking
it well enough. Captain Glendinning hired
him to. come and build six wooden houses—
three in Williamstown and three in South
Melbourne. He brought timber, Chinese
cedar, sufficient for the job. At that time
there were not more than 20 houses at either
place, the Marco Polo Hotel at South Mel-
bourne, still standing, being one of them.
After completing his contract and pouching
the money, Ah Mouy started as a carter to
the Ballarat and Bendigo goldfields. He got
£l6O a ton for loads to Ballarat and £2O
more for a load to Bendigo. Writing to his
brother at Canton to come and* join him on
tin new Tom Tiddler’s ground, the letter
was intercepted by the Chow authorities,
the news went around, and within two or
tin ee years there were 37,000 Chinese on
the goldfields. Ah Mouy turned the rush of
cheap labor to account in opening up mines
in the Yea district and elsew'here, and was
soon a rich man.

Master, but has been in private practice
since, has just en-

his experience, but
he has permission to
knock again after

what is pretty cer-
tainly a record —two
ex-Attorney Generals
on active service,
Denny and Vaughan.
LEONARD
RODWAY,
Tasmania's Botanist,
and father of
Florence, the artist.
(’harles Gray, secretary of the Vic. Trades
Hall Council, holds his head higher these
days: son Rowland, who left for France a
couple of years ago, has won the D.C.M. He
has a regimental sergeant-major’s job.
At Longwood, the little town in the Kelly
country of Victoria that has won’everything
from the D-C.M. to the V.C., is St. Helena,
the home of the Tubb family, who secured
one of the district’s two Victoria Crosses
and one of its numerous M.C’s. To para-
phrase Henry Lawson, “there’s a road from
St. Helena, . there’s a road they all must
take.” It leads straight into the firing-line.
Among the adventurous lads who travelled
it is Private Frank Sanderson,' whose
narents live close by. Four years ago, aged
37. he left Victoria on a Finnish sailing-
ship, reaching England just after war broke
out. He joined the Royal Marine Light
Infantry—the “giddy harumfrodites” who
heard the word “Over you go, Royals!” at
Zeebrugge the other day. Having helped to
sink several submarines, and been nearly
sunk himself by a tinfish which missed his
ship by a couple of feet ( but the submarine
which sped it forth will never loose another),
Sanderson transferred, and was at the Battle
of Jutland. Later he went to France with
the Naval Brigade, and now lies wounded
at Abbeville. His brother, in training for
the Church, went from Gallipoli to France,
where he was killed after gaining his com-
mission.
MISSED OPPORTUNITIES.
THE MAIDEN: “ You know, I do enjoy these
delightful birthday parties so. Yet I
never have celebrated one of my own!"
THE BRUTE: “Isn’t that too bad! And yon
with so many opportunities!”
An unusual pair of Australians are help-
ing the Sammies to go safely to sea. Hinton
White, a son of the late William White,
one of W illiamsto wn’s early-day ship-
builders, left Australia for the U.S.A. in
3.893 on a lecturing enterprise. He told
about Australia for 10 years, all up and
down the States, during which time he
married a Vassar girl. Brother Wilfred
followed to the U.S.A. five years later, adven-
turing, and presently found himself with a
job in an old compass-adjusting firm in
Boston. He‘also married a U.S. girl; and in
time he climbed up to become the American
representative of the renowned British firm
of Kelvin and Baird, with a partnership
therein; and brother Hinton came into the
business with him. Nowadays, sent for by
Washington to control the manufacture and
supply of nautical instruments for the U.S.
Navy. Wilfred (now Dr. White) is Yankee-
land’s boss expert in the business, and runs
three factories under the eye of the Govern-
ment.
It recently befel Gurney, of the N. S.
Wales Government Entomologist’s Depart-
ment, to visit a State farm in the Northern
Rivers area, with a view to dealing with cer-
tain potato-bugs, or boll-worms, or something
which were organising an offensive on the
crops. His department heralded his advent
by wiring: “Gurney arriving Tuesday re
maize pests.” Cows, of course, are the local
goddesses, and the receiving telegraph-
operator Sent out the wire this way:
“Guernsey arriving Tuesday re maize tests.”
As a result, Gurney found that a paddock of
maize had been placed at his disposal for
meals. It quite took away his appetite, and
not until lie had denied the fact on oath
would anyone believe that he was not a cow.
THE SIEGE GUNS AT ARMENTIERES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The Australian Siege Battery, caught in the
maelstrom at Armentieres, fought desperately for 24
hours. They extricated nil their guns. Theirs was
the last battery to leave. They refused to retire until
the enemy was outflanking them.”
The siege guns do not gad about ;
They much prefer in peace to sit;
And if you start to prod them out
From their secluded concrete pit
A dozen tractors you will need ;
The heavies are not built for speed.
If we retire they first depart;
They want at least a fortnight’s start.
But once they stayed just where they were—
Our siege guns at Armentieres!
Flung in the German rush to stem,
We infantry were backward pressed,
Until we found ourselves with them,
Our gunners, fighting breast to breast.
For they had stayed; each monster gun
Had waited just to see the fun.
Amid the gas, the sleet of shell
Those rest-eanip gunners gave them hell;
All day and night they did their share,
Our gunners at Armentieres I
With grey-clad masses on their flanks,
Those gunners harnessed up their guns;
They yanked those monsters big as tanks
Beyond the flood of swarming Huns.
They didn’t hurry much, ’tis true;
They couldn’t if they’d wanted to.
And yet this record proud they made:
Theirs was the battery that stayed,
The last to leave, the last to dare, i
Our siege guns at Armentieres !
x.s.w.
ARTHUR H, ADAMS.

In these days it is a fortunate banker who
knows his own profits. At the best of times
profits are a guess; but because the future
is much more uncertain than usual, profits
are far more of a guess than usual.
What we call present profits depend upon
future behavior. Really it is a very clumsy
proceeding to cut a transaction running over
many years into six-month lengths and
before the transaction is completed appor-
tion a certain profit to each period as it
passes. What should we say of a farmer
who, having sown his wheat, went to his
bahker at the end of the first month, with
the crop only just showing among the clods,
and said that he had made a profit of £100?
Yet the banker’s own position only differs
from that in this respect: every six months
some transactions are completed and the final
results are visible. If conditions were always
the same it would thus be possible to arrive
pretty well at the fair proportion of profits
to be credited to any term. But seeing that
conditions vary, the true figures even for
the present can only be determined by a
prophet.
This paper can't get a prophet’s license; so
the best it can do is to consider what a
future built upon present foundations is
likely to be. First, in case of defeat. De-
feat —something that could plainly be recog-
nised as such —would of course be followed,
if not by demand for Australia, at any rate
for a huge money indemnity or corresponding
advantages in connection with Australian
trade, especially in the matter of the supply
of raw materials. We have to face that
alternative to victory. It seems hopeless to
expect that victory should mean a similar
indemnity for us. Wo, shall have to pay our
own bill. But this paper isn’t so sure that
the payment of the bill is from the national
point of view going to be the most serious
thing in the future. If Smith and Jones
and Brown lend their Town Council £50,000
apiece, it may be very awkward for some of
the townspeople to produce their share of
the interest and sinking fund ; but as long
as Smith and Jones and Brown remain
townspeople and spend their money locally,
the raising of the money to pay them may
not be so difficult. Similarly the huge in-
ternal debt that we are asked to believe is
going to smash Germany will probably do
nothing of the kind either. Our foreign debt
will cause a lot more trouble; indeed, our
whole salvation lies in the fact that the
Commonwealth has for the most part done
what The Bulletin had been told consist-
ently for the previous 20 years was impos-
sible. However, let that go. The point
that has to be made now is that the payment
of interest and repayment of principal of the
big local war debt will probably not cause
much national, though it may cause some
individual, discomfort, A far more serious
disturbance will be caused by the more or
less sudden stoppage of the expendi-
ture of war-loan money, not only here
but elsewhere. The direct effect of
that on us will be a large amount of un-
employment ; the indirect effect a tremendous
fall in prices of some of our staples. If
we and the rest of the world can pass through
that experience without a financial crisis our
luck will have changed.
The - extent to which banks are preparing
to meet the highly probable shock depends
less perhaps upon -what they show than upon
what they conceal. You can see what re-
serves there are but you don’t know" what
reserves are wanted; and you can’t know
that until you know" the prudence (or other-
wise) with which advances have been made.
Still you can get some idea of the policy
which has been adopted from some such com-
parison as this:—
Liabilities.
1914. 1917. 1918.

Total liabilities now are £15,336,768, which
is a growth of £2,799,092 in the three war
years. Of that amount no less than
£2,212 933 is held liquid, and it is fair to
assume that more than twice the other half-
million -was employed financing the Wheat
Pools on March 31. And that is really ad-
vances to Government, too. So we come to
this: that over half a million more than
all the amount added to deposits, etc., since
the war started is held liquid, or as liquid
as the needs of the Government allow. This
suggests that at auy rate the National’s
directors are not deceived as to the real
nature of the present “prosperity.”

The control of this concern is in the hands
men with a long vision. Two years ago,
for instance, they proceeded .to do something
which has only in connection with the last
v\ ar Loan come to he generally regarded as
the right thing. “We know approximately
what amount will be added to our funds
each year,” they said in effect. “We know
also pretty well what other investments we
shall be able to realise within the next year.
It is our duty to make that money available
to the Commonwealth if we can get some-
body to make us temporary advances —some-
body whose business it is to make tempo-
rary advances and not permanent invest-
ments.” They found the somebody in the
Commonwealth Bank, which handles mil-
lions of Savings Bank money; and to the
M.L.C.’s bold policy must be credited the
setting of the pace in the matter of War
Loan subscriptions—it talked to other big
concerns in millions. The directors, who
cut their report down to a few brief sen-
tences, merely say on this point:—
Assets.—The assets amount to £12,763,735, of
which over 50 p.c. is invested in Government securi-
ties. The whole of the co.’s accumulations since
August, 1915, have been invested in War Loans.
But the trustees, who were appointed on
the amalgamation with the old M.L.A., are
a little more loquacious :
The recent subscription of £2,000,000 to the Com-
monwealth AVar Loan and £lOO,OOO to the Maoriland
War Loan brings the total financial assistance of the
co. to Empire war finance to over £7,000,000, a
fact of which we think policy-holders mav well be
proud.
Certainly the whole £7,000,000 hasn’t been
paid over yet; and the last £2,100,000 does
not come into these accounts, anyway. But
it is interesting to see how what has so far
been done has been managed. Take Decem-
ber, 1914, as a datum point; and this is how
it works out:—
Shareholders’ capital increased .. £91,775
Assurance funds increased. . .. 1,553,113
Sundry creditors increased .. 1,742,478
Loans on mortgage reduced .. 1,013,907
Total .. ..£4,401,273
The actual net increase in the holding of
Government securities in the three years has
been £3,774,173 : so that in addition to the
investments in War Loans the co. was able
in the early part of 1915 to invest a quarter
of a million in Canada, where it had been
invited to establish itself before the war. to
secure the freehold of its Sydney premises
and to add a little to its loans on policies.
But while the M.L.C. broke down a con-
vention and showed that it was for this
supremely important national purpose pre-
pared to borrow on the credit of its expected
accumulations, it saw clearly enough that
it could not do that and also take unknown
policy risks. And that war risks were un-
known risks has been clearly enough demon-
strated during the past three years. At the
outset, when it was going to be a march to
Berlin, offices issued war policies cheerfully
enough at 5 p.c. To-day some British office's
are charging 20 p.c. That men who go out
to fight for the nation should have to pay
anything at all extra for insurance is abomin-
able. The nation should pay ; there is very
little more reason why those who have in-
vested in insurance policies should do it
than those who have invested in bank or
brewery or shipping or any other shares.
There is an obligation upon holders of in-
surance policies to forego their bonuses, as
they are now asked to do, in order that
married men with dependants who go to the
Front may be provided with insurance
cover; but there is a heavy obligation also
upon investors in every other class of
security, and it is to be hoped they will not
lose sight of it.
The M.L.C. is a co-operative co. There
are shareholders who have guaranteed the
face value of the policies plus declared
bonuses and also a maximum expense rate.
In return for that —which is, among other
things, a guarantee of economical manage-
ment —the policy-holders in the Ordinary
department agree that the shareholders shall
have up to one-fifth of the profits that are
made. How this incentive to effective man-
agement works out: the trustees of the Aus-
tralian Widows’ Fund section certify in
their annual report:—
The great advantage of union to our policy-holders
is strongly evidenced by the fact that for the last
quinquennial period before the union the proportion
of surplus to premiums received by the Australian
Widows’ Fund was 14.6 p.c., whereas for the year just
closed it was 37.0 p.c.—a wonderful increase.
Of £254,357 of surplus divided for 1016 the
shareholders took £33,794. For 1017 the
actuary recommends “that £247,025 be
divided in accordance with the articles of
association and the amalgamation agree-
ments.” This means a slightly reduced
bonus —so far as M.L.C. and old Citizens’
policies, at any rate, are concerned. Com-
pared with the previous year (or with 1014)
the reversionary bonus on a whole-life policy
of 10 years’ duration is now £2 12s. as
against £3; on an endowment policy pay-
able at 60, £1 15s. as against £1 18s. per
£lOO of insurance in each case. The total
surplus is reduced partly by reason of
claims arising from the war (“claims by
death include £148,627 of war claims,” the
directors report), and partly by the adop-
tion throughout of a 3 p.c. pure premium
valuation. This, while reducing the present
bonus, makes it easier to pay larger bonuses
in the future. It means in plain English
that, throughout, it is now assumed that
future earnings will be only 3 p.c., and
reserves have been left sufficient to meet all
obligations if no more than that is earned.
All that is earned over 3 p.c. is available for
bonuses. The funds earned £4 17s. 2d. p.c.
last year. But this does not mean only an
increased capacity for paying bonuses. It
means a reserve for contingencies. This is
in addition to the general reserve of £.100,000
in the Ordinary department.

If the figures for every year were like those
for the past year The Bulletin would have
to confess that it was quite wrong in suggest-
ing, as it has done hitherto, that the eo.
should change its plan for distributing pro-
fits. But the past year has been exceptional
—so exceptional that, after making the usual
distributions, there is more to lay by than
there had been in the previous five. In those
five years, in fact, nothing had been put by
at all, but £12,000 of old profit had been
divided. Indeed, reserves have to be
encroached upon when the underwriting
profit is less than 20 p.c.; for after paying
S p.c. to shareholders 20 p.c. of the pre-
miums are divided equally between share-
holders and policy-holders. In the early
days of the co., when the business was very
select, a2O p.c. profit was easy; to-day when
this and other Australian cos. are in the
general scuffle for business, it is much more
unusual. Last year, as this next table shows,
the loss rate was exceptionally low: —

We find results of the inflation of prices in
m^st, unexpected places. Further, though
some* stocks are exceptionally light others
are exceptionally heavy because the people
who need them for manufacturing purposes
or for sale don’t know when they will be
able to get fresh supplies. Big stocks and
high prices mean big insurances; and that
no doubt explains how this co. can show a
reduced expense rate. Further, owing to
the bogus prosperity which is afflicting us,
the. moral hazard is greatly reduced. In
plain language, just at present it doesn’t pay
anybody to have a fire; and it does pay
thousands of people to take greater precau-
tions against fire than ever they took before.
Which largely explains the exceptionally low
loss rate. Later on
Taking risks (so far as one can judge
them by premiums) as a basis for compari-
son, the Australian Mutual is one of the
strongest fire offices in the world. This is in
a large measure due to the fact that for
many years the business was very conserva-
tive. At the end of 40 years the Australian
Mutual had a premium income of only about
£50,000. It has increased it by 50 p.c. in
the past five years. The result" of the slow
early development—which mostly meant a
very careful selection of risks —was that at
the end of 40 years its total funds were
times greater than its risks, as measured by
the premiums for a year; and even now thev
are three times greater. This is very nearly
twice the average strength of the leading
English cos. In bank balances, War Loan
and other liquid assets alone it has nearly
three times as mu,ch as the losses amounted
to in the worst year the co. ever experienced;
and it has another £145,000 in city property
and mortgages.

The latest copper-smelting plant to be erected in
Australia is that of the Mount Cuthbert Company
in North Queensland. It consists of buildings, power-
plant with boilers, generating sets and blowers,
blast furnace, copper converters and converter re-
fining plant, blister gathering and cleaning shed,
also steel bins for ore and coke. Wherever possible
the plant is electrically driven. The whole of the
plant was supplied by the Austral Otis Engineering
Co. of Melbourne, who have made numerous smelting
plants working in Australia at present.— Advt. f 2

Besides the bonus capital of £3,250,000,
carrying 6 p.c. preference, there is £250,000
ordinary capital subscribed by the parent
co., which thus gets all the divided- profit
in excess of the 6 p.c. paid to the prefs. The
latter, by the way, have no direct say in
the control of the co.; and if they can exer-
cise any indirect control they will be pretty
clever. In the three years the disclosed
profit of the two cos. has been :
O.S.R £829,155
Ditto (Fiji and N.Z.) .. 1,007,178
Total £1,836,333
Efen assuming that the cos. have not made
anything beyond that, they have still done
so well that, if it didn’t seem so like sacri-
lege, this paper would feel impelled to
exhort the Board to buck up. No capital
has been actually subscribed by shareholders
since 1907. when £l5 per share was paid for
15,000 £2O shares. That took the capital
to £2,500,000. Assume that the whole of
that was paid by shareholders in cash, then
the profits for the past three distressful
years of war have been equal to 73 p.c., an
average of just under 25 p.c. p.a. Whether
that is a fair burden to put upon an in-
dustry is a matter which scarcely admits of
argument.
We are, however, only concerned with
that here so far as it is likelv to affect the
future of the shares as an investment. What
case can the co. make out for itselfV First,
of course, it will urge that the greater part of
the profit is made out of the Fiji plantations
and mills and the M.L. refinery and connec-
tion. That is true enough, from one point
of view ; though some of the profit is really
made out of Australia, where a market for
the surplus Fiji production is found. But
this part of the profit is not made out of the
Australian producer. Governments which
permitted the co. to make such vast profits
in the past that, after paying 12 p.c. for
years, it could double its capital by produc-
ing £3,250,000 of undisclosed assets, cannot,
however, fairly go back and say that the co.
is now entitled to make profits only on the
capital actually subscribed by shareholders.
But they are entitled to say, as they
have said or tried to say in connection
with gas cos., that the C.S.R. shall not make
such profits again—that it shall make charges
sufficient to show, say, 7 p.c. on the present
value of its assets, and no more. So far the
Queensland Government, which is the most
interested, has attempted to deal with what
it feels is a rapacious monopoly by methods
which have admittedly failed; but it is not
likely to stop there. The great financial
strength of the co., backed by the nimble wit
of the management, has saved it from many
assaults in the past; and it is for the in-
vestor to decide whether that is going to save
the co. from the attempts at regulation which
are bound to come in the future. Nothing
could be much worse for the cane-growing
industry than the uncertainty which at
present prevails. Here and there a grower
is doing well; but for the most part the
heart is being ground out of him between
the upper and the nether millstones.
The Queensland Government has at last,
in consequence of “the disclosures made in
the report of the Gas Referee,” requested
the Auditor-General to make an examina-
tion and audit of the accounts of the South
Brisbane Gas Co. Some action upon that
report was undeniably called for. Either
the Gas Referee, a semi-judicial officer, was
violently wrong in his conclusions or the
management of the co. had been guilty of
something very like conspiracy to defeat the
law. The Bulletin some time ago called
upon the Government to take action, which
it thought might be done by a prosecution
for conspiracy. The directors were autho-
rised at their last general meeting to pro-
ceed against The Bulletin for libel for
making this suggestion. The directors have
now, however, issued a circular to the share-
holders : —•
There is not even a scintilla of truth in the
charges made, nor can we conceive there is a single
shareholder who will for a moment believe that the
directors and others aimed at would stoop so low as
to sully the reputations they have built up for
honesty and honorable dealings. Under the circum-
stances, therefore, we do not think any good purpose
can be served by spending the shareholders’ money in
prosecuting The Bulletin.
That is a matter entirely for the directors
and their advisers. The one thing about
which this paper is concerned is that the
charges made by a public officer and the im-
plications shall be'Cleared up. The Govern-
ment is evidently of the same mind; and
the appointment of the Auditor-General to
make an examination of the accounts is a
very proper first step. After that The
Bulletin will consider the propriety of
taking its own action upon the directors’
circular.
# * * *
“Good Australian”: It will probably be
too late for this protest to have any effect,
yet it is well to put on record that one of
the Commonwealth’s three finance bills con-
tains a dangerous departure in public finance.
Introducing the War Loan Securities Repur-
chase Bill Treasurer Watt stated that “its
primary object was to provide the Treasurer
with money out of War Loans to purchase
War Loan stock and ke_ep the market
healthy.” Another expression he used was
“to take the slack out of the market.” Watt s
defence for asking this power to buy bonds on
the Stock Exchange with loan money was
that the State Government always had the
power to use sinking funds to buy its own
stock on the open market. Now this new
power has an entirely different aim. He
asks for the right to set aside £1,000,000 of
loan money to dabble in Commonwealth
bonds on the Stock Exchanges of Australia.
Of all the dangerous, delusive and damaging
schemes to kill the market for war bonds
and create public distrust this surely is the
very worst. It is merely a revival of the
old land-boom methods in Melbourne, where
failing banks, building societies and finance
cos. used their own capital to buy in' their
own shares on a constantly dropping mar-
ket. The whole business looks sinister, and
some bad advice is obviously being given
the Treasurer by a person or persons accus-
tomed to a deceptive Stock Exchange prac-
tice of supporting a falling stock.
* * * *
From Gerald R. Campbell:—
In the timely article dealing with the subject of
the Insurance of Soldiers, The Bulletin concludes
that policy-holders in life offices should be prepared
to forego current bonuses in order that soldiers may
obtain relief from the present heavy premiums for
War Risk Policies. Certainly some general scheme
in this direction is most necessary in all Australian
life offices, if recruiting is to be facilitated and the
soldiers fairly dealt with by the civilians who do not
take the war risk. In our largest mutual life office,
at any rate, something has been done in the Ordinary
department at the cost of bonuses—especially that one
just declared—and it is to be hoped that the relief
will be still further extended by the management.
But I think you will agree that the burden must not
fall only on the policy-holders’ bonuses. In proprie-
tary or co-operative life offices the shareholders’
bonuses should also clearly share the burden; for
these, too, are portions of the surplus, which almost
entirely arises from premiums (and interest thereon)
paid by policy-holders in excess of actual require-
ments ; and the recent report of the most successful
Australian office of this class shows that shareholders’
bonuses are matters of some importance.
* * * *
Mammoth Molybdenite (N.S.W.) is giving
the Sydney Stock Exchange plenty of excite-
ment. The concentrating plant, like others
of its kind, takes knocking into shape, and
arrangements for sorting ore are primitive.
In a run of 85 hours it treated 101 tons
assaying 1.35 p.c. for 22161 b. of 85 p.c.
molybdenite. Excessive sliming involved a
heavy loss of mineral, but some of this is
recoverable. These teething troubles are
unavoidable. They are not half so import-
ant as the determination of whether there is
any considerable quantity of decent-grade ore
that can be got without costly picking.
* * # . *
Adelong Creek (N.S.W.), after scraping
along with a monthly yield of 90oz. from two
dredges, suddenly flashes the news that No. 1
plant alone has saved 130 oz. But No. 2
has done so poorly that it has been sent
scraping along the surface till it can dip into
better stuff ahead. While this change in for-
tune has befallen the Creek, Monty Cohen’s
Estates is dredging into poorer dirt. But
the Estates will give more rich patches yet.
* * * *
Cope’s Creek Central, Tingha (N.S.W.),
looks like having to pay a big war-time profits
tax, as since February it has shot out £9OOO
in divs., a sum almost equal to paid-up
capital. The co. was off the div. list from
1914 until a couple of months ago, so the
present run about evens up things. Of course
it is only rnacfe possible by the record price of
tin, so the war tax is fair enough, in spite
of some of the State Premiers.
* * * *
An interesting little game of cut-throat
is being played in western N. S. Wales.
Years ago, when George Blakemore was at
Great Cobar, he became interested in the
C.S.A. Great Cobar didn’t treat him well;
and he probably doesn’t feel that he owes
any debt of gratitude to the present co.
Since he left it the concern has suffered'
disaster after disaster, while the rejected and
despised C.S.A. has come further and fur-
ther into the limelight. Great Cobar has
fallen into the hands of the receiver; C.S.A.
has lately got a railway—built with
public money—and has started copper
production. The question is now which
is the dog and which the tail. In the
struggle to save something from the wreck
Cobar is promoting a movement to form a
co-operative co., on the lines of the Associ-
ated Smelters. It observes that that is
what the Broken Hill Prop, did when it
saw that the time was coming when it
wouldn’t have the ore to employ its furnaces.
But the Cobar district isn’t quite Broken
Hill, and the committee which has the job
in hand has a tough proposition. Meantime
C.S.A. stands out and nurses the 400,000
tons of ore which Blakemore told the Works
Committee there is in the mine of so low a
grade that “it would not pay them to get it
out and treat it. But,” he added, “if the line
were built they could get it out and send it
to Great Cobar, and the costs would so work
out that Great Cobar would get its flux for
nothing after the gold and silver had been
taken out of it.” This question of basic
ore supplies is of the utmost importance to
Great Cobar. Of course C.S.A. knows it,
and isn’t without hope that some day the
tail will w T ag the dog. Meantime, however,
while both sides dig in, the public in those
parts is likely to do a perish. The other
day Great Cobar applied for six months’
suspension on its Cobar Gold and Chesney
leases, which suggests that the before-men-
tioned perish has begun. Somebody ought
to have power to see that these lessees of
public property don’t use it as pawns in a
game. If the two properties must be worked
together for the public good and the benefit
of the public works which have been pro-
vided at great expense to serve them, then
both parties are likely to receive an uncom-
fortable jolt if they don’t promptly find a
way to pull together.
* * * *
A flutter is on in the Speck because John
Craze has got a couple of veins in the face
of the bottom tunnel of the Round Hill.
These have given samples of galena assaying
(55 p.e. lead, 91oz. silver and 4dwt. gold per
ton —welcome stuff, especially as Craze has
been stuck up with a big fault which appar-
ently affected the grade of the ore adversely.
His idea is that he may have reached the
downward continuation of the shoot that
carried rich galena in the upper level. But
the veins are so small that it isn’t worth
while making a fuss till a lot more develop-
ment has been done.
* * * *
Lyell (Tas.) is to distribute Is. 3d., which
means a morsel of £80,575 for shareholders.
Bulls have been bellowing for 25., but the co.
has to finance Mount Read and Rosebery till
a float or a loan is possible, and David Lums-
den isn’t the sort to take risks in war-time.
Union-secretary Little is issuing warnings to
miners not to go to Queenstown, because of
some mysterious trouble which is impending.
The story is that Sticht prefers the non-
unionist. If Little braves Bass Straits he will
find that all Sticht prays for is willing hands
of any sort to keep the two furnaces going
while copper is saleable at over £lOO.
* * * *
Broken Hill Block 14, after spending years
looking for another mine and finding only
poor imitations, met the unexpected at King
Island, Bass Strait. Of course the war
helped the wolfram proposition there; out
that doesn’t matter —except that the war
won’t last for ever. Meantime the King
Island Scheelite Co., with a capital of £30,000,
half of it representing mine purchase, is
showing a net profit of 60 p.c. p.a. Last
half-year it made £9444 net, and 2s. per share
(£10,000) has been paid in divs.-—the first.iu
December, the second last month. The mill,
handling 1.24 p.c. ore, treated 8264 tons for
tons concentrates assaying 71.7 p.c.
metal valued at £17,890 = £2 3s. 3d. per ton
crudes at a mine cost (including develop-
ment) of 15s. 0.84 d. The extraction is only
68.12 p.c. of contents; but by re-treating
concentrates manager Venn Blown expects
to add 6s. or 7s. per ton net to the recovery.
Additions to plant to do that and also raise
the duty from 500 tons to 750 tons per day
are in hand. The cost will be £lO,OOO,
which will steady divs. for the rest of the
year. But it is worth while; for there will
be at least a good market for wolfram long
after the war, the world’s available supply
at a moderate price being very small, and
this co. has at least 78,000 tons of ore of a
shade better than 1 p.c. almost ready for
handling. On March 31 —that was before
the second div. was paid—the co. had, besides
stores, about £10,500 net of liquid assets.
* * * *
A big effort is being made to get the
Deborah claim, lying between the Great
Southern and Lansell’s Red, White and Blue,
at work. McColl, Rankin. Stanistreet and
Cundy are the sponsors. They reckon that
if 42,000 shares of Is. 3d. each go off they
can give the ground a decent trial. The at-
, traction is that east and west in the belt in
which the Deborah finds a home there are
mines that have shown up among the best
in Bendigo. Cundy is a believer in the zone
theory of gold deposition. As this block of
1000 yards of centre-country reef-channel has
never yet been probed, he submits it with an
unctuous benediction suited to the times.
* * * *
Golden Fleece, Bendigo, has belted its way
for two years into the reefs associated with
the Fryingpan back, and now talks about
breaking the record of divs. won in 2dwt.
ground. Most of the time was spent on the
Comet run of stone, with a bit of side-step-
ping now and again on to the reef east of
the big Stafford fault plane. All this work
only went to prove much quartz and no gold,
so the co. is back again on to the Fryingpan
make to pick up the shoot of gold that went
underfoot at the levels above 730 ft. The
scramble for divs. on 2dwt. dirt would have
a better chance if Golden Fleece had a big
battery instead of its present power.
Extract from the half-yearly report of
Edna May Consolidated Extended : —•
Very hard cemented over-burden has been passed
through, and it is probable the celebrated gneissic
channel will be intercepted very shortly.
Well, what is going to happen then? So
far nothing has been seen in the Extended to
justify it being called a mine, and as far as
a Bulletin man who saw the show re-
cently could tell, there isn’t anything to
build on in the future. ,

Last year S. Australia dug up 7213 tons of
copper valued at £902,475. This was 00
tons less than in 1916, but it was worth
£BO,OOO more. The production of iron—all
at the B. H. Prop.’s quarries—increased to
328,386 tons, worth £359,723. The grade is
over 60 p.c., and millions of tons are avail-
able. Gold production amounted to only
71450 z., and silver-lead was valued at
£12,018. There was a serious falling off in
the output of salt —46,858 tons, compared
with 66,400 tons for the previous year.
