Sydney, May 13, 191 S.
My Dear Arinia—
When the Premiers came to Sydney to
talk over the sins of the Commonwealth
they brought their wives and as much bag-
gage as the Censor for War-time Propriety
among Politicians would pass without a
frown. For the week-end there was a whirl
ia joyous autumn weather to Jenolan ; and
this week the Governor’s lady devotes herself
to personally-conducted tours to adjacent
sights. Jaunts to National Park. Newport
and other breezy spots crowd' the ladies’
itinerary.
The opening of flip Conference was a pon-
derous affair in the Leg. Council Chamber,
wherein the marble noses of long-dead legis-
lators point eternally towards the chair.
Premier Holman, having been chosen presi-
dent, took the throne. Governor Davidson,
with Aides Saltmarshe and Stanhara, wear-
ing that expression of dignified martyrdom
which young Englishmen bring to such occa-
sions, had official sec. Budge to cheer the
proceedings. Governor Davidson’s lady sat
near by. Mrs. Holman came in late —she’d
been opening something—and trod on the
skirts of Premier Lawson’s speech. West-
ralian Lefroy was the only politician with a
pair of gloves, and he carried them gingerly
iu one hand. When Theodore, Queensland’s
Treasurer - , got up, there was a rustle
amongst the millinery present; but nothing
happened, and he didn’t have to be put in
irons.
The beauty of the decorations at the Pre-
miers’ feast at the Australia matched the
brilliance of W. A. Holman’s oratory. A
huge map of Australia hung over the centre
table, and about 70 knives and forks were
laid in a garden of autumn leaves and
exquisite crimson blossoms.
Premier Lee, of the Apple Isle, was
trained for the Methody Church, but changed
his mind before he turned his collar. Ilis
remarks retain a pulpit flavor, and there is
a solemnity about his long frock-coat —a
garment of unusual cut in a sac-suited Con-
ference.
The broad-shouldered Tevita Tui Vakano,«
Premier of Tonga, went to look at the con-
ference of the States’ Premiers. He has
himself been five years at the helm. With a
British consul as adviser, the tiny kingdom
manages its own affairs. Vakano, whose
mahogany face beams with benevolence (the
Tui means “noble”), had some education in
Auckland, though he must still handle Eng-
lish with care. He brought his wife to see
the shops—her first trip out of the land of
the hibiscus.
In his own kingdom Willie Holman lias
been First Lord since June, 1913, which
makes the two Premiers almost twins. Natu-
rally, therefore, a call on Ills brother was one
of the Tongan’s first pleasures when he
reached Sydney. You’ve got to imagine that
meeting. The visitor is at last seated. To
him what happens in Tonga is more import-
ant than anything that can happen any-
where else in the world. So he regrets to
inform his distinguished brother of the demise
of the great and never-to-be-forgotten King
George the Second. To which Brother
William replies that the throat of Australia
filled with emotion and its heart beat in
sorrowing sympathy for a smitten people
when the sad news went through the Com-
monwealth that the most distinguished
George the Second of Tonga had gone to his
eternal reward.
Probably Holman thought that that finished
it. But you and I may be quite sure .it
hadn’t. Tonga by the mouth of its Premier
next would have the great honor to announce
the accession of the beloved Charlotte
Sarlotta, if you please —to the Throne.
Whereupon it would become the bounden
duty of Australia by the mouth of its Ma
State Premier to say that to its profound
sorrow for the death of the distinguished
George the Commonwealth added the liveliest
appreciation of the virtues of the altogether
desirable Sarlotta. And then let s assume
that they bowed seven times each, the Pre-
mier of Tonga gingerly walking down the
steps and bemoaning an unhappy fate that
compels him to wear boots, while his brother
returned to the Treasurer’s old job of finding
another half-crown.
In Sydney there is a rapidly-growing
public opinion which intends to eliminate
hideous hoarding advts. and substitute Art.
To hasten the glad day the Minister in
charge of Local Government, J. D.
Fitzgerald, opened an exhibition of English
and American posters collected by Charles
Lloyd Jones, now o» this side, and hinted at
trouble to come. The trouble will be served
out by new legislation which will deal with
advertisers who flaunt their wares in un-
seemly fashion. Meanwhile there are, of
course, many Australian artists who are
doing attractive advt. work, and many manu-
facturers who turn plain boiled trade into
French meringue. War Chest angels, stand. at
the door of the exhibition collecting six-
pences, while others serve tea for the cause.
Surrounded by the posters, Wilfred John-
ston, a Comforts Fund Commissioner just
back, shook out the tattered remnants of an
Australian Comforts flag. It had been shot
off its base, so to speak, right up behind the
firing-line in France.
During bleak February, in Britain, Lieut.
G. Lees, of the Australian Flying Corps,
tucked the hand of Doris Russell .Tones
under his arm, after giving the lady a plain
gold ring. .She belongs to an old Sydney
legal family ; and he’s a Sydney boy.
There’s news of the engagement of David,
junior,—the Geology Professor’s medical son.
An English girl is the chosen.
The drawing of War Loan prizes in the
Hordern-Fariuer lottery scheme was as ex-
citing ns a church collection. The Town
Hall, so far from seething with citizens who
expected the £lOOO, wasn’t one-*bird full.
On the platform a huge blackboard gave the
lucky numbers that would send the winners
home in a taxi. But the cheerful pessimist
—a more frequent citizen than is generally
supposed—screwed up his eyes to read the
figures with a faith in his capacity for miss-
ing. The chief diversion was Mr. Speaker
Cohen’s game of marbles. Inadvertently the
little barrel was swung round without hav-
ing its front door closed, and out rolled the
numbered beads; whereupon the elect took to
their knees and groped under chairs to find
the agates. Norman Pope represented
Farmer’s; manager Harris was there for
Anthony Horderns’. The platform had its
photo, taken three times, but not once when
it was at its devotions.
Word has come through that Godfrey Lud-
low. the violinist son of a Sydney doctor, has
at last secured his release from internment
at Ruhleben, where he had been since the
outbreak of the war. He will go to Sweden,
where Leopold Auer has domiciled himself,
and will resume his studies under the famous
violin pedagogue.
There are not
many Australian
women who choose
the organ as their
pet. In Sydney.
Lilian Frost- —a Tas-
manian—is the best
known. But young
Australia is begin-
ning to put her fin-
ger on the stops.
Amy Chudleigli, _ a
pupil of Lilian
Frost, is, though a
mere slip of a girl,
assistant organist at
the Pitt-street Con-
gregational taber-
nacle ; and next
Sunday afternoon
she will probably
play on the Town Hall giant. Last year
the bright Sydney girl went to TJ.S.A. with
father. Australians don’t understand the
ways of Sammy-land till they get there. It
was summer. All the leading music teachers
and professors were having their three
months’ annual holiday. Besides this
breather, there’s a week at Christmas and
another at Easter —the rest of the year
solid work. “As I meant to take lessons
from a famous New York organist, it was
a disappointment to find he was just leaving
town for 12 weeks. All the teachers of any
importance were also away. So the best I
could do was to listen to some great organ-
ists giving recitals.” Amy chats of Lemaire,
once here on a visit, and just then (May,
1917) appointed city organist in ’Frisco.
The Auditorium there holds 10,000 people.
“Echo organs are placed in the walls, and
worked bv electricity from the big organ—a
wonderful effect!” Ocean Grove, a seaside
resort in New Jersey, claims the biggest
organ in the world; and there are other
U.S. places where half-hour recitals are part
of the day’s routine.
Canada had no w’hite bread on the table
when the Chudleighs came home that way.
It had its meatless and baconless days, and
no one worried. One has to go _ away to
know what a fortunate place this is at pre-
sent. Juditlt Fletcher supplies the photo.
Miss Badham, head of the C. of E. Girls’
Grammar, is back to her usual form after
her misadventure with appendicitis. So
there was a bazaar by day and theatricals
bv night in the roomy old mansion in Forbes-
street. Ostensibly it was to help war and
mission funds; but the party turned it into
a joyous welcome-home to the learned prin-
cipal'. Miss Badham is too big mentally to
have catty ways, so the Boy, if not en-
couraged, is at least tolerated at these school
sprees. Generally he is Grammar or King’s,
and gives a piquancy to muslin frocks and
butterfly bows.
Major Plant, D. 5.0., of Brisbane, was
recently wed in London. He chose a Queens-
land girl—Oona Brown.
Sydney Public Li-
brary is the homely
parent put in the
shade by a fashion-
able daughter. Even
the very ordinary
citizen can chat
fluently about the
Mitchell Library, the
dressy offspring of
the brown old mother
over the way in Mae-
quarie-street. Its
old-Australian flavor,
its rare pictures of
Sydney and its hand-
some cover all arrest
attention. Yet the
Public Library, in
spite of its air of
•stodge and respecta-
bility, can provide reading matter as sensa-
tional as any American picture-film—for
those who need that stimulus. Behind its
swinging doors thousands of books and news-
papers (these date back to early days) and
countless magazines await the public
pleasure. They are yours to read for the
mere asking. Books to the number of about
300,000, ranging from the fluff of modern
fiction to serious research works; pamphlets
on boiler-making and recipes for orange mar-
malade —they and their arrangement and
their cataloguing are mute witnesses to the
task of a highly-trained librarian.
Here is his photo. But if you ask W. 11.
Ifculd —Sydney stole him from Adelaide
about six years ago—exactly what a librarian
has to know, he ducks his head. “Possibly,”
says he, “I have the best staff, for their own
special work, of any man in this city. Thirty
of them—the majority therefore —are women.
Many are graduates. All are well-educated
and keen.” But the building is eminently
unsuited for its business. Some day it will
be replaced by a modern temple of which
the Mitchell Library will form a wing.
Meantime, light has taken the place of gloom,
burnished brass and shining window-panes
replace the grime of years ago. For relief
from all this the ardent chief goes home to
his almost equally beloved flowers, and on
the way dreams perhaps of the old age he’ll
spend in his orchard on the Murray.
With Dr. Stoiie Dixson in command, the
St. John Ambulance workers tramped on
the grass near Government House one day
last week. The Bonnets swept past, and
the ambulance brigade went through an
heroic display of their useful habits as
stretcher-bearers. Raridwick division has
raised the wind and produced a car. Gov-
ernor Davidson, who was ticketed as a
Knight of Grace of the Order of St. John,
gave his blessing to the new chaise.
Some time ago a little agitation was started
by Arthur Streeton and a few of the older
Australian artists in England, who thought
that the work of making on-the-spot war-
records in France should not be left entirely
to the younger men. Streeton had a special
grievance because, in spite of his years, he
lias been doing a lot of war service in khaki.
Now news comes through that Streeton,
John Longstaff and Fullwood have been
sent to France to do their bit of canvassing
under fire.
The guests at Taronga Park Zoo had
courtesy calls from the Premiers and their
ladies on Friday afternoon. A Government
launch whisked the party over to Athol,
where they were met by the three white
bears —always the first to hold out a paw
to the visitor from over the water.
Re-married a week or two ago, Mrs.
Ivirchener, recently of Killara, whose late
husband was a cousin of the French artist
Paul Ivirchener. The lady, who is slim and
charming, changed her name to Currie. lie
grows mutton in Maoriland.
Look here, Arini. mothers are patient; but
even their supply of the goods isn’t inexhaust-
ible. They pick up the paper in the morning
and see column upon column of—well,
for instance, this morning there is the simply
staggering information that >Mr. Andrew
Fisher had lunch one day last week with
Messrs. Smith and Jones —or two people
equally unknown. And Australian mothers,
who are simply aching for news of their boys
after the awful fivhting of the past six weeks,
go to bed sick with anxiety—will word never
come? Presently the message arrives—seven
weeks after it has been lodged. Cables
delivered this morning were lodged in Eng-
land on March 27 —six weeks and four days
ago. Such messages are far worse than
nothing; they simply give mothers a feel-
ing of utter helplessness; and if that is good
for recruiting, prussic acid is good for cats.
Meantime, however, they have the joy of
knowing that their cables have been held up
in order that we may know that Mr. Smith
and Mr. Jones had a drink one day last week
with Mr. Fisher.
The new Scotchbyterian Moderator, David
Smith, M.A., is a tall, kindly Scot long resi-
dent in the Mamma State. Soon after com-
ing this way he read the Wurrud in Chal-
mers’ Church, and married a child of
the manse—Margaret, eldest daughter of the
late Dr. Kinross, at that time head of St.
Andrew’s.
Another of the nice-looking Yates sisters
of Bondi has gone to the altar. Muriel was
quietly annexed by Lieut. Mae Donnell in
St. Mary’s, Waverley,. the other afternoon.
Her sister, who married an A.I.F. man.
Lieut. Pattrick, a year or so ago, was Matron
of Honor.
Lister Lister’s oils and water-colors are
hanging in Anthony Horderns’ art-gallery.
They reflect shadow and shine on the face of
the waters —South Coast, Middle Harbor
and Mosman's are in the catalogue—and
blue mists on mountain tops. Lister’s old
friend the white gum-tree is also present.
Howard Hadley, a fine pianist here for
a while, was at the second piano in portion
of Jessie Masson’s brilliant performance last
week in the King’s Hall. This recital
showed the Victorian girl as one of _ the
most forceful of Australian women pianists,
with a clean and clever technique. There’s
a flash in the lady’s black eyes which hints
at “temperament”—a useful thing, if the lid
doesn’t fly off, on a concert platform.
Nurse Hay, matron of Clarmore, returned
this morning after two and a. half years
fnriously-busy war-work, first in Egypt and
then in' France. It was a tearful home-
coming. On touching Australia she had
word that her father was dying at Cremorne.
After that she felt like getting out and push-
ing the tardy troopship. But even that
wouldn’t have got it here in time. Mr. Hay-
died on Tuesday.
Tickets for “Cupid Camouflaged,” the ama-
teur film-play which is guaranteed to contain
about 200 people, may be had from Mrs.
Spencer Brunton, Gladswood, Double Bay.
Don’t blink at the prices—a guinea, 10s. 6d.
and 5s. —for the lot goes to the Red Cross.
The J.C.W. firm and Goldwyn Pictures,

May 81. The Alex. McCormick lady is

lion, sec., with useful help from Mrs. Ander-
son, who used to be Gladys Gould and surges
with energy.
Writing from Kobe, Japan, Gwen Fussell,
a North Sydney girl, sends word of her
sister, the wife of the ex-Sydney man, Com-
modore Domerchikoff, the Russian naval
officer of the marvellous adventures: —
She longs, of course, for Australia, as Russia is
an uncertain volcano for home-building. “Dom” is
on the Admiralty staff, but has never recovered the
effects of long imrhersion in icy waters when his
ship was torpedoed.... They want me, when travel-
ling is possible, to go to Petrograd to them; but ——
Here in Kobe we had the whitest Christmas —so
white that the hills couldn't shoulder back the snow.
Travellers come this way with thrilling tales of the
Bolsheviks, but we take some of them with a pound
of salt. Here is one. The mob dashed through a big,
crowded hotel, slaughtering people on sight, and
looted everything. “And what was everybody doing ?”
I asked. “Did no one in the crowd attempt to defend
himself?” “Er,” said this eye-witness, “I don’t
know; I left the day before!”
Tank headgear for women hasn’t reached
Sydney yet. Possibly the next War Loan
campaign will produce girls with their locks
in Victory hats. The millinery, which lias
already appeared in U.S.A., is fashioned like
the big grey hulk that lurched round our city
a few weeks back. It’s tied under the chin
and carries an electric torchlight in front.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Melbourne, May 13, 1018.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Saturday saw the Melburnians (Church of
England Grammar) Head of the River. The
climax turned the Dark Blues on the bank
into dancing dervishes. The Pale Blues
(Geelong), who were Heads last year, were
glum but good-natured about it.
All the week crowds and motor parties
smothered in school colors have been strangling
the traffic from the Block to the Bridge. It
has been the week of the schoolboy and school-
girl—and of the Old Boy and the Old Girl,
too. In May Old Boys migrate to Melbourne
from the ends of Australia. Many are
wearing returned colors as well as their
school rainbow. For the time being mothers
and fathers have taken a back seat, and par-
ties are run by the children. Dances, din-
ners, house-parties and theatre-frivols have
made some swanky young sassiety matrons
feel years older.
Mrs. Leslie Stuart’s daughters (not yet in
their teens) gave a dinner at Menzies’ on
Saturday, and a theatre-party in Her
Majesty’s stalls. Edmund Jo we tt. had a big
theatre-party. The Billy Williamses were
the guests of their brood. The Stewart
Bruntons were hustled in a most undignified
manner by their party of excited flappers.
Bowes Kelly looked used to it; he is always
taking his lot about. Mrs. Dan Taylor’s girls
were theatre hostesses. And young squatto-
crat Campbell, of the winning Melburnian
crew, gave a dinner at Scott’s and the inevit-
able theatre-party.
There was a fealifully swanky Red Cross
party at the Mia Mia last Thursday. Sas-
siety “took” tea tables and filled them with
guests—Edward Miller, Prof. Baldwin
Spencer’s wife, Mrs. Geoff Syme, Miss Mait-
land McMicking, Mrs. Courtney Dix, the
rich Dalrymples, the Douglas Menzies and
others were among the hosts. Every
niche was occupied. There were two musical
programmes, one upstairs and the other
down. Downstairs had a start of half an
hour; then upstairs wailed its way into the
musical bedlam. There was an auction, of
course, and singing and dancing. Polly
Hurry painted some special programmes
with Australian bush scenes. Mrs. Gilruth
was a guest, and Mrs. Brennan, from
Bananaland. Scobie MacKinnons, Landales,
Brodribbs and Morrisons all came to tea
and shed money freely; and Miss Eadith
Walker from Sydney thinned out her fat
cheque-book a weeny bit. Captain Clifford
helped some of the professional sassiety
flower-sellers, and a batch of joyous naval
johnnies were handy-men.
The O.H.M.S. Fete is being organised by
every section of the Commonwealth Public
Service to help the Red Cross and Returned
Soldiers’ Funds. It happens in the Exhibi-
tion on May 18 and 20 (Saturday and Mon-
day). Governor Stanley will open it; and
there will be raffles and stalls and an autho-
rised post office for the despatch of parcels
to soldiers overseas. The Fete originally in-
tended squeezing into the Town Hall, but
grew to such proportions that only the Exhi-
bition will hold it.
Camperdown, the sheepiest of Western
District centres, has been giving a sniff qf its
woolly glories to the G.-G. and his lady.
Donald Mackinnon, the recruiter, made one
of the motor-party, which took a sandwich
at Booran, the Mackinnon family ranch.
Later, the Stewart Blacks, at Glenormiston,
handed round a cup of tea; and at the Cam-
perdown H.Q. of the Red Cross, with Mrs.
Edward Manifold offering the glad hand on
the doorstep, the Sisterhood put its best sock
forward amid deep sighs of complete happi-
ness.

and their two daughters have come over

Lady Helen. Baby-daughter Susan furled
her fair hair to join the party as a grown-
up. She has been enduring numerous opera-
tions on her foot, and is limping along the
road to recovery.
Captain Booth, the new genthelp to the
Tasmanian Newdegates, has come over with
the family party. He is a youngster of
about 25 who has to get round with a stick,
having been put out of action in France.
The Newdegates are nervous about him —
they have had such a storm of matrimonial
engagements among their aides.
As it is the personal desire of his Ex. and
Lady Helen, Melba will probably return

ish Empire.

Ilolford Wettefihall, of Glen Holford, who in
forgotten years used to sit in the Leg. Coun-

The late Alexander Dennis, who used to own

ran has learnt that her son, Major F.E.,

He was ( a Hamilton College and Melbourne
’Varsity' boy, who went to help settle the

returned to see the finish, and th(m remained

Remember Ruby Moore, poor little Sumner
Locke’s pal, whose taste for amateur theatri-
cals and skill in teaching kiddies folk-dancing
brought her into notice? Like Summie, she
married a soldier after a trip to America and
England, and her hubby, Lieut. D. F. G.

While at Singapore lately the manager
of the Felton-Grimwade business in Mel-
bourne was struck by the fine appearance of
a crowd of Serbian officers who had travelled
from Roumania through Russia, and had
shipped at Vladivostock. Some Serbian
rank and file were less impressive, but the
officers in their new uniforms made a fine
martial show. They were booked for Salonika
—a journey of about 18.000 miles to go a
few hundred as the crow flies.
Strong-man Clarence Weber, of Brighton,
lost his wife after a sudden illness last week.
There is a set of six young Webers to
mitigate the pain of the tragic departure.
Mrs. Bupert Mason Cox, who has been
appealing in Sydney for money from the
estate of her late father, Matthew Harris,
married one of Melbourne’s best-dressed men.
Bupert belonged to Melbourne and the Block
before he went to Sydney, and Melbourne
cannot believe anything bad of him. It knew
him as a busy young man who went soaring
through accountancy exams, and always
came out on top. His spare time was spent
in the fierce light that beats upon the Block
and the best ballroom floors; and his socks
and' ties were ever the wonder of parading
damsels.

But tinder the new rule how can he do it unless he

The casualty lists are again hard upon us.
Amongst too many others, Major Priestley and Lieut.
Farmer have been killed in Palestine, and the John
Tassies’ son in France. Lieut. Farmer left for the
Front in January, 1915, and he had been fighting
almost continuously ever since. In 1916 he and
Ruby Adams were married at Port Said. The bride
went on to London, where she got work at the War
Office, but returned home in February last.
Alma Baker, who is appealing for battleplanes, has
received £2703, the cost of one properly-equipped
’bus, from Mrs. Syd Kidman. Her husband gave
the first Australian battleplane, in August, 1916.

action in November last, has come to life again as a
prisoner of war in Germany, where he is in the same
camp as Lieut. Whitington, another S. Australian
who was officially reported killed before he had time
to die.

From a girl in the U.S.A.
Melba has been singing to the Sammies. Her
first concert was at Camp Lewis, where over 40,000
of the good Californian type are training. This,
camp is really wonderful from the organisation point
of view. Nothing that will make for the comfort of
the men has been overlooked, and a, huge Liberty
Theatre is part of the scheme. This night it w r as
packed with khaki, and stars and stripes were all
over the place. When the singing was over Melba
came out and, unfurling an American flag, asked
the boys to give three cheers for the Allies. They
obliged and added three for Melba. Her second
khaki concert was at Camp Kierhey, in California.
She also opened the new theatre on this spot, but
she sang in the open in the midst of a sea of khaki.
The boys refused to let her stop at the end of a
large programme, so she gave them “Annie Laurie”
as a wind-up.
News has come to U.S.A. from Australia that the
Lady Susan Fitzclarence is going to face the footlights
professionally. There is no truth in the report.
She has not any intention of doing more than
she did in Melbourne, when she acted Lady Teazle
with Cyril Maude. She is quite content to remain a
good amateur. At present she is touring America
with Melba, and when that lady settles do,wn for the
summer months in the house she has taken in Cali-
fornia the Lady Susan will still be her guest.
Ida Fink and her mother are nowadays located at
Los Angeles, California, where the movie studios are.
The local papers somehow or other got the idea that
Ida was the Rahnee, and big headlines stood out
above sensational stories which told of the youthful
marriage of the young Australian to the, Indian
prince. But she could not suppress her longing to
be a movie star, and eventually left “the court” for
Los Angeles. With such publicity for a beginning it
looks as if Miss Fink should go well in the movie
world.
Stella Power has returned with an impos-
ing collection of Yankee clothes and a view-
point to match. She sang at concerts given
by Melba at Philadelphia and Brooklyn, and
received some beautiful paper for other
warbles. Musical affairs in the States are
at a standstill, but Stella hopes to return
there for the next season. Meanwhile she is
booking Australian engagements. Melba will
be in Australia in August (submarines per-
mitting), but Freda Sternberg, her secretary,
will stay on to try her luck with her pen. On
arriving at Spencer-street Stella rushed for-
ward to hug a young man in khaki, and the
waiting crowd at once smelt romance. But
the kissee was only her brother, Lieut. Cyril
Power, who has just been invalided home.
Mrs. Ernest Scott (Emily Dyason) has
already given two concerts for the Red
Cross, each a great success financially and
musically. She is now preparing another,
which is to be held at the Melba Hall on
May 29. Nora Kyffin Thomas, the violinist
from Adelaide, and soprano Clarice Malyon
will help. Tickets, four shillings.
As we didn’t have a mechanically-minded
Government to give it a lift, it has
taken a long time for wool-spinning
to catch on in Melbourne. Long ago,
when knitting-wool was scarce and
dear, Miss Gordon, of Ballangeieh, dug
up a spinning-wheel and had several others
built from the model. This year, on Founda-
tion Day, Melbourne had an exhibition of
spinning-results. at which Mrs. John
McKellar of Ballangeieh and Mrs. John
Cook of Ellerslie both won first prizes. The
interest shown in that display has now’ begun
to bear fruit; of late weeks' there has been
quite a boom in the production of home-made
knitting-wool and a new voluntary-spinner
is coming forward every little while. A
bike-wheel, by the way, is the chief feature
or’ the Ballangeieh lAaehine, and a pair of
carders go with each, though some fleeces
don’t need carding.
Guy B. Post may be an actor, but he isn’t
an auctioneer. In an interval of “The
Masquerader” on Friday he tried to coax a
packed boatrace-night audience to bid for
one of the stage boxes at the forthcoming
Bed Cross matinee. The affair was
wretchedly stage-managed. Nobody had
made arrangements for a few bookmakers or
other affluent citizens to start the bidding :
the subdued lighting of the house amounted
practically to gloom, and the auctioneer
murmured his remarks in the tone of a ner-
vous young curate offering thin bread and
butter. I lis call for tenders was received
with deafening silence. After a while Guy
relieved the embarrassment by himself mak-
ing a bid of £25. Then followed some more
gentle pleading, to which there was no re-
sponse. The actor shrugged his shoulders,
washed his hands of the matter and fled.
Whereupon arose a buzz of nervous conver-
sation such as is usually employed to
smother a faux pas.
Artist Dorothy Meason Coates is busy in
London on kitchen clinics —educational in-
stitutions which show home cooks how to get
a roast-beef effect out of a handful of nuts
and do other conjuring tricks. But she has
been sparing time from the kitchen to get a
painting or so hung in conspicuous places
at recent exhibitions.
Tours sincerely.

THE MACNAH.
By Sir H. Habbotn, R.A»
THE ORIGINAL PICTURE THE PROPERTY .OF JOHN .DEWAR BLSONS LTtt
“THE MACNAB.”
24,200 GUINEAS FOR A RAEBURN.
When Raeburn painted this mighty
portrait of “The Macnab” he succeeded
once and for all in making a canvas blaze
with the unquenchable spirit of the High-
lander in all its dour ferocity. Resplen-
dent in the uniform of the eighteenth cen-
tury national service of the Breadalbane
Fencibles, the twelfth (and last) lineal
laird of Macnab was seen by Raeburn
as he grimly knew himself to be, and as
the proud and indomitable Highlander
would always be. And well does the
enemy know it to-day. The spirit of
The Macnab is still existent, and in the
fields of Flanders there have been done
deeds by Highlanders worthy of the tra-
ditions of the race. There is gtill a
kind of Homeric legend in Scotland about
The Macnab, and it was in full song
shortly after the death of the Chieftain
who lies buried near the grave of Fingal.
In the rare “Voyage d’un Francais” a
French visitor in 1816 bore witness to
this Scottish “heros d’Homhre” who left
a posterity of “trente k quarante Mac-
nabs.” Lockhart in his “Life” of Sir
Walter Scott states that “this singular
personage spent his life almost entirely
in a district where a boat was the usual
conveyance.” Sir Thomas Lawrence
declared the Raeburn “Macnab” to be
the finest portrait of a man within his
ken.
The Macnabs are a very old clan,
and in the Gaelic they are called
“The Children of the Abbot,” tracing
their descent to the Abbots of Glen-
dochart. In the reign of Alexander III.
they were acknowledged as a powerful
and influential family. Their possessions
were situated on the side of Loch Tay
and stretched along the course of the
Dochart to the head of Strathfillan.
Francis, the twelfth chieftain of the clan,
died in May, 1816, and was succeeded by
his nephew, who, however, was compelled
to sell his his estates and, with some
hundreds of his claasfolk, emigrated to
Canada.
In this notable painting Raeburn de-
picts “The Macnab” in the flamboyant
uniform of his regiment, consisting of a
green jacket with silver braid, red tar-
tan vest, kilt and plaid, badger-head
sporran, tartan stockings and black shoes ;
armed with a dirk, broadsword and pisfbl;
a bonnet of tall black and white feathers
on • his head. He is shown standing in
a mountain pass holding a pistol in his
right hand. The picture was exhibited
at the Royal Academy in 1819 and has
since been on view at various Loan Ex-
hibitions, notably in Rome in 1911, while
reproductions have made it familiar to the
general public.
Last year the painting, which had
formed part of the collection of the
Marquis of Breadalbane, was offered at
auction at Christie’s (London), and, after
spirited competition, was purchased by
Sir Thomas Dewar for 24,200 guineas,
exceeding the highest price previously
paid for a Raeburn.
Looking at the painting one can appre-
ciate the truth of Scott’s lines :—
_. _ Come one, come all! This rock shall fly
From its firm base as soon as I.

ABORIGINALITIES
“Eddyson”: This is the story of Dollman’s
sacrifice, as told by ’Enry the braceman. Dollman
bad drifted into the bar of Kate’s hotel at Swingle-
tree, taken his medium beer and drifted out again.
He should have been a tallish man, but was doubled
almost into a hunchback. “Rheumatism ?” I asked.
Enry grinned. “So,” said lie. “Ain’t yeh heard
how Dollman got that? I’ll have to tell yeh. It is
a great ole joke. But don’t mention it to Dollman
or he’ll fling a spade at yeh. It happened when the
ole Grecian Bend was workin’ ’cross the common.
All round here’s rotten ground, but the Bend shaft
was plumb in the centre of the rottenest patch. You
never knew what the reef was up to there. It done
everythin’ but talk, ’n’ ’twas like the Devil had
set it t’ get ez many men ez possible afore the lead
give out. One night there was a fall. in the T drive
where Dollman ’n’ Errickson was workin’. It got
the pair of ’em, but just on the edge of the drop,
both pinned more or less with broken timber ’n’
drift. Errickson was caught under a big log that
come right across the drive. Dollman saw how ’twas
with the ’elp of the few matches he had, ’n' found
that, though he couldn't free his own leg, lie was
able, by gettin’ a twist on himself, to get his body
under the log V hoist it enough t’ take the dead
weight off Errickson, ’n’ give him a charnce iv seein'
it out till the rescue party burrowed through. Clawin’
at the slabs on his side of the drive, ’n’ makin’ his
own back a stop for the log, Dollman held things
that way for 11 hours. ’N’ that’s how the fellers
found him when they broke through. The nails was
torn from his fingers, his eyes was startin’ from his
head, ’n’ his spine got that kink in it, but—’n’ here
comes the joke —Errickson idasn’t there! He'd
crawled out!”

“Tropicus”: The convolvulus that fes-
toons New Guinea’s beaches with its bell-shaped blue
and heliotrope flowers is an honored member of brown
brother’s pharmacopoeia. A decoction of the young-
buds and flowers is used by the dusky belles in coun-
tering the accidents of hasty affection, and a poultice
of the young leaves and tendrils, beaten into a green-
ish mass and applied to a “New Guinea sore,” works
wonders. I have used it myself, and can vouch for
its cleansing and healing properties. The sma?lied-up
leaves applied to bung eyes caused by insect bites act
like magic in reducing inflammation and swelling.
As a blood tonic it is extensively used, while an
extract of the leaves and flowers, boiled until the
water becomes the color of weak tea, is the native
cure for venereal disease. I have heard several
traders and bffehe-de-mer hunters say they have used
the extract successfully in treating dysentery, but
although the plant is a strong astringent I would
not ree -mmend its use in this connection without
ful l her advice.

“Bad Australian”: Even in this, ungodly
land (Argentina) they had sense enough to see the
possibilities of the ants’ mound, and after many
generations the custom has become so ingrained in
the people of the small pueblos that they build their
new brick homos (ovens) to exactly the same plan,
section and elevation as the white-ant nest. The out-
back South American builds his homo around and over
a circular pile of closely-packed reeds or pampa
grass, and when the brickwork sets he puts a match
to the stuffing and burns the innards out. Then he
lets the “poisoner” loose.
“Murua”: The little-known Trobriand
Group are a cluster of palm-clothed gems that stud
the emerald Pacific a hundred-odd miles north-east
of Samarai (Papua). They are the real thing in coral
romance. The natives are of the Melanesian type,
happy people with a fondness (especially on the
part of the star-eyed maidens) for Europeans, who
have exploited the rich pearl fishery. They are
advanced in arts and crafts, and produce a tremen-
dous quantity of carved weapons and ornaments,
which are eagerly bought by tourists from curio-
dealers in Sydney and Melbourne. Before competi-
tion set in the islanders did not get much for their
pearls. Stones worth £lO and £l5 were freely
bought for as many pence. The group is flat, but is
richly clothed in jungle, and produces vast quantities
of excellent yams, which are traded. Good climate,
gorgeous scenery, blue seas like billowing silk, dia-
mond-dusted beaches and splendid fishing and shoot-
ing! Add that the cost of living is negligible, and
what more do you want ?
“Ibis”: Re “W.M.S.” (B. 28/3/T8), re-
garding the policeman-fly. The wasps that he men-
tions are not by any means the only species Aus-
tralia possesses, and some of them for showy coloring
can rival any insect. The two species of Scolia
flower-wasp, for instance, are of a magnificent metal-
lic blue, the smaller (about jin. long) with a head-
light of bright yellow. Their food is pollen and
honey. Another species, the showiest of the lot.
wears a coat of metallic verdigris green, amazingly
brilliant—probably the only member of the insect
world to sport this color. (Its only parallel in the
flower world is the green Ixia.) This gentleman is
too much of a dandy to work, so he is found as a
parasite in bees’ nests. A third species has a black
head and thorax, dusky orange abdomen and irides-
cent purple wings; while another, of the mud-cell
building variety, is black banded with silver. By
the way, the old nursery rhyme immortalising the
wasp’s viciousness seems a libel. I have never known
of anyone being stung, though in our district (Lee-
ton, N.S.W.) we have great numbers of the black
and gold species. I have seen my pup making a toy
of one of these fellows without the yelps we expected.
“Bar goo” : Few have seen the porcupine
in its own family, circle, as the mother gives birth to
the children (three to five at a time) in some very
secluded spot. It is a delightful sight to a nature
lover to watch the parent pore, poke her nose out of
the home front door, scrutinise the vicinity for pro-
bable danger, and come forth with her babies ami
lead them away on a feeding expedition to the
nearest red- or meat-ants’ nest, the young follow-
ing in Indian file. The youngsters’ quills at this
stage, about four weeks after birth, are just begin-
ning to stiffen. The young are suckled as with the
pig, and if pickled for a week, even when they’re old,
their flesh cannot possibly be distinguished from
pickled pork. Immediately the family arrives at an
ant-nest, mother roots it up well to liven the dinner ;
then the party will quietly poke out their tongues, of
which there is an enormous development, and these
will at once become thickly covered with insects. As
a tunneller the porcupine stands alone. He bores
in a few minutes through the largest white-ant heap.
On an old cattle-yard I captured one of the family,
and, intending to cook him for breakfast, I dropped
him down a hollow corner-post and put a weight on
top. That’s the last I saw of him. He dug to the
surface 12ft. away from the post, and did the job in
a night.
“Te Whare”: The wild-birds when mating
have each their own peculiar methods of courtship.
For tenderness I select the dusky wood-swallow (bee-
bird), which snuggles up close to the lady of his
choice after bringing her grubs and other succulent
insects. On the other hand, some of the parrot tribe
make love much after the stone-age fashion, when
the caveman simply clubbed his heart’s desire insen-
sible and dragged her home by the hair. A male
rosella, parrot, for instance, having found a suitable
partner, invariably begins by biting her, presumably
to inspire her with respect. After this operation
the gaudily-dressed suitor spreads his tail fanwise,
flirts his wings and displays his points, in order to
attract the lady’s admiration. If she coyly murmurs
“Righto,” the pair look round for a suitable hollow.
* * * *
“Masetto”: Wangaratta (Vic.) furnishes
an instance of a snake fiercely attacking human
beings when disturbed. A citizen and his wife and
two children were driving in a gig when the wheel
collided with a reptile on the road. Getting entangled
among the spokes it was carried round and thrown
among the party and immediately fastened on to the
lower lip of one of the children. The mother promptly
seized the reptile and pulled it off, but in the
struggle was bitten on the hand and had some diffi-
culty in knocking the brute away. The driver applied
first aid to the boy and a doctor from the township
hurried and put the patients out of danger.
(Continued, on page 24.)
MULGA MICK'S REVENGE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Mulga Mick he took a wife —a wife who wasn’t
his;
Though, judging by the way she went, ’twas all the
same to Liz.
He took her out across the miles, and in a sunset
town
The pair picked out a domicile and duly settled
down.
Bill Smith, who was her rightful man in judgment of
the law,
Evolved no threat that he would break old Mulga's
nose or jaw ;
He simply went about his work, contented and
serene,
And did jobs on the level runs, with big- sprees in
between.
The years went by. In Piker’s pub the men one
morning met.
"Good morning, Mulga!” Smith exclaimed. ‘‘Will
you join me in a wet?”
“I will,” said Mulga cheerfully, “if you’ll have one
with me.”
And so began what,might have been a most pro-
digious spree.
“Say, Mulga, why d’you clear with Liz?—jes’ tell
me really why!”
Said Bill when sober etiquette had gone a bit awry.
“I never did you any harm, I’m ’anged if I can see
The reason for that bolt that made a bachelor of me.”
“You know the reason well enough,” old Mulga Mick
replied;
“I swore that I would have revenge the day that
Dingo died.
You poisoned him, that dog o’ mine—a paltry thing
to do;
I swore revenge, and, by the gods, this day I’m
square with you!”
Bill Smith upon the counter laid his glass, and in
his eyes
Gleamed out a light that signified remarkable sur-
prise.
“Me killed your blarsted dorg! Get out! I alwiz
thought you luiowed:
’Twas Liz! What’s that I hear you say?—you’ll
poke along the road?”

FOSTER’S LAGER
“Sliprail”: Let me help “Breeza” (B.
18/4/’18) to kill crows with the aid of half a dozen
rabbit-traps. Shoot, trap or otherwise obtain a couple
of bunnies. Skin them and fix them securely to the
ground. Set the traps right up against the rabbits
and then go along three times a day before breakfast,
pull out the astonished catch and jog off to the
nearest P.P. inspector.
* * * *
“Dial Sight”: “Jimmie Pannikin’s” fare-
well plea for the planting of native trees on the
face of the continent he loved so well was worthy of
so good an Mistralian. During the last three years
spent in trying to do my bit I have seen a fair
amount of France, England and Egypt. The pic-
turesqueness of the first-named country is solely due
to the assiduity with which tree-planting has been
carried on for centuries. From Marseilles, up the
fine valley of the Rhone, right to the north-west
coast at Le Havre we passed endless avenues of
trees, mainly elms, poplars and oaks. No village,
however humble, seems to have neglected this work,
and many a hamlet that would have looked drab and
depressing was made bright and beautiful by reason
of the avenues into which the cobblestoned roads
had been transformed. How I wished to shout a
message to a certain far-off up-country town in
Aussie, treeless and baking under a summer sun, of
the effect of these plantations!
* * * *
“Melos”: If you want a two-piece suit
worth at least a fiver get two cornsacks and treat
thus: Slit No. 1 along the bottom and cut out an
inverted V-shaped piece, starting 12 inches from
each corner and working up to a point about a
foot from the top. Stitch the wound with twine.
You can please yourself about pockets and other
adornments. Then take No. 2, make an opening
for your headpiece, and cut armholes, by slicing
off corners. There’s no need to bother about sleeves
if you are in the habit of wearing a shirt.
* * * *
“H. Harrington” : Another casual! Jack
Dunne, of Priory (N.S.W.), was on his way from
Cobar to Shuttleton with loading on his five drays.
One, transporting rabbit poison, amongst other
goods, suddenly took fire, through some of the stuff
finding its way out of a broken tin. Dunne stopped
the horses and remembered that in this dray there
were also three cases of gelignite and about 1000
caps. In about three seconds the vehicle was in
flames and the horse, screaming with terror, had
the best part of his tail burnt off. There was no
time to take a referendum in a case like this, so
Dunne tipped the load and chanced the luck. Result,
two bottles of tomato-sauce broken! I wouldn’t
have done it for £lOOO.
» * * *
“Roger Roller”: In south-western N.S.W.
the depredations of the galah are enormous. His
tribe is legion and increasing, and they lay waste
the wheat-stacks at grass. In one instance, of 29
acres of hay only five were Cocky’s portion. The
bird has also a humorous twist. At Carrathool he
developed a fad of nipping the telephone wires
between railway stations, and so cutting us off from
the Big Smoke. His beak did the trick. The matter
was reported this way to engineer’s 3taff: “Galahs
bad cutting telephone wires.” Inquisitive officials
at once required the number of galahs concerned. I
have an idea they were only used to one. The man
who has to supply the information is now in the
throes of desperation, as the feathered frolickers
exist in millions, and until they are counted no one
can expect any brass hat to go farther in the matter.

“Wee Bobbin” : Will a tropical horseman
oblige? About 75 per cent, of the horses in the
Solomon Islands suffer from a form of swamp cancer
which attacks the pasterns only. This seems to be
the disease of any consequence that affects our prads,
and it’s got us beat.
* * * *
“Chink”: N. S. Wales is the only State
that has done anything definite in providing
troughs and feed-tables for wild birds, and .there
the good work lies to the credit not so much of the
Education Department as of individual enthusi-
asts like “Jimmie Pannikin” —men who have used
their mateship with Young Australia as a lever
to eject some of the savage element possessed by
every healthy kid. In Queensland the best record
I know of in this respect belongs to the little school
at outback Rolleston. There the kiddies have had
at least half a dozen species of birds housekeeping
in trees planted for that special purpose, and every
day the feathered Australians join up with their
human cobbers at lunch-time.
* * * #
“Aquila”: Ever hear of an eagle attempt-
ing murder and then committing suicide? It hap-
pened at Mount Read, on the Tas. West Coast, when
W. Fisher, a Hobart firm’s foreman, was climbing up
a haulage line. He had to grip the line with both
hands, and consequently had nothing wherewith to
repel the bird, which drove at him twice, and
knocked him down each time. Then, soaring high, it
volplaned straight down, missed its target, and struck
the ground with such force that it killed itself. It
measured 3ft. 6in. from tip to tip.
* * * *
“Felis Domestica”: Our cat, a well-con-
ducted black-and-white Souter who for a good reason
never proposes to the female of his species, behaves
like a clerk in Holy Orders, which he closely resem-
bles in appearance, until the moon is at the full.
Then he spends the hours of dusk and early night in
careering all over the house, running Marathons up
and down the stairs, tobogganing on the mats across
the polished floor, and behaving like a whiz-bang
until utterly exhausted. The waning moon puts an
automatic brake on him. Why? IIow?

“Steve Hart”: Phosphorus is the right
stuff to settle crows, and this is the way to go about
it. When you skin a jumbuek leave a bit of flesh
and fat on the pelt, paint it with the poison, and
hang it on the fence or limb of a tree. I have got
27 crows from one skin. It is as well to remember
that you get no second catch in the same place. The
next bait must be at least half a mile away.

“G. 8.”: For the extermination of crows I
have proved the following l method very successful.
Instead of treating a dead carcase put a little
rabbit poison en small pieces of fat or liver and
scatter them round the corpse. I have stiffened
up to 20 of the cruel pests at the one spot. They
can never resist the fat, and liver is a good second.

“Wart”: The Tasmanian blackfellow was
much behind his mainland cousin in the matter of
weapons. Where the mainland black had the boomer-
ang, the spear-thrower, shield, barbed and stone-
pointed spears and ground and hafted axes and
knives, the islander had only a pointed stick for
spear, a short waddy, chipped stone knives and axes
without handles. Yet he had got ahead of the main-
lander in inventing an intoxicating drink. The trees
from which the sap was obtained are still called
cider-gums. They grow principally up toward the
lakes, one or two very fine specimens being on the
eastern shore of Great Lake. Binghi cut a V-
shaped channel in the bark when the sap was rising
at spring time. The juice was caught and covered
over and left two or three days to ferment. The
resulting brew would make a blackfellow imagine
that he owned the earth and had a mortgage falling
due on the sea and sky. The receptacle used must
have been either the large mutton-fish shells which
the natives sometimes used for drinking vessels, or
the baskets made out of a single leaf of giant kelp
which were used for carrying water.
* ♦ * #
“Silas Snell”: Skilly had just lost his
only farm-hand, and he engaged Warbois on sight
although the man was carrying a donkey-load of
firewood on his back in heavily-timbered country,
and bore in his left hand an ordinary iron bucket
containing water, notwithstanding that the season
was winter, and wet at that. It did not
occur to Skilly to ask Warbois why he had
half destroyed himself carrying that waste
burden all day. Skilly was not an observant
man; if he had a besetting sin it was not
curiosity. And, anyhow, Warbois’s fancy for sail-
ing in ballast was no business of his. True, War-
bois did offer a sort of explanation. “A bit iv
wood’s ’andy,” he said. “So’s water. You offen
want water.” This was perfectly true, as Skilly
was quick to admit. Warbois put the wood and
the water in bis hut, and always locked the door
when he came away. He had a passion for collect-
ing firewood, and when opportunity* offered he carried
water up from the river and poured it in a hole by
the hut. The hole was not water-tight, and Warbois
grew suspicious. He said that it was a fearful dis-
trict for water thieves. He said a mate of his,
name of Carr, once died on the Great Western
plain through running short of water. He thought
something should be done 'to the water thieves.
He reckoned he would buy a gun. Warbois spent a
lot of his spare time watching the river, and said
it was a great shame the way good water was allowed
to escape. He thought he would tie the river up
one of these days. “How’d it be to hobble her?”
he said. Another thing that troubled W arbois was
the great profusion of eggs on Skilly’s table. There
had been a record crop of eggs, and Skilly was
feeding everybody on them, every day and each
meal of the day. Warbois said he thought Skilly
didn’t orter eat them. They orter be saved for food,
Warbois said. He collected egga secretly, and when
Skilly found 10 dozen in the bed in the hut War-
bois explained that he was making pets of them.
He cried when Skilly took the eggs away, and said
his was a lonely, loveless life. Later Warbois
came to the conclusion it was a mistake cows having
whiskers on the end of their tails, and had removed
seven before he was discovered. He had tied one
bunch to a cow’s chin. He said it would grow
there if encouraged. When it rained Warbois put
all the milk-pails and dishes out, and once when
he caught Skilly having a tub in the stable he
attacked his boss in a fury of indignation. “You
dirty swine,” he roared, “spoilin’ water, ’n’ people
dyin’ for the want of it." After a year of this
sort of conduct Warbois was still with Skilly. Then
one morning he turned up to breakfast with a gun
and fired at Skilly. He also fired at Mrs. Skilly,
and he hit Miss Skilly over the head with the butt.
All three were more or less injured. The police
removed Warbois. Warbois said he was a society
for the prevention of cruelty to eggs. What Skilly
said with open-eyed amazement was: “Damn if I
don’t believe now that that feller’s bin a bit off
his head all the time.”

“Koojack”: For as long as I can remem-
ber I have heard dozens of improbable and impossible
theories advanced as to the method employed by
Binghi to fix his handprints upon a rock. Now, at
last, I know. Find a browned sandstone, within a
cave for preference, take a piece of white sandstone,
powder it very fine, and load it into Binghi’s mouth.
Then place your (or his) hand on the surface of afore-
said brown stone and let him squirt the white sand
over and around the hand. In 10 minutes the print
is a fixture.

“Padju'd”: I agree with “Country Ped”
(B. 11/4/’18) that “correspondence” col-
leges urgently require Government investiga-
tion. Recently a telegraph operator came
to me to laiow if I could fit him to pass the
Commonwealth wireless test. Having had
five years’ land and sea expedience I said I
thought I could. He informed me. as some-
thing of a joke, that he was radio instructor
at Gr*ab and Steele’s College. Astounded, I
asked him how he managed. “I read it out
from a’- correspondence course,” he replied.
•‘But suppose,” I said, “a student wants
something elucidatedV” “I bluster through
somehow,” he replied. An outlay of £1
would buy the three text-books from which
these exorbitantly dear lessons are mainly
lifted, but in my opinion it is doubtful if
anything tan really be learnt therefrom.
Most of these schools “guarantee” positions,
but as the wireless section of the Australian
mercantile marine is the monopoly of one co.
(to which none of the remarks in this par.
apply)? that guarantee is quite as useful as a
guarantee of a place in the Angel Choir.
“G.T.”: As what “Country Ped” (B.
11/4/T8) knows about correspondence col-
leges appears to have been learned in the
dormitory of one of them, let someone give
him a second lesson. The “reputable”
correspondence colleges which also run per-
sonal instruction schools (I admit they can,
unfortunately, be counted on the fingers of
one hand) in a great measure employ their
personal-instruction staffs to conduct the
postal tuition, such teachers giving certain
specified time each day to the setting of
lessons and correction of papers, said lessons
not being set from text-books and not being
corrected from keys. Personal explanations,
from the teacher’s own wide experience, are
given when mistakes occur. Also, these
teachers are generally experts in their par-
ticular branches. They’ve got to be. The
keen competition between the _ colleges de-
mands it. In the higher technical subjects,
such as engineering, architecture, surveying,
etc., these colleges, in many cases, pay high
percentages to highly-qualified, practical,
outside men to conduct their lessons for
them, thus ensuring sound, practical in-
struction and guaranteeing satisfaction to
those students prepared to work conscienti-
ously. “Certainly hundreds of ambitious
innocents are wasting their money,” says
“Country Ped.” Certainly thousands of
ambitious triers are getting sound instruc-
tion and being lifted out of the rut, where,
if correspondence colleges were not in exist-
ence, they would have no chance of receiving
instruction at all. “Registration and Gov-
ernment supervision is the remedy,” says
“Country Ped.” Correct. And the colleges
I have in mind would welcome the change
with open arms. It would give them a bet-
ter standing and wipe out the “penny-
snatchers.” However, when the registra-
tion and examination of teachers in busi-
ness colleges commenced in Victoria, the
Council of Education, or some similar body,
ser the papers for shorthand teachers. The
theory section of the exam, (the shell, ker-
nel, stem and branch of any shorthand
teacher’s equipment) could have been passed
by any student of average intelligence after
a few months’ instruction, while the speed
test was made the whole thing. Ability to
write high speed is a secondary consideration
for any first-class shorthand teacher. If he
has the ability so much the better for him-
self. though it does not necessarily follow
that his sudents will benefit. Registration
and supervision by till means, but let it be
by men who know.
* * * *
“F.N.T.”: A new Australian trade has
sprung up since the war put up the freight
on pianos. The business consists of refitting
dead pianos and putting them back on the
market at attractive prices. Young gentle-
men of brisk appearance and ingratiating
manners tour the suburbs as piano-tuners, a
job which isn’t like pulling teeth or repair-
ing weak hearts, no registration being neces-
sary—and no knowledge of piano-tuning
either. The “tuner” can get into most
houses “to have a look at the piano” by
quoting a bargain, rate for a plain tuning.
Once he gets the piano’s organs strewn
around on the carpet he discovers that the
moths have been at their work and a lot of
repairs are necessary, re-felting for instance.
He quotes a ridiculously low figure for that
job, too. A plausible practitioner, putting
his best gold-tooth forward, can soon smile
the housewife into letting him take a part
of the piano away for treatment, to be re-
turned in three, or four days; and that
closes the transaction. The removed part is
never handed back, but goes to provide a new
lung or liver for a decayed piano owned by
some obscure dealer far away.
* * * *
“Plumber” : The report of the Inter-State
Commission on the High Prices of Groceries
is an illuminating document in the best
academic style. The Commish discovered an
“absurd waste of effort and capital” among
small retail grocers (the 69 shops in the Mel-
bourne metropolitan area whose returns were
investigated actually lost £1874 last year) ;
also it found an “utter lack of organisa-
tion on the part of the public.” It recom-
mends co-operation in place of the eternal
wail about high prices and the everlasting
appeals to politicians to “do something.”
This advice has virtues, but is largely outed
by the Commish’s own asseverations regard-
ing the “dominating position occupied by
associations of wholesale merchants,” who
“frequently contravene the prices fixed by
the authorities.” Any small co-operative
concern dealing with them would have the
same chance of survival as the Servians had
with the Huns; but if trade unionists would
but tackle the job energetically they could
make themselves strong enough to be their
own wholesale merchant; or, at least, dic-
tate their own terms to the existing firms.
Such a scheme would put more money in
their pockets than miles of deputations or
gallons of talk—but it means hard work,
unselfish co-operation and little glory.
* * * *
“Wart”: The Serpentine Paint Co., which
is mining oxide at Beaconsfield (Tas.) and
turning it into paint at Launceston, will
have its product on the market this month.
Local users who have tried samples swear
it is the best paint in the world. The co. is
turning out burnt sienna, raw sienna, red
oxide, chrome oxide, mast color and sage
green—the last said to be unique in natural
oxides.
* * * *
“Werrimee”: Send this item from an old
(1874) file of the Narrabri (N.S.W.)
Herald to William Noah Hughes, ship-
builder :
In the Mehi River, a short distance down from
Moree, there was successfully launched a paddle-
wheel boat, named the Banjo Box. It. was built by
Mr. Michael McCabe. The machinery was con-
structed by Mr. McMahon. Dimensions: length of
keel, 16ft. ; breadth of beam, 3ft. 9in.; extreme
depth of hold, ISirj. She was worked by side wheels,
the motive power being supplied by the pedal ex-
tremities of her crew. Commander, Captain Blue-
skin. She was to sail for Moree; from thence to the
Barwon, down it to the Darling, to its confluence
with the Murray, straight then for Adelaide.
She wasn’t much of a vessel, but she was
worth more than a bag of speeches.
* * * *
In its noble work of eking out the meat-
supply, Australia’s bunny gets little moral
support from Professor Osborne, of Mel-
bourne Uni. The prof, has issued a warning
against the excessive use of rabbit as a meat-
substitute. Too much wile rabby may cause
a craving for “something stronger,” with a
possibility of malnutrition and anaemia.
Still, on many remote selections where the
housewife knows how to cook and where the
sizzling of beefsteak on the grid is seldom
heard, stuffed bunny will continue to. be a
pleasant change from roast ribs of pumpkin.
# * * *
“Peta”: It looks as if the Yanco irrigation
area (N.S.W.) is going to be the seed-bed of
noxious weeds for the entire Riverina. In
ordinary conditions dry spells kill off all or
almost all weeds, but here, where there is
always water in canals and drains, they know
no check. At the present time every road-
side on the area is thickly lined with stink-
wort, often Bft. high. So, too, are the canal
banks. Paddocks on neglected farms present
an extraordinary appearance, with their full
crop of what is locally known as Sendall’s
lucerne, in compliment to the Resident Com-
missioner. The seed is light as thistledown
and in a high wind travels indefinitely. A
little later on, as the docks take possession
of canal banks, seeds will blow into the
water and be carried from farm to farm
amidst thehearty cheers of the man on the land.
* * * *
All the pirates are not in U-boats. The
“associated” rate for water freightage
between Sydney and Melbourne is 14s. per
ton nowadays; but shipping space is very
scarce. Recently, under the very nose of
the Shipping Control Board, some enterpris-
ing Sydney people who had secured a boat
offered to transport stuff to Melbourne at
455. per ton —the 31s. in excess of the fixed
rate being for “commission” and “expenses” !
A similar little scheme was baulked several
months ago when the charterer of a collier
wanted £5OOO for a run to Adelaide. Later,
however, he was beaten down to about half
that figure; but before arrangements could
be finalised the Shipping Board stepped in,
seized the vessel and restored it to the inter-
state coal trade.
* * * *
During the few months Napoleon I. spent
in Egypt he started schools, libraries, col-
leges, a new code of laws, model farms and
sundries; also he incited the native, who had
given up hope under the Turk, to dig the silt
out of scores of miles of irrigation canals and
drive back the encroaching desert. Now the
British in Mesopotamia are showing that
Napoleon wasn’t the only boulder on the
beach. There was nothing wrong with that
country except the Turk, who had breathed
on the vast irrigation system for 400 years
and dried up Paradise. For a teetotaller the
Turk has a very hot breath. Already the
new British authority has reclaimed 300,000
acres by the simple process of setting the in-
habitants to dig the sand out of the irriga-
tion channels, and the inhabitant expects his
first square meal in centuries.
THE PRICE O’ VICTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Eh mon, the warl's gang- sad amiss,
The times is sail- agley ;
Waesucks for days o’ joy an’ bliss —
A’ tint it seems tae me.
There’s nobbut war an’ bluidy death
An’ wranglin’ fit tae deave ye,
An’ censor loons wha’s every breath
Is breathed for tae deceive ye.
’Tis all gone wrang ; there’s naething richt —
A’ daft frae last tae first—
As I was sayin’ yesternicht,
Tae my frien’ Jock McThirst:
I’m no, ye mind, avairse tae strife
In reason; I’m no lavish
In humbleness afore the wife,
Like puir, daft Rab McTavish.
But there’s in a’ a medium grist,
An’, look ye now, this war
Has gone the noo, ye mind me, juist
A mickle bit tae far.
Not that I grutch the price we’ve paid
In bluid an’ bone an’ muscle;
Ye maun pay that when a’ is said
In ilka muekle tussle.
But whiles we’ve done oor cantie bit
Tae niak’ yon Kaisov sair,
'Tis well tae fash oorsels a whit
Abobt what’s daein’ mail - .
I’m tellin’ ye it’s well tae mind
In a’ consairns ye nearly,
Ye dinna at the end o’t find
Ye’ve purchased aught tae dearly.
Now let ma perroration tell
The tboet that’s in ma heid,
That he wha runs ma vairses well
The same may richtly read:
Is it no' time tae stop the fray,
As I spiered o’ McClusky,
When they wad mak ’ a Christian pay
Ninepulicc the gill for whusky?
N.B.W.
ALISTER McALISTER McPHAJRSON.
The Inky Way.
Emily Mary Williams set out from the
Homoeopathic Hospital, Melbourne, on the
morning of April 25 to interview Peter. She
had long been associated with country jour-
nalism, and her signature, “Penelope,” was
especially well known in Geelong.
* * * *
Sydney D.T. on the Zeebrugge raid:—
It is not difficult to realise that two shiploads of
quick-setting comment, . sunk right across the
entrance to the enemy’s main advanced base for sub-
marines, constitutes a very effective achievement.
Thank Heaven, they’ve found a use for
Win-tlie-War speeches at last!
* * * *
“Chevron”: Talking of the noble record
of Longwood (Vic.), which has won more
war honors, probably, than any town of its
size in the whole wide world, I remember
going up there for the Aug us on one of the
late Allan McLean’s political tours. I met
the Argus correspondent, a delightful old
gentleman named Treyvaud. who assumed
(it wouldn’t be a safe bet nowadays) that,
being an Argus man, I must be a classical
scholar. lie mentioned that Napoleon’s resi-
dence at St. Helena was called Longwood,
and that a local school-teacher had christened
his house St. Helena. And he quoted a
classical tag. In after years the elder son
of that school-teacher won the Victoria Cross
in Gallipoli, and was killed in action- in
France. He was Major Frederick Tubb'.
And in France his brother, Captain Frank
Tubb. won the Military Cross. The appro-
priateness of that old gentleman’s quotation
comes back to me: “Exegi monumentum
acre perennius .”
* ■* * *
“Eddyson”: Luther Bradley, the Ameri-
can artist, began his career as a journalist on
a Melbourne weekly called Life, owned by
W. H. Williams, the tenor printer. Bradley
was artist and editor, and Victor Daley was
one of his valued contributors; but fre-
quently wide breaches in the space allotted
to Daley had to be patched by the harried
sub. with tatters from the American ex-
changes. Then came Thompson, who brought
glad tidings of temperance benefits and made
something of a hit in Melbourne. Bradley
had known Thompson as a medical student
in old Ma-aschewsetts. The temperance
evangelist haunted Life office, and a quaint
companionship was established between him
and Daley. The poet reformed, and was
notoriously sober for weeks, with the result
that confidence was restored, and Bradley
consented to start his serial. It ran several
weeks, dealing with a young man in a
trance, whom two Bohemian companions took
through the country, exhibiting him as a
mummy—the only long story Daley ever
attempted. Then there was a sudden re-
lapse ; the week’s consignment of the serial
did not turn up. Next week the copy was
still missing. So was the distinguished
author. Bradley finished the story himself
with a jolt calculated to dislocate the neck
of an interested reader, and shortly
afterwards Daley put in an appear-
ance. He explained: “It’s poor Thompson,
my dear fellow. Many of the people
he converts bring him votive offerings
of whisky. They bring their bottles
and place them at his feet as a demonstra-
tion of good faith, and an assurance that
they will never again taste, touch, nor
handle. He gets a quantity of whisky in this
way, a great deal more than he can drink
himself. You will remember I undertook to
write three temperance hymns for him.
Being a little short of money, he is paying
me in whisky—a bottle a hymn.” Daley lin-
gered in the doorway. “I shall not resume
the serial "this month,” he said. “I am writ-
ing a whole book of hymns.”
* * * *
In consequence of the shortage of paper
in England —and it is getting nearly as
bad in Australia—The Times daily output
is now limited to 120,000 copies, and people
who want it have to sign a form that they
will share their copy with at least one other
reader. The price has been put back to
threepence. The Telegraph, after a
prosperous career of nearly 60 years as a
penny paper, apologises profusely for raising
the price to twopence.
OLD POETS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where are the pleasing bards I knew,
Whose songs so glorified my 3'outh,
And rippled all the keen day through
With warp of beauty and of truth?
no robes of purple sheen
Nor wreaths (unless they be of rue),
But honest was their work, I ween.
Where are the singing men I knew?
Where do my early poets bide?
I hear at times a far-off strain,
But in no chariot do they ride,
Their homes glow never on the plain.
I see them not in gardens fair
Where good old men sit side by side,
Cut off from need, absolved of care.
Where do my early poets bide?
Where rest the poets in their age,
Whose melody was part of life,
Whose splendid violence did wage
With liars all inspiring strife?
I see the faded doctor there,
Bent low, and picking at the page;
His greying life no troubles wear.
Where rest the poets in their age?
To what kind haven have they gone
Now fire but sparkles in the ash ?
The lawyer sits his life to con
In soft contentment when the clash
Of argument is done, and he
Steps down to let the world go on.
Where are the bards once dear to me;
To what kind haven have they gone?
Where are the poets once I knew?
The tradesman crowned with snow
hath ease,
The broker drives the city through.
His age hath everything to please.
All other men have gentle end
To, mark new life with placid view,
In pillowed peace their souls to mend—
Where are the poets once I knew ?
VICTORIA.
E. D.

SAYS PHOSPHATE INCREASES
STRENGTH AND ENDURANCE.
Weak, nervous, debilitated men and
women who become exhausted after the
slightest mental or physical exertion, to
whom sleep is denied, and whose drawn,
haggard features bear mute testimony to
their pitiable condition, can regain health,
strength and endurance in a few weeks by
obtaining from the chemist a supply of
5-grain bitro-pliosphate tablets, and taking
one regularly at each meal. All the abovg
painful conditions are due to weak nerves
and lack of nervous energy. Bitro-phosphate
feeds the starved, shrunken nerves, and
builds up a reserve of nervous energy by
supplying in the only assimilable form
those phosphoric food elements which are
the life of the nervous system. The results
following its use are most gratifying.
Sleeplessness usually disappears within a
week or ten days, thin people put on flesh,
strength returns to shrunken muscles, the
power of endurance is doubled, sunken cheeks
fill out, the eyes become bright, and all the
old nervous worries and dreads disappear,
and life becomes what it should be —worth
living. Try it and see.— Advt.
