Somewhere—A Long Time Ago—
We read a pretty little story
About two sweethearts who
planted two acorns
Close together—
And .the young people were
separated—or one died
(It’s a long time since we read
the story) ;
But the acorns became oak
tree's,
Large ones, with wide, spread-
ing branches,
That twined about each other —
Like sweethearts —•
And one tree could not be cut
down
Without destroying the other.
And we in the industry are like
that —■
We’re mutually interdependent.
One end of the business
Is so wrapped about the other
That its success or failure
Depends on the success or
failure of the other.
Imagine a Film industry
Without Producers and Dis-
tributors—•
Or without Exhibitors!
It couldn’t be.
So let’s “pull” together—
And win together—•
How else can we serve you ?
PARAMOUNT PICTURES.
ALEC LORIMORE, Managing Director, 302 Pitt Street, Sydney.
POLITICAL
POINTS
HIS COMPLAINT.
“The Labor party stands for Peace by negotia-
tion.” — Vic. Labor Manifesto.
“ What’s up with tile little fellow?”
“Mistaken identity, my dear sir. He thinks
he’s the big fellow.”
The story was told last week that Premier
Ryan, of Queensland, was going to become
leader of the Federal Labor party—that it
had all been arranged with Tudor, who had
agreed to the change. Of course, Tudor,
when seen, announced that he hadn’t done
anything of the sort. But he knows quite
well that if Ryan did come into Federal
politics there would be a change not long
afterwards. As a party whip Tudor was
unequalled. He is patient, assiduous and
personally popular. The same qualities made
him a very valuable Minister for Customs.
But he is not a leader. He has little imagi-
nation ; he has less boldness. He is always
measuring his own weakness against the
strength of the party machine, and the result
is timidity. He could keep his wicket up
right through an innings, but he couldn’t
make the runs to win a match.
The truth about Ryan of Queensland is
that he doesn’t know what In; is going to do.
Under our Federal system he believes there
is. with normal conditions, more work and
scope in State than in national politics, there
being no national politics to speak of. This
indicates afresh the weakness of our present
system. Under war conditions the Common-
wealth Government has taken vast powers
REINFORCEMENTS.
ANZAC: “’Ullo, Bill, they’re sending the
Win-the-War blokes. They must be
gettin' down to the last man and the
last shilling.”
and is responsible for huge expenditures ; yet,
because State activities make so strong
an appeal in normal times, the Federal work
is being done by a very few first-raters and
a vast majority of second- and third- and
fourth-raters, while vastly superior men in
State politics stand by and do nothing much
except tinker vfth the village pump.
That Lloyd George should have to put up
with military “intrigues”—his Government
has just passed through another “crisis” thus
caused—is nobody’s fault but his own. When
he took over the job from the hesitating
Asquith he started to work with the old
bureaucratic War machine instead of mak-
ing a clean sweep; and every time since
then that he has used the small hand-broom
to shift some single incompetent there has
been trouble.
McGrath, out of khaki and speaking in
the Commonwealth Parliament of the Aus-
tralian soldiers who wanted to transfer to
the flying service and couldn’t get permis-
sion, lately put the case thus
Australians make the best flying- men in the world,
and many thousands of them desire to transfer to
the flying corps. Great Britain has the machines,
but is waiting for the men to mount them. General
Birdwood has granted some transfers, but has refused
them to many skilled mechanics and others who
would make good pilots.
Now it is explained by the British authori-
ties that “the establishment of new Austra-
lian squadrons would mean the withdrawal
ol fighting men from the lines.” So one
specially deplorable result of the shortage-
in Australian man-power at the Front is the
military necessity for keeping men in the
trenches who would be more valuably. em-
ployed in dropping bombs from the air if
the divisions in the field were not crying out
for reinforcements.
Sydney Telegraph on a financial matter:
The Federal Treasurer. .. .threatens to take action
against persons idly disparaging the soundness of
the national currencv. These persons, whoever they
are, will quite deserve all they get.... The Federal
notes are issued on the security of Australia un-
limited. The gold, of which the Federal Government
has an ample stock, is only the first reserve Back
of that is every stick of property and every penny of
income in the Commonwealth.
The italicised words are worthy of all con-
sideration. They apply to any paper cur-
rency—German, Austrian, Turkish. \\ ild-
cat-Central- American-republican, assignats of
the first French revolution, etc. Also they
apply to every public debt that is or ever
was —even though half of them or more are
in default and are as stiff as Jeff Davis’s
debentures. The Telegraph didn't finish
its story. In the last resort the only thing
that is behind anything in the way of pub-
lic lOU’s is the character of the people, and
if they have been taught that it is better to
borrow than to learn how to be sufficient for
themselves, and been assured by comfortable
daily papers that the “boundless natural
resources” will always see them through, the
character may prove rather flabby when it
is wanted.
Winchelsea Shire Council has sensibly
asked that provision should be made in the
new. Vic. Health Bill for
the licensing of boarding-
houses. At present any-
body can take in boarders
or be taken in by them,
there being no supervision
of the kitchen or sanitary
arrangements, or any regu-
lation insisting upon a
proper allowance of cubic
space per victim. The
kitchens of some compara-
tively classy East Mel-
bourne hash-foundries
would earn for their pro-
prietors a stiff tine if
attached to a pub or cafe,
and some of the cubicles
that masquerade as bed-
rooms howl for inspection.
But at the present rate of
progress the Health Bill
will not reach Parliament
until this generation of
boarders dies or acquires a
missus.
Victoria suffers not only
socially but financially
from the shamefully in-
efficient administration of
its Venereal Diseases Act.
Since the measure came
into force about 6000 male
sufferers have reported;
yet cases which require
constant treatment have
still to be sent to the
Langwarrin military camp.
The Commonwealth is paid
for their accommodation
and the cost is greater
than if the State attended
properly to the job. The position as
regards women is a scandal. Between
SOO- and 1000 have reported, but, owing to
the absence of night clinics at which girls
unable to leave their occupations in the day-
time could be dealt with, the health authori-
ties are faced with an impossible task.
For the nine months to the end of March
the Commonwealth’s war expenditure out of
borrowed money averaged £1,107,000 per
week, and out of taxation £177.000 per week.
The £177,000 apparently goes mainly in
etceteras. But it doesn't include all the
etceteras by any means. Even the cost of
the piebald Conscription referendum was
passed on to the kid.
The “wets” can’t get any comfort from the
latest trial of strength—at Launceston and
thereabouts, for a Leg. Council seat. The
retiring member, hon. Minister Shields, who
had the vote of Bung and the moderates on
a previous occasion, was this time vigorously
opposed by the former. Bung ran Oldham,
a wine and spirit merchant, who gave Shields
a close run when he first wooed the electors.
The third candidate was Peter Gannon, put
up by the Labor party—the first time this
section had nominated a candidate for the
Council. The result was a romp home for
Shields, who had the wholehearted support
of the Water Waggon. Gannon was lost in
the dust.
The door of N. S. Wales’s only match-
factory has been banged. The manufacturer
is generally imagined to be a person who
finds his raw material at his hand, so that
when his price is fixed by law it is only the
wage question which has to be considered.
But the raw material of one industry is
often the finished product of another, and
isn’t “raw” at all; and for want of a thou-
sand little industries which we haven’t got
and hardly dream of the manufacturer is
often only one remove further from the
importing biz than the importer himself, and
just one degree less a slave to the war-price
extortioner. The match-factory reported
briefly that its imported raw materials had
gone up so high that matches at 3jd. per
dozen were impossible, so it put on its hat
and took the mostly-imported tram-car home
at the increased fare.
A TOUGH OF THE REAL THING.
“The new Recruiting Depot in Sydney will have
bright surroundings. A brass band will be in
attendance.”
BILLJIM : “That’s orlright; but a bit o’ this
music ’d wake 'em up a darn sight
quicker.”
From the Gallery : —•
Labor's chance of achieving office appears horribly
remote when men like Higgs, Catts and Considine
are allowed to pour out their damaging eloquence.
Higgs is the party’s spruiker for a “negotiated peace”
and his advocacy is sustained by the delusion that
Fritz will eat our conditions out of our hand. He
scoffs at the “bitter-enders” ; apparently be prefers
to be a never-ender whose artless pacifism would give
Germany opportunities for further war. But on this
matter Higgs is quite insensible to reason or facts.
Catts is hardly less dangerous, though his insanity
takes the form of sowing distrust of certain inter-
national relations. When you add to those two the
truculent Considine panting to pose as a “Boshevik”
and succeeding very well, you feel that those and
their kind will be the. ruin of Labor. A while back
the now-absent Anstey declared that Labor was march-
ing to its Sedan ; it looks as if it is going there
via Bedlam and Broken Hill.
The great Washington, in April, 1778: —
We can talk about patriotism and cite examples
from antiquity of heroic deeds performed under! its
influence only ; but whoever shall rely on this, founda-
tion for the conduct of a long and bloody war shall
find out his mistake before it is all over. I do not
ignore patriotism, and I know that in war much has
been accomplished under its influence ; but I will not
dare to say that any important and long war could be
sustained by any such principle.
Well, thank God, that’s some consolation.
We are no worse than Washington expected
a country to be under circumstances such as
ours. But it only makes it clearer that
Hughes and Pearce, in heaping scorn upon
this paper’s efforts to get universal service
adopted early in the war, played Germany’s
game—doubtless through their profound igno-
rance of history.
THE COSMOPOLITES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“A meeting was held in Brisbane and addressed
by orators in a variety of tongues. Russian, Finnish
and several other languages were spoken and polyglot
anthems were interspersed. Denunciation of the war
was the topic.”
We held a glorious meeting up in Brisbane Sunday
night
To down them ’owlin’ Jingoes an’ to help the cause
of Right.
We ’eard a power of speeches from a crowd with
lungs o’ leather
An’ we sung a ’eap of anthems, one by one and
altogether.
First Petrovitch the Rooslmn give his testimony
straight—■
’E knew, he sed, the Bolsheviks ’ad got the proper
gait;
They’d turned old Russia upside down, ’e owned, as
it was plain,
An’ now they only wanted time to get ’er right
again."
An’ then the glorious anthem of ’is motherland he
sung—
And ’e spit the words out bonzer in ’is own melojus
tongue;
We chimed along to ’elp ’im, an’ we done the best we
could,
Though the ’armohy resultin’ didn’t strike me as it
should.
Then Andrew Andrezinscki —'e’s a kind of Deutscher
Pole—■
Got up an’ told the ’eathens that his country ’ad a
Soul;
That Peace was all she wanted, an’ until she got the
same
She’d never ’elp the Allies in their sordid little game.
An’ then our chairman took the floor an’ read a
touehin’ letter
From Comrade Schwillinghausen, who remarked: “1
dinks it better,
Considerin' dot yOur Government dey vant to cut my
vizen,
To gif my speed) geschrieben, und to dodge der
tyrant's prison.
“I hope you vill gontinue to denounce dis bloody
var;
Der Kaiser did not vant it, as he told you vonce
before;
If France und England drop deir arms, der Krieg
vill soon be over.
Den all der vorld vill frisk aboudt, mein friendts,
among der clover.”
And then we resolooted in a general sort of way
That War’s a bloody madness and that we ’ad come
ter stay ;
That “this cosmopolite meetin’ is the universe’s
hub”—
An’ then we broke up cheerin’ an’ departed for the
pub.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

NEEDS
Possibly there has never been better-class
fields for an Adelaide Cup meeting than those at the
three days just ended. - On the first day the only
wins for visitors were the first and last events, both
of which went to Robinson and Clark. In the Elder
Stakes, w.f.a., the brilliant Magpie had his first
race in Australia, and won comfortably from Wedge,
Whitefield third. The partnership s other win was
a Maiden Plate; the much-thonglit-of Escomb had
come down to that, after his failure in the Aus-
tralian Cup, and scored a win at hist. In that
company he was backed down to 10 to 1 on—a 225.
dividend on the tote. By way of contrast, the very
next race, a hurdle, returned £62 10s., with the
consolation of £9 2s. for the second. Local owners
did well on the day. Stagegirl (bred by Sol Green)
won the S.A. Stakes (2-year-old) for Patterson and
Bullock, Broken Glass (Eye Glass—Explosion mare)
the Leger for Mrs. Hawker, from Scobie’s Seabound.
And the big six-furlong Goodwood Handicap, with
Brewer’s Trillion favorite, was won easily by Flan-
nagan’s Pistolarie from Hawker's Mareca. It was
Dempsey’s first ride and first win in S.A., and the
time, lmin. 13sec., is a record for the State.
Flannagan came within a nose of completing .a
big double with the Adelaide Cup, his Shacko get-
ting as close as that to Robinson and Clark’s Els-
don (Symington—Eleetra), which had been backed
as though he could not lose. This Englishman came
to Australia as a sprinter, but is now clearly a
stayer. The time, 2min. 46sec. for a mile and 5 fur-
longs, is easily S.A. record, and within a fraction
of Fortune Teller’s Australian record, at Randwick.
J. V. Smith’s Rosanna was a bad third. Wedge was
top weight, but can hardly stay the distance, and
news from Hobart had something to do with
his starting at all. Robinson and Clark always
support the Adelaide meetings well, but this was
their first Cup. Elsdon paid only £2 9s. ; Shacko
gave £9 Is. —which would have been over £27 if
he had won.
* * * *
Sydney punters went ouh to Rosebill on
Saturday armed to the teeth and landed home with a
heavy cargo of spoil. I. Mo got hit both ways—•
straight out and on the double. Of seven winners
four were outright favorites, two were next door and
there was enough support for the ex-pony Toorak at
10’s to make the pay-out on the Granville Stakes a
painful business. The tote turned over £10,209,
and in no ca?e did the machine nay a winning divi-
dend equal to the price available in Israel. In five
cases machine place-bettors got less than they put
in ; once they got their money back and four times
they laid odds on.
* » * *
Imported Early Morn did it again on
Saturday. Starting at 3’s in the Rosehill Handicap
he came with a late run at the right spot and just
snuffed out the long-priced Dynasty on the line. Hav-
ing given this nag a chance to win. a race or two,
the probabilities are that the will now
get busy and see about stopping him. The main
point about the race was the evidence it gave of
Bruyere’s false price in the Newcastle Cup. Solely
on the strength of a flash run before getting punc-
tured in a race at Randwick a couple of months ago
and of some smart track work, the mare was backed
for the Newcastle event down to s’s in a field of 87.
On Saturday at Rosehill weight of money brought
her to 9 to 4. The stable seemed to reckon it a
certainty, but at no time did she look a winner and
finished a long way back. Certainly she had some
bad luck in the running, but it was a very ordinary
field. The performance suggests that the mare has
been a bit over-rated.
* * * *
Petalster. full brother of that good horse
First Principle, put up a record for the Rosehill
Course on Saturday, running the mile Auburn Handi-
cap in “39L” The little fellow, who is not so par-
tial to weight as his brother was, carried 7st. 121 b.
and had an easy win.
* # * *
Crossing the last jump in the Hurdles at
Rosehill on Saturday Bazil K. and Blue Gull had a
bump. The first-named got the worst of it, and Blue
Gull beat him home. Whereupon several excited
ppnters, reckoning that (1) there would be a protest,
and (2) that Bazil K. would get the race, started
doing business on those lines. But there was no
protest, nor did the stewards interfere—rightly, this
page thinks, since Bazil K. brought the trouble on
himself by running down the fence. At the settling
several of these late backers reckoned that in the
absence of protest they should not pay. The practice
in such a case is well established. They lost their
money, and deserved to.
* # * *
There was a time when the Earlston horse
Winston was reckoned good enough to start in the
best company, though Heaven only knows what he
was given a run in the last Metropolitan for. But
Winston won’t be started any more. He fell in the
Hurdles at Rosehill (Sydney) on Saturday and broke
his neck. He cost £577 as a yearling, and in the
five intervening years had not won a race.
* * * *
When Panacre started in the Shorts (seven
furlongs), the first event on the menu at Newcastle
on Saturday, he carried lOst. 41b. and most of the
cash. He was beaten out of place. When he went
out with lOst. 31b. in the last race, the Totalisator
Handicap, offers of 8 to l,were not rushed. He won
as he liked; whereupon there were loud yells of
derision from the crowd and the horse was led away
amid a chorus of “Boo” and “Hoo.” The noise was
not justifiable. There were some fairly smart horses
in the first race. In the second the field was con-
spicuously inferior, and, further, the journey was a
mile, which is Panacre's pet distance. It was later
explained by' the owner that he had backed the nag
in the mile for only 90 to 20 on a concession bet and
took 35 to 10 about another horse. To this page it
looked as if someone who lost a dollar over the Shorts
started to howl and all hands joined in on general
principles. The number of times the public stands
being rooked in silence and bursts into agonised yelps
over nothing is one of the most astonishing things in
connection with racing. #
There are a fearful lot of scrubbers racing
on Sydney’ pony courses, but sometimes a little ’on
happens along who can do things worth while. One
of these is named Arrow Head. On Saturday she
won the 14.2 Handicap at Ascot with list. 81b. on
board. This included jewt. of lead. Half a dozen
of the starters were burdened with Ast. lib. less than
the winner. *
* * * *
The A.J.C. looks far ahead and announces
the prize-money for next season’s big events, and the
Leger and Sires’ Produce Stakes of 1920. Half the
prize-money will be paid in war-savings certificates ;
and any surplus after exes, have been met will, as of
late, be passed on to the Patriotic till.
* * * *
N. S. Wales country pony-racing clubs are
demanding to know how it helps to win the war to
stop them holding a meeting while letting the Sydney
ponies have a run every week. No one has been able to
answer them, and this paper fears no one ever will
until they get hold of theright sort of string to pull.
Then the rest will be quite easy.
* * * *
Young Bracken seems certain to be at the
top of the winning jock,s’ list when the season ends.
At Rosehill on Saturday he had six rides and got a
double and a second. D. O’Connor, an auprentice,
had a couple of wins. Barden was in front once
and second twice. There are at least four apprentices
riding at Sydney meetings just now who look likely
to become really good horsemen —and at least two
jockeys who have been near the top for some time
and seem to be getting no better.
* # * *
Weighed out for the Final Handicap—
Denny Callinan, one of the best-known Australian
horsemen. In the years before the war Callinan had
been on winners all over the Commonwealth. He was
killed on Anzac Day in France, and the time hoard
said “35.”

RANDWICK
TRAINER
DICK O'CONNOR.
An honest figure disappeared from sport-
ing circles in Sydney last week when Spencer
Cornford (“Early Bird”) went west. Like most of
the early sporting writers he started as a compositor
and was in the S. M. Herald “companionship” in
the day’s when “Bill” Higgs, since then Common-
wealth Treasurer, was its caricaturist and chief
jester. For a whole generation he had been with the
Stab and Sun. and hung to his job until compelled
to give up through sheer weakness. He was Well
known as a judge at the pony meetings. At one of
his last course engagements, when the shadow of
death was already over him, there was a hostile
demonstration against one of his decisions, but the
little man was right, as he usually was in these
matters. One of his last scores as a tipster for the
Sydney Sun was Bronzetti for the Caulfield Cup, and
for the Melbourne Cup he refused to hear of anything
but Westcourt.
* * * *
“Mujik”: Buyers of yearlings at the
autumn sales are probably looking for names for
the nags. I don’t know where owners go for the
outrages they so often discover, but in case they look
on this page for an idea here are a few: Paralytic,
Pair of Crutches, Locomotor Ataxia, Indigestion, Pork
Pie, Anesthesia, Stop Short, Cold Feet, Sockdarner,
Stone Dead, War Weary, Breach of Promise, Holdme-
purse, Backslider, Misser, Dopewalloper, Stringtied,
Haltela. If there is nothing in that lot how about
Bedridden ?
* * * *
“Y.Z.”: It was $t a small race-meeting
near Wallace Town (Vic.). Two men had
trundled a barrel of beer into the trees at the
back of the grandstand, and here we found it. The
day was hot; we were a-thirst. Round the portly
barrel was a cardboard notice: “All Drinks Six-
pence.” There were two pannikins on top 'of the
barrel, and the two owners were just beyond, one
lying down drowsily drunk, one standing up, just as
drunk, and just as drowsy. My cobber tendered a
shilling, and asked for two pints. The standing
drunk kicked the prostrate one. “Dan!” he said.
“Dan! Dan! Here’s a bloke wantin’ to buy our
beer.” Dan sat up and blinked. “Wantin’ buy
our beer?” he hiccuped. “Well, stoush the cow!”
* * * *
To win 10s. and a sucking-pig a citizen
of Newcastle started to spend a recent Saturday
swallowing beer. To acquire the cash and the animal
he had to get outside 60 pints in eight hours. He
finished 2i hours ahead of timetable. It was a fool-
hardy thing to do, and could only have been per-
petrated by a man of exceptional recklessness or
stupendous ignorance about the limitations of the
human kidney. A pint every smin. 45see. for nearly
six hours may be something in the way of a record
guzzle. But it is a disgusting performance which
might well be left alone. If the winner scoffed the
pig for dinner on Sunday he ought to have felt fairly
full. But perhaps it was given to him for company.
* * * *
“Henry H.”: “Umfaan,” who writes (B.
9/5/’18) about the camel as a racer, didn’t add much
new aspect to the versatile beast. He can do almost
everything except cook. Even as a cab-horse he has
his points. I have seen a gaunt member of the tribe
who looked greenish in a side-light dragging an Arab
family in a four-wheeler (the kind you hire at
Hobart, where they all smell of cigars) at Aden,
while fat, middle-aged Pa Arab hit him with a colored
cotton brolly to make him improve his pace. And
I knew a small camel that seemed to do nothing but
reside in a back yard, where he had a job as watch-
dog. He is a weight-carrier, a sprinter, a trades
unionist (you should see him go on strike),
a bushranger (you should see an indignant
bull-camel hold up a small town), a scent factory, a
disinfectant (when he is hot the smell of him would
kill almost anything except himself), a fighter accord-
ing to no recognised rules, a Sunday-school lesson
among those who never saw’ him, a standing example
of ingratitude, and a sort of bagpipes when he lifts
up his voice in real earnest.
* * * *
Young H. A. Parsons attempted to shave a
little off the 22-hour motor side-car record between
Melbourne and Sydney last week. Perched on a
7-h.p. Indian, with W. Jenkins as travelling com-
panion, he lost an hour and a half through car-
buretter troubles on the run to Seymour. With a
fresh carburetter, however, he licked up the deficit
at Albury after covering' 33 miles of fearful road
near Euroa in a trifle under the hour. In the Ma State
he knocked minutes off the previous best up to 28 miles
of the Harbor; then the axle of the side-car broke
and the rest of the hurry had to be abandoned.
Parsons nromises another offensive on the record soon.
*' « * *
.Tack Worrall, who is having a hard battle
these times with a “leg,” can look back to the days
when he was the greatest of Victorian footballers
and a front-rank cricketer. And what he knew he
unselfishly passed on as coach to schools and clubs.
It is doubtful if there is anyone in Australia who
has done more for the two branches of athletics,
Twenty years ago John was the daddy of rovers in
premiership football; at cricket he captained Vic-
toria to victory in the Sheffield Shield, and tripped
with the ’BB and ’99 Australian Xl’s. His biggest
innings was 417 not out (out of 992) for Carlton
against University in ’96. Worrall’s pluck and cour-
age won many matches. 1-Ie left his 55th year behind
last week.
* * * #
A recent writer about the Papuans ex-
plains that when Brown Brother started playing
cricket he did it 200 a-side. Why not? If the
plan w’ere adopted in Australia the game might get
a new lease of life. There would be no need for
playing out premierships with several rounds. And
with half a hundred good solid bats at work the rest
could get away to the Front and be back before they
were wanted. The effect of an Australia v. England
rubber on these lines would be to add permanently to
the population, because it would take at least six
years to play off, and no Englishman would want to
break up his home and go back to find he was a back-
number.
* * * *
Burly Jim Stanbury felt the fire of youth
burning last week and tried to convince the military
medical officers at Sydney that 50 years and 18 stone
would be useful. But, like many another good sport,
he was passed out. It is 27 years since James
defeated John McLean for the world’s sculling cham-
pionship on the Parramatta River. Nowadays he is
as extensive in superficial area as three ordinary
citizens, and looks as if he was still growing.
* * * *
With flags half-masted and black arm-
bands worn for many a comrade gone down in that
greater game, Vic. League footballers started the
season on Saturday. Two more clubs—St. Kilda and
Essendon—decided that the games are little, if any,
hindrance to recruiting; only Melbourne now stands
out. Crowds were moderate and unusually courteous,
especially at Richmond, where umpire Nugent took
up the thankless job again. Nugent is now a Cap-
tain and M.C., and the barracker who loses his.block
over him will probably be handed some well-chosen
insults to think over. Collingwood celebrated its
silver jubilee by hoisting last season’s pennant and
emphasised the occasion by walloping its ancient
enemy Carlton by 65 to SO. As the Saints haven’t
played since ’l5, ’Roy, last year’s runners-up, were
considerably shocked at having to return from the
seaside leaving the thick end of the stick behind —
St. Kilda 40, ’Roy 34. Next Saturday the Ass.
resumes operations, and barrackers will get a
chance to break their two years’ silence. Wherefore
the uproar will resemble the bellowing of the Bulls
of Bashan, from Footscray even unto Preston and the
parts beyond.
* * # *
The final of the Melbourne public-schools
race on the Yarra last Saturday was rowed under
quite unusual conditions. As a rule, the crew that
draws the south bank has the advantage, and only a
direct north wind can give them the worst of the
luck. The Wesley boys got the outside berth this
year, and an untimely north wind handicapped them
a bit —a considerable bit, seeing that the eight
Wesleyans averaged 81b. per man less than the Mel-
bourne Grammar and 111 b. less than the Geelong
Grammar lot. Nevertheless the Melburnians can be
reckoned to have won on their merits, though with
positions reversed they wouldn’t have finished 24
lengths ahead. The first heats, on the Friday’, seemed
to give them a rather better show than either Wesley
or Geelong Gram. The neat-styled Wesley boys were
not fully taxed in beating the weak Xavier crew,
and Geelong Grammar didn’t make a very easy job
of beating the unfancied Geelong College, whereas
Melbourne Gram, had to row a hard race against
Scotch College, and kept well together in doing it.
The final lost some of its interest when Geelong was
seen to be in difficulties just after rounding the turn.
Somebody caught a crab and the boat practically
stopped for a couple of seconds. In getting within
half a length of Wesley at the finish the visitors
proved to be making good use of their big pull in
the weights.
* m *• * *
“Le Noy”: Somewhere in Western
Bananaland are two hills. At 500yds. from one
hill there is a small creek where ’roos go regularly
for refreshments. From the hilltop 500 yards from
the creek ideal ’roo-shooting is possible, without
changing one’s position. My mate, an expert shot,
using a .303 military rifle sighted at (say) 531
yards, has always hit in a direct line with the ’roo,
but about 10 yards short. Though the sight has
been adjusted accordingly, a direct hit could never
be wagered on. But when changing positions with
the ’roo he has scored a direct hit every time with
the sight at 531 yards and the ’roo just clearing the
hilltop. No readjustment of the sight has ever been
necessary. Can any ’roo-shooter tell me if all this
was due to gravitation and whether there is any
rule in regard to sight-adjustment when shooting
from a higher to a lower level?

“Ironbar” : Here’s a bigger eel than the
one caught in the Manning (N.S.W.) which weighed
181 b. when dressed, and was 4ft. 2in. long. My
candidate was caught in a very small stream on the
South Coast by Joseph Ralph, now on active service.
It weighed 251 b. and measured sft. 3in. Another
was dragged out which went 18jlb. Some of these
large fellows when opened are found to contain full-
sized rabbits.
* * * *
“Orrie Cowie”: “Douglas T.” is quite
right when he votes for sudden death for the cor-
morant or shag as a fish-eating pest. Near Port Pirie
(S.A.), opposite Mount Ferguson, there is a small
inlet known as Shag Creek, where there are hundreds
of thousands of cormorants. Many times I have
taken parties for an afternoon’s slaughter amongst
them, and all seemed amazed at the sight which met
their gaze. We have frequently accounted for from
500 upwards with pea-rifles, and in nine cases out
of ten when the birds were hit they disgorged a
silver whiting from nine inches to a foot in length.
Until recently the S.A. Government paid a bonus of
Id. per head for the gorgers. As for “Leo Lear’s”
defence (B. 2/5/’18) that the birds deserve a verdict
in their favor owing to their destruction of the
yabbie pest, let me tell him that there are no yab-
bies within hundreds of miles of Port Pirie.
* * * *
“F.N.T.”: The steam-driven motor-car,
according to “Fifth Wheel” (B. 9/5/’18), has
“arrived” in the U.S.A. Interesting to note that
Australia’s first motor-car, built by Herbert Thom-
son, of Armadale (Vic.), was steam-pushed. Fur-
ther, Melbourne’s pioneer small cars, imported by the
same Thomson, were motor-buggies, using kerosene
fuel, as do the latest types. Dr. Bull, of the Uni.
Bacteriological School, drove one of them during its
occasional intervals of good health. The first large
steam-car imported to Melbourne was owned by vet.-
surgeon Adeney, of Kew. It was produced by the
White Co., of Cleveland, which concern long ago
switched to the internal-combustion engine. The
great difficulty in those days was the making of
small parts strong enough to stand the racking
strain of the road. When a steam-car baulked by
the wayside it generally meant a long subsequent
visit to the repair-shop. The matter of the water-
supply, too, was a serious consideration, as the stream
was used but once, and only a small tank could be
carried. Yet what speed and smoothness and driving
ease while the jigger was jigging 1

Public Notice.
CUTLERS' COMPANY.
SHEFFIELD
ADVISORY COMMITTEE.
A few years ago the Sheffield Cutlers’
Company, being aware of the great damage
done to the City and Trade of Sheffield by
the improper use by unscrupulous Manufac-
turers and Merchants in all parts of the
World of the name “Sheffield” upon goods
not manufactured there, established a Fund,
with the assistance of the City generally,
amounting to about £lO,OOO, to enable action
to be taken to suppress such frauds.
The Sheffield Advisory Committee, which
administers this Fund, has already taken
action through the Courts, and in other ways
in various parts of the World, against
Manufacturers and Merchants who have
unscrupulously taken advantage of the repu-
tation of Sheffield by using the name of
Sheffield upon their goods.
The Cutlers’ Company desires to bring the
existence of this Fund before the notice of
Manufacturers and Merchants all over the
World, and to say that they will commence
proceedings, without hesitation, against any-
one who illegally uses the name of Sheffield
upon goods which are not manufactured in
that City, upon proof being furnished to
them of the perpetration of such fraud.
The Cutlers’ Company will be grateful if
anyone in doubt as to the honest origin of
goods supposed to be of Sheffield manufacture
will at once communicate any information in
their possession to the Law Clerk at the
Cutlers’ Hall, Sheffield, England, so that
the matter can be fully investigated.
(By Order)
R. T. WILSON, Law Clerk.

This co., which got into rough water and had
to jettison half of its capital (represented
by goodwill and frittered leasehold items),
feels better now. Still, a large part of the
capital of £7OOO consists of perishable
assets, the leaseholds of Paris House and the
adjoining Balfour Hotel. When the leases
run out these assets will, of course, dis-
appear; so in addition to paying dividends
the profits have to provide for a return of
capital. This is how things have moved
since the clean-up :—-
Sept., 1914. Mar., 1918.
Liabilities .. .. •• £8,297 £2562
Leases, plant, etc, .. 11,807 5827
Liquid assets .. .. 6,308 5082
If the unexoired portion of the leases is
worth all the balance-sheet says it is, and if
full allowance has been made for deprecia-
tion of plant, etc., then for every 10s. share
there are assets worth 11s. 3d. after provid-
ing for all liabilities and paying the current
dividend. Excluding the leaseholds and plant
the net assets are worth 6s. Bd. per share.
Future earnings, however, are uncertain.
The co. sub-leases its restaurant; the rest of
its profits come from the Balfour. And the
advance in the wholesale prices of spirits
without any increased charges to the public
is knocking holes in liquor profits: •

Some of the profit credited to the past
year really belongs to its predecessor, for,
owing to a delay in appraising, the co. had
12,442 bales unsold and in store as at March
PI, 1917, which was 10,000 bales in excess of
the previous carry-forward. Of the past
year’s consignments (55,134 bales). 7358
bales were still awaiting appraisement when
the financial year closed. Glance down the
“bonus to clients” column in the table and
you will see what the co-operative side of
the co. has meant to the primary producer.
But it does more than is shown by those
figures. The co. makes no receiving charge
for wool; and it collects lj p.c. commission
all round, thus putting the little mail on the
same footing as the big one. The rebates
on the selling charges—3o p.c. on wool and
20 p.c. on other produce —have returned over
£46,000 to producers in the past 10 years,
and taking into account other allowances the
saving is considerably more than that. In
one recent five years the Co-operative calcu-
lated that it kept £30,000 in the pockets of
its wool clients.
Gross earnings last year. £29.920, were a
record, due principally to the high price paid
for the wool-clip; and higher prices mean
bigger commissions. Still the growth of the
business is not as brisk as might be expected.
Capital is £40,000. with reserves of an almost
equal amount. Into its Pyrmont, freehold
the co. has put £71,357. there is. £lO.OOO in
War Loan, and a further £lllO in cash and
odds and ends. The co. owes £193,624, and
on the other side is owed £194.385 —but
these figures are quite abnormal and no doubt
involve a lot of money in connection with the
Imperial purchase scheme.
* * * *
Papua still Gills to the prospector. J. H.
Sloane and two mates returned lately from
Domara, on the south coast, after prospect-
ing several branches of the Musa River. No
reward claim has been applied for, but the
prospectors brought back 660 z. gold obtained
by sluicing Puasia Creek, which is five days
north of Domara. The gold was a good
sample, the largest piece weighing soz. 7dwt.
Four days from Puasia another creek was
found to carry gold, and 23dwt. was obtained
by panning.
* * * *
A reciuest has been made to the West-
ralian Government to insist on the value of
all gold yields being declared at the time the
crushing is announced. Pure gold is worth
within a fraction of £4 ss. per oz., but if the
bullion contains silver, zinc, copper or im-
purities of any kind the value shrinks.
Joburg mill gold is worth about 755. per oz.;
cyanide gold only 60s. Kalgoorlie battery
bullion used to be worth about 80s., but at
Coolgardie it was worth only a shade over
705., and sometimes shillings below that. The
value of the bullion from some of the other
fields was often lower still. Nowadays when
a crushing of so many., tons for so many
ounces is announced there is nothing to show,
unless the value is given, whether the gold is
worth nearly 855., 60s. or 50s. This may
mean all the difference between profit and
loss.

SIXTIETH REPORT.
Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary Meeting on 30th April, 1918.
The Directors have pleasure in placing before the Shareholders the annexed Balance Sheet and State-
ment of Accounts for the half-year ending '2Bth March, duly audited and certified.
The profits, after providing for a bonus to the staff, amount to .. .. £25,255 13 2
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz.: —•
To pay a dividend at the rate of 8 per cent, per annum
Transfer to the Reserve Fund .. • •
And to add to the amount carried forward from last balance
.. £12,000 0 0
.. 10,000 0 0
3,255 13 2
£25,255 13 2
The balance carried forward from last half-year, £8461 18s. 6d., with the above addition, £3255
13s. 2d., will amount to £11,717 11s. Bd,, from which it is proposed to transfer to the Reserve Fund a
further sum of £lO,OOO, leaving a balance to be carried forward of £1717 11s. Bd. The Reserve
Fund will then amount to £300,000.
During the half-year we have sustained a severe loss by the death of our valued Director, Mr.
William G. Sharp, and since the close of the half-year a further vacancy on the Board has been created
by the resignation of the Hon. Sir William H. Irvine, on his appointment to the position of Chief
Justice of the Supreme Court.
To fill the first vacancy, the Board, in accordance with the Articles, have appointed Mr. Edward
Norton Grimwade, of Messrs. Felton, Grimwade and Co.; and, in place of Sir William Irvine, Mr. Alfred
Ernest Bright, of Messrs. Gibbs, Bright and Co.
By order of the Board, H. T. WILSON, General Manager.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character ) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped,
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

“II. Horsecollar”: If recruiting can be
helped by raising a kilted regiment as pro-
posed in Sydney—why, let us have a dozen
kilted regiments! Very very few people in
Australia ever wore a kilt except once in a
thousand years as fancy dress, and it is a
great rarity even in Scotland. In fact,
most of Scotland’s population never wore it
even in the old, dead, romantic centuries.
Still the soldier can easily learn to struggle
into the unaccustomed garment which ex-
poses the knees to frost, sunburn, dog-bite
and ants, and the preliminary bashlulness
when the clo’ flies up in a high wind is a
temporary emotion. But how r about a
Binghi brigade—dyed, of course? In the
G. Washington v. George III. war a corps
of painted white irregulars—the Blue-eyed
Indians —bought on the British side, so there
is precedent to go upon.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free. 1/3. Free sample posted.

HOW TO STOP PAIN IN STOMACH.
THE VALUE OF MAGNESIA AND HOT
WATER.
Generations of people have employed hot
water as a soother of pain in the stomach,
but modern physicians and specialists have
discovered and used successfully an addition
which renders'the hot water trebly effica-
cious. Hot water is still recommended in all
cases of stomach pain, from whatever cause
arising, but still better results are obtained
if a little bisurated magnesia is added. Hot
water attracts blood to the painful part, and
the bisurated magnesia neutralises the acid,
which is usually the underlying cause of the
pain. That is why such excellent results
follow combination of the two. Readers who
are subject to pain in the stomach, especially
after eating, should try this hot water and
magnesia recipe. It renders dieting quite
unnecessary, for by slowly drinking half a
glassful of hot water containing half a tea-
spoonful of bisurated magnesia immediately
after meals, the dangerous stomach acids are
destroyed, and food remains bland and sweet
until perfectly digested. The bisurated mag-
nesia, which is extensively used in hospitals,
can readily be obtained from chemists every-
where ; but to avoid confusion with the
acetates, citrates and oxides of magnesia or
equally unsuitable mixtures of bismuth and
magnesia, it is important that bisurated'
should be asked for very distinctly. —Advt.

“Smike”: Owing to the fact that the
heavy scenery of “The Masquerader” will
take four or five days to shift to Sydney
under present transport arrangements, the
Taits have been compelled to consider
another attraction as an alternative to keep-
ing the theatre shut up for a week. It is
for that reason that one of Post’s former
successes. “The Nigger,” is being rehearsed,
though “8.J.” suggests (B. 9/5/T8) that it
will only be put on in the event of “The
Masquerader” failing. “The Nigger” will
probably go on in any case, unless the Taits
consider it cJumper to hand over a bagful of
rent for nothing.
“Peter”: Lionel Belmore, who buttles
strenuously in “The Masquerader” at Mel-
bourne King’s, must be one of the most
experienced pros, we have ever had in these
parts. He was 15 years with Irving, eight
with Wilson Barrett, two with Olga
Nethersole, and 12 months with Lily
Langtry, and he has also served under
William Faversliam, the Shuberts, the
Frohmans and several other managers. He
can look back on 40 years or more of grease-
paint, and has crowned his long career by
“directing” some of the biggest stars in the
movies.
Bailey and Grant have taken over the act-
ing rights in
“G rump y,” and
will tour that
audacious . comedy
through towns un-
trodden by the
stylish boot of
Cyril Maude, with
Bailey in the star
part. Herbert’s
treatment of
Grumpy should be
an interesting con-
trast to Maude’s
picture of a dod-
dering solicitor who
was alternately
senile and alert ;
wherefore when he
has finished with
the small towns he
will find that the
cities where
Grumpy boomed
last year will be
“De Luxe Annie," at
Sydney Palace.

ready to see it again under the new con-
ditions.
“0.K.”: Lionel Belmore, the Brock in “The
Masquerader,” is a son of George Belmore, a
famous London comedian. Belmore did
notable things in his time. He was a remark-
able Silas Wegg, the wooden-legged impostor
in “The Golden Dustman,” an adaptation of
“Our Mutual Friend.” Nat Gosling, the
horse-trainer in “Flying Scud,” was another
of his parts, and he was picked to play the old
boat-builder in “Formosa,” at Drury Lane,
when the original Dion Boucicault produced
that first of the many melodramas in which
the demi-monde loomed large. He was asso-
ciated with Henry Irving at the outset of the
latter’s long reign at the Lyceum. Irving
played Jingle in a potted version of “Pick-
wick.” and George Belmore was Sam Weller.
Subsequently, when Irving appeared as
Charles the First in Will’s poetic drama,
wherein the King was heroised, the old
comedian assumed a solemn face with warts
on it, and played Oliver Cromwell. Daisy
Belmore, who came to Australia with Wilson
Barrett, was a sister of Lionel; her voice
w r as as thin and shrill as Lionel’s is thick
and fruity.
Fred Collier, the Melbourne basso now
managing weekly Auditorium concerts for
Chappell and Co., is a nephew of Collier, the
old-time Australian actor. In the days when
"Uncle Tom's Cabin” was a power on the
stage Collier was the representative Simon
Legree; and in the domain of poetic drama
he was an Othello of some class. Nephew
Fred’s one bid for dramatic honors was when
he sang the part of Mephisto to Melba’s
Marguerite at a Melbourne matinee perform-
ance. v

houses.

“.T.F.TI.” from London :
On March 11 “Romance” reached its thousandth
consecutive performance at London Lyric amid mu Oh
enthusiasm, congratulation and generous distribution
of artistic souvenirs. There is no previous instance
of a serious play achieving such a score in London,
although of course the record has been exceeded by
things of the “Charley’s Aunt” and “Private Secre-
tary” order. Yet “Romance” was at first received
by the dramatic critics with coldness, and for the
first week or two it looked like failure and finish.
But Doris Keane was a firm believer in its ultimate
success, and she steered it through its early troubled
waters to packed houses and busy box-offices. During
its thousand nights in London she has been absent
only half a dozen times. She has, in fact, been play-
ing the heroine for five years, with very brief breaks,
appearing in it first at the Maxine Elliott Theatre,
N’York, in February, 1913, On the same night that
“Romance” reached its thousand at the Lyric, Oscar
Asche and Lily Brayton were rejoicing with their
friends and a densely-thronged His Majesty’s over
the 700th performance of glittering “Chu Chin
Chow.” It also will certainly achieve its thousand
in due course unless the nitrate king who recently
purchased His Majesty’s should get sick and tired of
its phenomenal prosperity, and discover some legal
method of bundling Oscar and Lily off the premises.
So popular is the Oriental show that Oscar and Lily
aer now playing it twice daily, with every prospect
of being compelled to do so until Christmas is well
in sight.
Found drowned last week in the Macquarie,
at Dubbo (N.S.W.), Jimmy Ashton, founder
of Ashton’s Circus and father of a whole
tribe of sawdust performers. He was
travelling with Cole’s Circus when the Ring-
master called him out. Death came very
simply. Apparently he leaned down to the
river to drink, fell forward and couldn’t
recover himself. The body was found half
submerged, with the legs resting on the
bank.
Lalla Miranda has joined the Carl Rosa
Co. in London.
Sylvia Bremer, now a film star in the
U.S.A., has become Braemar.

“Private Boxer”: The Firm is going to
stage a new war comedy by one of the
authors of “The Man Who Stayed at Home’’
ai; Melbourne Theatre Royal on June 1. Its
title is “General Post,” but it has no con-
nection with the Taits’ prop at the King’s.
“O. Blin”: “Dr. Nil” (B. 2/5/H8)
appears to be incredulous regarding the
theatrical performances at the Ruhleben
camp near /Berlin. He needn’t be. The
Ruhleben Theatrical Co. started with Shaw’s
“Androcles and the Lion” —-an ambitious
project which was almost wrecked by the
scarcity of lions and ladies. John Roker,
an English pro. who before the war was
“producing” at the Metropol Theatre, Ber-
lin. is the mainspring of the machine.
Puccini is at work on three short operas,
comical, tragical and sentimental respec-
tively. The three pieces are to be produced
together. It is to be hoped that they w T ill be
properly labelled, so that the critics won’t get
them mixed.
Guy Bates Post, congratulating Aus-
tralians on their felicitous position in the
dramatic world, says : “Other countries have
to take the bnd with the good. After the
sifting out, Australians get the pick of the
successes, without being troubled to any ex-
tent by the failures.” That is all very well,
but Mr. Post cannot understand the. craving
Australia has, after a surfeit -of these “good
plays,” to see a few of the really bad ones.
London’s newest manager, .T. L. Sacks,
who has taken over the Empire in Leicester
Square and made a good start with a
sprightly musical comedy, “The Lilac
Domino,” is one of the rare smilers upon the
friendless, unacted genius. He has opened
an office in Gerrard-street, where, he says,
“anybody shall be at liberty to come with
any sort of proposition, sure of receiving the
welcome of civil treatment.”
Despite the broadening of public opinion
brought about by recent productions of
“Ghosts” and “Damaged Goods,” the Rritish
Lord Chamberlain would not pass Ibsen’s
“Realities” without alterations. Austin
Fryers, who is responsible for the production,
claims that the changes “will interfere with
Ibsen’s original conception and remove in
some measure that inevitability of purpose
and action so essential to the development of
Ibsen’s theory of heredity.” “Realities” is a
sequel to “Ghosts.”
“J.F.H.”: A memorial to members of the
profession who have fallen in the war has
been unveiled at the Old Vic., South London.
This remarkable old barn had a long period
of prosperity as the Royal Victoria Theatre.
It was originally the Coburg, but when Queen
Vic. selected it for her first visit to a theatre
after ascending the throne, it was rechrist-
ened. It is now the Royal Victoria Hall,
and presents plays, generally Shakspeare,
two nights of the week, opera two nights,
and lectures two. Popular prices—three-
pence gallery, shilling stalls. The memorial
which has been set up in this historic house
was carved out of a fragment of the old
battleship Britannia, blessed by the Bishop
of Southwark, and orated over by General
Ashmore.
The son of the late Antoinette Sterling
was called up for home soldiering in London
a few weeks ago. He’s 41 and runs a sing-
ing school.
Margaret Anglin is one of the not very
many stars Australia has seen on the up-
grade. The forceful woman lately did a
season of Greek tragely in N’York and
scored heavily in “Medea.”
Guy Bates Post tells the Argus that
his impression is that the church and the
stage till very similar functions:—
This Post impression, many feel,
In truth is rooted deep ;
Both church and stage delight to deal
With things that are or seem unreal,
And both conduce to sleep!
The rationed Londoner now' gets en-
couragement to fast from a song “It’s Up
to You,” specially put out by the Food
Economy League. “The war will be won
on bread.” says the song; and “You must
lose a bit of dinner if you want to be a
winner.”
“J.F.IL”: London has been revelling in a
bustling week of peripatetic tanks that have
collected millions to help on the war. Marie
Bohr weighed in with a tidy £lO.OOO. She
sat each day in the vestibule of her theatre,
tlm Globe, selling war-bonds to all comers.
She sold £2OOO ? worth to a well-known
dramatist. £5OO to Sir Charles Wyndham,
£5OO to May Moore (Lady Wyndham), and
£5OO to Sir George Buchanan. Mrs. Kendal,
in whose touring company Marie started hev
English career, also called and invested.
Josef .Kretschman, the well-beloved old
Bohemian who taught two generations of
young Australians to extract music from the
piano and violin, professed to be a Christian
Scientist, bequeathed his purse to the Salva-
tion Army, and was buried in the Roman
Catholic portion of South Head cemetery,
prayers being read by a Lutheran pastor.
There was nothing narrow about delightful
old Kretschman.
“Fax’’: The latest story re the Kaiser’s
“dippiness” leaves me unconvinced. William
is and always has been, plainly obsessed with
the idea of world-conquest, and in a quite
natural German spirit he talks of God as
bis partner. The Huns expect it of him.
His speeches, as Emil Reich explained in
“Germany’s Swelled Head,” written years
before the Vwar, are just what his people
want. But it isn’t likely that he is suffer-
ing from any more delusion than all ros r al
heirs-apparent are bred and bound to enter-
tain concerning their distinguished selves.
If he ever goes stark staring mad he will, in
a natural state of mental revolution, regard
himself as a starving beggar or a crushed
worm. Hallucinations run by the rule of
contrary.
THE PRECIOUS POSSESSION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How Leslie Stormer scorned the press
I’ve often heard him say.
“They cannot mar a man, much less
Can they assure a fool success,
These creatures of a day !
"I scorn their praise, despise their blame,”
Said Leslie once to me.
“What know the scribblers of our game?
1 give the paper to the flame
Whate’er it print of me.”
Then came the fire at his hotel.
He saved himself, but wore
The scantest garb, lie stood a spell,
Then turned again, and with a yell
He dashed in at the door.
It seemed a splendid act. They thought
Of some dear child forgot,
And through the smoke once more be fought;
lint no sweet maid or babe lie brought—
He fainted on the spot.
The rescued tiling they gently took
From fingers reft of life;
The cro\Vd pressed pagCrly to look:
He'd saved his fat “Press Cuttings” book—
A fireman saved bis wife!
VICTOIIIA.
STARGAZER.
FLORAL TRIBUTES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With a haunting, Mona Lisa smile,
A smile she’d rehearsed for hours,
The star bowed low as the juvenile
Collected a stack of flow’rs.
She traversed the house with a beaming eye,
Elate with her laurel crown,
And she flung a kiss to the gods on high
As the curtain rustled down.
But her smile dissolved when the curtain fell,
And her eye betokened rage;
She strangled a temperamental yell,
And fled from the flow’r-strewn stage.
Then the prompter questioned the juvenile
As he dabbled Jug harassed brow,
“In the name of all that’s imbecile
What’s rattled the old girl now?”
Said the juvenile: “A cruel slight
Has been offered her once again,
She only got nine bouquets to-night,
And she ordered and paid for ten!”
N.B.W.

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdkll, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
tp kill the hair roots permanently. For this
purpose pure powdered phemlnol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair-
growth. The recommended treatment is
. designed not only to instantly remove the
hair, but to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Removing Her Skin.
The quickest and surest way to make' a
good complexion out of a bad one is to
actually remove the thin veil of faded or dis-
colored outer skin from the face. This can
be done easily, safely and privately by any
woman. The process is one of gentle absorp-
tion.
Get some pure inercolised wax from your
chemist and apply it at night like cold cream,
washing it off in the morning. In a few
days the mercolide in the wax will have
absorbed the disfiguring cuticle, revealing
the fresh young skin underneath; you will
have a beautiful, clear, natural complexion.
The process is pleasant, and harmless, making
(he face look brilliantly attractive and youth-
ful. It also effectually removes such blem-
ishes as freckles, tan. moth patches, liver
spots, pimples, etc. Every woman should
keep a little mereolised wax on hand, as this
simple, old-fashioned remedy is the best com-
plexioned benutlfler and preserver known,
Blaeklie&ds Instantly Go.
A very simple, harmless and pleasant pro-
cess is now used to remove blackheads and
correct greasiness and large pores in the
skin. You have only to drop a tablet of
sty mol, obtained from the chemist, into a
glass of hot water and bathe the face with
the liquid after the effervescence lias sub-
sided. The blackheads will then come right
off on the towel. The enlarged pores immedi-
ately contract to normal and the greasiness
disappears, leaving the skin smooth, soft and
cool and free from blemish. But to make sure
that this desirable result is permanent it' is
advisable to repeat the treatment several
times at intervals of say about four or five
days.
Grey Hair Unnecessary.
One need not resort to the very question-
able expedient of hair dye in order not to
have grey hair. The grey hair can easily be
' hanged back to a natural color in a few
days' time merely by the application' of a
simple, old-fashioned and perfectly harmless
home-made lotion. Procure from your cbem
ist two. ounces of tammalite concentrate and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this to the hair a few times with a small
"pongo, and you will soon have the pleasure
of seeing your grey hair gradually darkening
to the desired shade. The lotion is pleasant,
not sticky or greasy, and does not injure the
hair in any way.
Naturally Wavy Hair.
Good stallax not only makes the best possi-
ble shampoo, but has the peculiar property of
leaving the hair with a pronounced natural
“wave,” an effect presumably desired by many
ladies. A teaspoonful of the stallax granules
dissolved in a cup of hot water, is ample for
a luxurious head wash, and leaves the hair
with a peculiar lustre and fluffiness obtainable
from nothing else as far as known. It is per-
fectly harmless, and can be obtained from
most chemists, although since it has been but
little used for this purpose it comes only in
jib. sealed original packages, enough to make
up 25 or 30 individual shampoos. [2

when to take risks, banks on his
superior nerve to pull him through.
And if by daily habit and by simple
precautions he has kept his body fit
and vigorous always, you may be sure
his nerve will not fail him in any crisis.
Such men succeed where others dare
not even try to succeed.

ful of Kruschen Salts
in a tumbler of hot
water every morning
before rising clears the

cleanses the system
and promotes daily good health. You sleep

pleasure in the effort if only you keep your body fit
while your brain works. The Kruschen habit makes you fit and

Fry-Bacon. —On the 30th March, at Croydon,
William Fry, Athelstone, to Asinath Bacon, Henley

In his diary of Collins’s voyage of explora-
tion to Port Phillip, “Bobby” Knopwood
records that on June 11, ISO 3, Mary Wiggins,
wife of a marine, was delivered of a son. Old
Harry Wiggins, a son of that son, is still
living at Blackman’s Bay (Tas.). The marine
and his family went on to Hobart after the
few months spent by the expedition at Sor-
rento, and the Wigginses are now a numerous
clan in the Speck.
A notable Melbourne prize-winner is Kath-
leen, daughter of Mrs. Mclnerney, the auto-
crat of the National Council of Women, who
is the first of her sex to gain the Caroline
Kay Scholarship in Geology. It was up to a
woman to do this, having regard to the his-
tory of the prize, which was founded
in memory of the daughter of a British
admiral who married a young Scotchbyterian
parson against the wishes of her parents and
lived with him in the Western District for
many years. There was a reconciliation
before pa’s death, and the old sea dog left, her
£5OOO, which, when she went to join him,
was handed by the widower to the University
to found two scholarships.
“Melb”: The silver jubilee of the Central
Mission, Wesley Church, has just been
marked by what they call a reminiscence
meeting. The most extraordinary is about
the organ. It seems to have been made in
Europe in some far-away time. Then it was
in a Dublin music-hall. A South American
priest bought it for his church, and shipped
it in a vessel bound for Melbourne but touch-
ing at Itio. However, baffling winds kept the
ship from entering Itio, and a storm wrecked
her at Melbourne Heads. But the organ
came out all right, and the Wesleyans bought
it for £5OO.
“8.M.”: It happened at a flying-school
where there were a number of Australians,
myself included. One day we had a party of
much social and political weight to inspect
the ’planes. My duties were to tidy up,
unlock the doors and look impressed. By the
time the tour of inspection reached the larg-
est hangar, the camp Low had caricatured,
faithfully and cruelly, the whole party, on
the dark green doors. Also he had written
their names. As I fumbled with the locks,
the visitors had a good view of their portraits
in chalk. After the victims had gone the
O.C. had us all lined up in front of the pic-
ture gallery. “Who did this?” he asked in
his best court-martial voice. No answer.
“What ass did it?” he repeated. No
answer. “Very well then. All leave stopped
for a month, and you'll hear more of this.”
The silence could be heard for miles'. Then
the culprit, a good sport, stepped out. He
was the son of one of the titled visitors, and
the cruellest sketch was that, of his own dad.
We didn’t “hear more of this.”
A. J. Hannan, a clever Adelaide junior
lawyer, has raised a new point in the in-
quiry about land purchases by the S. Aus.
Government at. big figures. He did not have,
he said, to show that the valuation of the
Surveyor-General—whose conduct was
under inquiry at the time —was wrong.
“Even if the Surveyor-General had hit the
correct figure by good luck, his negligence
was just as great if it could be shown that
his methods were totally wrong.” But The
Bulletin remembers a remark of Edward
MacFarlane, formerly Under-Sec. for Lands
in N. S. Wales, and for many years a very
capable district surveyor and chairman of
Land Boards. “We lay down most unim-
peachable rules for calculating annual rents
and capital values; then we apply our rules,
and commonsense tells us in half the cases
that the results are wrong.

buster :—-
The death of the King of Tonga recalls the regime
of one of the most remarkable characters that ever
stalked through fact or fiction. Shirley Waldemar
Baker was a renegade missionary, who, when his
actions called for censure from his Board, and his
removal from Tonga was advised, conceived the
brilliant idea of advising the King to establish a
church of his own. He understood well the Tongan
character, with its love of pomp. As well as the
national church, he devised all the outward signs of
an important monarchy to gratify the old monarch.
He designed a national ensign, bought a gilt crown
and robes of royalty—velveteen and white rabbit skin
—and built an imposing wooden palace and a royal
chapel. Soon George Tabou I. became merely a
figurehead. Baker was supreme. He tangled the
unfortunate natives up with so many and such petty
laws that a dusky Adonis dared not rub noses with
his Venus in the dusk without fear of fine and im-
prisonment. Baker established himself and his
family at the palace, so that during his long absences
in Maoriland he had someone at the King’s elbow to
watch his interests. His most drastic laws were
usually framed in Auckland and imposed in his
absence, so that, on his return, the simple natives
would have got used to them. But the unwilling
subjects were not so simple. They began to hate the
despot and made attempts on his life. A son and
daughter of Baker’s were injured in the natives’
crude attempts to free themselves, and his savagery
in punishing without being sure of the offenders
raised such a revulsion among Europeans that they
threatened Baker and sent for the British High Com-
missioner from Fiji, then Sir Charles Mitchell.
Baker’s cunning secured him some respite; but he
did not keep his promises to the High Commissioner.
He thought leniency was weakness, became more
arrogant, drew pensions from the depleted treasury
for his injured‘Children, and even went the length of
hurling accusations at the High Commissioner. Two
years later, in 1890, he was offered protection on the
warship Rapid, until the arrival of a steamer, after
Sir John Thurston had conferred with the King and
the chiefs, and the doors of the palace had been shut
against him. Baker was forced to accept the position;
yet at daybreak the next morning he made an attempt
to force his way into the palace. He was met at the
gate by the King’s great-grandson and heir—the
King whose death has just been announced. The
infuriated Baker abused the youthful prince and
reminded him of his sojourns at the Baker home in
Maoriland. “You shall pay for your keep at my
house in Auckland!” was his parting shot. “You
have more than cut that out,” returned the boy, “for
the years you and your family have been living in
my grandfather’s house! ” Baker was paltry
enough to send the bill to the young prince. Need-
less to say it was never paid.

Never force children
to take ill-tasting medicines.
START when they are babies and give them
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy at the first
symptom of a cough or cold.
There will be no forcing them to take it—they
will ask for it for the kiddies just love Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy.
There are parents who to-day give this medicine
to their children because they remember how eagerly
they themselves took it when they were kiddies.
But this is not the only reason.
They know from experience that for the treat-
ment of coughs and colds, croup and whooping
cough, nothing equals Chamberlain’s Cough
Remedy.
It contains no narcotics and every mother should
be sure on this point about the medicine she
gives her child. Too much prominence cannot
be given to the danger of giving children
medicines containing opium or other narcotics
for many a little one has been permanently
affected by medicines of this description.

COUGH REMEDY
BE PREPARED.
Dike many other dangerous maladies diarrhoea often comes on unexpectedly and frequently
before the patient is aware of it some serious disorder has developed. During the warm
weather attacks of this kind are very frequent and often so swift in their results that life is in danger before the physician
can be summoned.
Those who have the interests of their family at heart keep a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy on
hand for immediate use in cases of this kind. It is without doubt the best medicine prepared for diarrhoea, dysentery and
other disorders of the bowels. If given at the first unusual looseness of the bowels the attack may be avoided but even in
advanced stages it is a quick and most effective remedy.
Never leave home without a bottle of Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy in your luggage. The excitement
incidental to travelling and change of water and food often bring on diarrhoea, colic and pains in the stomach for which
speedy relief must be obtained and Chamberlain’s Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy can always be relied upon to do this.

Legal Notices.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Chancery Division
1 of the High Court of Justice dated the 7th
February 1018 made in an Action in the Matter of
the Estate of ANDREW SELKIRK JACK deceased,
Jack v. Jack (1917 J. No. 771) the following Inquiry
was directed, namely:—
“(1) An Inquiry who. was the heir-at-law of the
Testator at the time of his death and whether such
heir is living or dead and if dead who by devise
descent or otherwise is entitled to such real estate of
the Testator as descended to such heir-at-law.”
Notice is hereby given that all persons claiming to
be entitled under the said Inquiry are by themselves
or their Solicitors on or before the 30th day of
September 1918 to come in and enter their names
and addresses in the book kept for that purpose in
Room 317 at the Chambers of Mr. Justice Sargant
and Mr. Justice Younger the Royal Courts of Justice,
Strand, London, England, and prove their claims or
in default thereof they will be peremptorily ex-
cluded from the benefit of the said Order.
Tuesday the 15th of October 1918 at 12.30 of the
clock in the afternoon at the said Chambers Room
No. 315 is appointed for hearing and adjudicating
upon the claims.
Dated this 15th day of March 1918.
CHAS. lIULBERT,
Master of the Supreme Court.
Note.— The said Andrew Selkirk Jack late of 72
Hough Street in the City of Chester and who died
on the 17th October 1915 was born in Scotland of
Scottish parents on the 9th June 1837 and is
believed to have died a bachelor. He had several
brothers the eldest of whom was Alexander Jack
born at Largo, Fife, Scotland, in 1828 and who
went to Australia between 50 and 60 years ago.
Another brother John Jack was born at Largo in
1830 and who is believed to have died before the
Testator. It is not known whether either brother
left any issue but persons descended from either of
them might claim to be interested in the real
estate of the said Alexander Selkirk Jack.
FIELD, ROSCOE & CO.,
36 Lincoln’s Inn Fields, London, England,
Agents for Joliffe and Hope, Chester, England,
Solicitors for the Plaintiffs.

DIETING TO KEEP WELL.
Some people are born with a tendency to
certain diseases. What they eat either
aggravates this condition or tends to correct
it.
Anyone can see the importance of a proper
diet, but human beings, unlike the lower
animals, lack the instinct that tells them
what to eat and what to avoid.
It is a fact that proper diet and a good
tonic will keep people well under most con-
ditions, and restore ailing folk to health
except in grievous diseases where the need
of a physician is imperative.
Start to-day by writing to the I)r. Wil-
liams’ Medicine Co., Box 594 G.P.0., Syd-
ney, for a free copy of book “What to Eat
and How to Eat.” It contains useful infor-
mation on diet and describes the tonic treat-
ment with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for indi-
gestion, nervous dyspepsia, troublesome con-
ditions of flatulency or gas on the stomach,
and discomfort after eating caused by lack
of tone in the digestive organs. Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People supply the
stomach with well-oxygenated blood, without
which good digestion is impossible.
So, besides dieting, begin a short treat-
ment of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to-day.
Your dealer sells them ; an improvement will
soon show in increased appetite, better
digestion, refreshing sleep and ouiet nerves;
but never waste time trying substitutes—
always ask for Dr. Williams’.— Advt.
Government Notices.
Department of the Navy,
Navy Office, Melbourne,
24th April, 191 S.
Special Entry of Naval Cadets in the
Royal Navy.
TTENTION is invited to the Common-
wealth of Australia Gazette of
25th April, 1918, in which appears a copy of
the Regulations for the Special Entry of
Naval Cadets to the Royal Navy for train-
ing at the Royal Naval Colleges at Osborne
and Dartmouth.
Candidates for this Special Entry must
be not more than 181 nor less than 171 years
of age on the Ist of June of the year in which
the examination is held. No nomination is
required.
Applications should be made to the Secre-
tary of the Admiralty, Whitehall, London,
S.W., not later than the Ist of April of each
year. A form of application to be filled in
by the parent or guardian of the candidate
may be obtained from the Admiralty, Lon-
don.
Copies of the Regulations may be obtained
on application to the Naval Secretary,
Navy Office, Lonsdale Street, Melbourne.

Minister of State for the Navy.

“Chickadee”: As boyish and modest a sol-
dier as ever returned with honor is a young
friend of mine who was recently invalided
home with a Military Medal. His proud
mother insisted that he should show it to his
rich uncle, and the lad, against his will,
consented. The avuncular right-thinker
gazed fish-eyed at the heavy bit. of silver on
its rich silken ribbon of red, white and blue,
and then said he: “H’m —very pretty. But
wait and I’ll show you my golf medals!”
Peterborough (till lately Petersburg), on
the line between Adelaide and Broken Hill
—it is where the Broken Hill line butts into
the East-and-West transcontinental —begins
to think that its water-supply may not be
above suspicion. It has carefully placed the
saleyards, the cemetery and the sanitary
depot all within the catchment area. The
hydraulic-engineer first called attention to
this coincidence, nobody on the spot having
apparently noticed it; and now the Director
of Chemistry in- Adelaide, after one long,
careful look at the resulting fluid, has no
hesitation in giving the opinion that it
shouldn’t be fed to the pigs.
Some of Adelaide’s best-known hotels have
been warned that they will have to argue in
June why the Licensing Bench should not
wipe them out. There is no suggestion
against them in particular, but the Bench
has come to the conclusion that there are
too many in the city. (The Full Court of
S. Aus. said the Licensing Bench had no
power to consider the question, but the
High Court says it has.) So about 30,
mostly those which do not specially cater
for boarders, will have to show that they
are reasonably necessary to the public. The
best known are the Norfolk Arms in Rundle-
street, the Napoleon in King William-street
and Ware’s Exchange in Ilindley-street.
The S. Australian courts will one of
these days be declared black or bogus, they
are always defying Costs and doing some-
thing sensible. A bar door has to be locked
at certain times. One was not; but as the
handle had been taken away the effect was
the same. A Victorian case has decided
that “locked” simply means “securely fast-
ened.” The Adelaide Licensing Court
thought the same, but asked the Crown
whether it would like to have the point
reserved; then it added that, as the offence
was merely technical, if there was a convic-
tion there wouldn’t be a penalty. The
Crown took the hint and declined to bother.
“0.L.”: The customary seasonal outcry
is now being raised in Melbourne against
the “ragging” of freshmen at the University.
Mostly the choice spirits who conduct the
“initiations” are second-year students, try-
ing to go one better than their own perse-
cutors of the previous year. It is a con-
temptible business from any viewpoint; but
the two outstanding features of the case are
(1) that the “raggers” aye mostly of mili-
tary age and (2) that they prudently re-
frain from molesting any freshman w'ho owns
a ready left. The mob spares no lame duck,
however.
The shambling Defence Department,
which has lost and been robbed of so much,
isn’t quite as dead as it looks. It had a
warrant-officer in Melbourne Criminal Court
last week. He had taken away openly the
following Departmental treasures: Part of
an old fowl-house; the wreck of a wheel-
barrow ; an overcoat of 42 b.c. ; a toothless
rake ; a gun cover; some old clothes. Judge
Wasley put it to the jury that, while a
timber-yard employee would be technically
wrong in taking a handful of sawdust, it
wouldn’t be sensible to make a criminal of
the man for doing it. The jury thought so too,
and the verdict of “Not guilty” was cheered
outside the court. The poor old D.D. is a
whale on watching the rubbish-heap and let-
ting the safe take care of itself. You see,
the Best People would never think of going
to the rubbish-heap.
The president of the N. S. Wales Insti-
tute of Architects has been telling us for
our discomfort that, apart from theatres,
the authorities have no control over the con-
struction of buildings so far as their fire-
resisting qualities are concerned. Yet most
fashionable tea-rooms are crowded up next
the stars, and in some cases the alternative
to the lift is a spiral staircase made of soft-
woods. One panic shout of “Fire!” and
over would go the unfixed tables and chairs
in the rush to the exits. In the president’s
expert opinion there could be a first-class
disaster in such places without a sign of
fire, while “the actual event would be a
holocaust.” Another temptation to the Devil
is the large residential flat with softwood
floors and stairways and projecting oriel
windows of softwood and stamped metal
—things which “seem almost specially
designed to facilitate the spread of fire from
storey to storey, forming in some cases
practically uninterrunted flues from the first
floor to the top.”
“George Hooligan”: The Defence Depart-
ment has its own system of economy. Offi-
cers in camp are entitled to mess allowance.
If an officer misses two or more meals,
thus saving the Department something, it
encourages him not to do it again by stop-
ping his mess allowance for that day. For
instance, if an officer, going out on week-end
leave, remains in camp until after lunch
on Saturday he is entitled to his meal
allowance for that day. If he goes out
before lunch and does not eat the food he
doesn’t get the allowance either. It’s the
same in the detail camps. I know a bright
youth whose home was near one of them.
He used to turn out bright and early in the
morning and have a breakfast there. If he
coul.l hang on till lunch-time he collected
3s. 6d. If, however, there was nobody to
play Donkey or some other favorite game
with, and he left before he had eaten an-
other Defence Department meal, he had to
forfeit 3s. 6d.-
