The Urchin.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TIRED of the city, and spent,
Drawn by a scent on the breeze,
Out of the turmoil I went,
Back to the birds and the trees.
Not as a stranger I drew
Nigh to them, spirits aglow,
But as the urchin they knew
Ages and ages ago.
Under the sun and the moon,
Drawn by that magical scent,
Feet keeping: time to a tune,
Onward and upward I went.
Questing for what I had known,
Seeking for something, in truth,
Known to an urchin alone,
Wise with the wonder of Youth.
There in the song of the birds,
There in the forest, to meet
Something that is not for words,
Wild and elusive and sweet.
Hither and thither I sped,
Pushing,my way through the trees —
Ferns underfoot, and o’erhead
Flowers and the murmur of bees.
Breasting the bracken, I woke.
Blooms of the branch and the vine,
Wattle and laurel and oak,
Myrtle and gum-tree and pine
Mantling the earth like a cloth,
Green lay the moss o’er all things ;
Butterfly, beetle and moth
Jewelled the noontide with wings.
Beautiful birds of the Bush
Thrilled me with sweetness again—
Iloneybird, magpie and thrush,
Redhead and robin and wren.
Ybt, ah, the pity to tell!
Gone was the something I sought,
Gone was the magical spell,
Youth with his wizardry wrought.
Common the gold and the green,
Splendid no more with a glow
Such as the urchin had seen
Ages and ages ago.
Tame were the ways that were wild.
Vanished their freshness and joy—-
Gone with the mind of the child,
Fled with the heart of the boy.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Pansy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
nMIIERE had been an episode in the
career of Lieutenant Gregory Wicks,
11. N., which that young sailor-man
never referred to without using language
that can only be described as discreditable tb
an officer and a gentleman—even in days
when the habitual expletives, adjurations and
objurgations of the quarter-deck were lurid,
obscene and blasphemous beyond measure.
If anyone else referred to it, his brown face
would take on a tinge of crimson, his laugh-
ing blue eyes harden, and a moment of great
danger to the facial beauty of him who had
the temerity to mention it would ensue. So
much did its recalling anger him that even
the relations existing between himself and
the friend of his bosom, Ensign Patrick Cal-
der of the 46th, were once, in danger of
violent rupture because of it, and the Settle-
ment was on the verge of being entertained
by a duel between these bright young orna-
ments of the sister services and the garrison
life of Sydney. He had long learned to
answer to the nickname of Pansy because he
had to; but it was extremely unsafe for any-
one to bring up the story that had led to its
universal bestowal upon him. However,
since the bones of the gallant Captain Wicks
have lain these many years near those of
good Sir John Franklin, within the white
wastes of the Arctic circle, the incident may
safely be recalled now.
Very briefly, this is the tale of the girl at
Gosport and Mr. Midshipman Wicks, of
H.M.S. Victory.
In the year 1803, when the flagship of
Admiral Lord Nelson was preparing for sea
at Portsmouth, at the commencement of her
most celebrated commission, Mr. Gregory
Wicks, officially described as one of the
“young gentlemen” of the famous old line-o’-
battleship—already nearly 40 years afloat —
was sent into the village of Gosport for the
purpose of securing recruits for the Naval
forces of Gfeat Britain, who might be will-
ing or otherwise. It was immaterial to the
fulfilment of Mr. Wicks’s orders whether
they were the one or the other. With him
was a loud-voiced boatswain’s mate named
Thomas Martin, who had a red chin-beard
and w'hiskers, and ten men of some length
of service in the Navy, considerable hardness
of heart and fist, and a sardonic humor that
was well adapted to the business of the press
gang. They were afmed with cutlasses and
stout oaken cudgels, whilst their command-
ing-officer had his pistols and a hanger. It
was raining dismally and was very dark
when they landed on Gosport Hard about
eight o’clock in the evening.
Two men Mr. Wicks left with the boat.
There is no martinet like the martinet of
seventeen, and he breathed awful threats of
terrible and vague portent ere he led the
others up into the village.
“We’ll be back at any moment,” he said,
“and may the Lord help ye, ye swabs,
if ye leave the boat an instant. If any come
offering ye grog, drive ’em away; for if I
smell it on ye, ’tis two dozen apiece for ye.
Ye’ll see the First Lootenant, my hearties,
an’ ’twill be to your disadvantage, I pro-
mise ye. So mind what I say!”
“Th’ young snippet,” growled one of
them as Mr. Wicks and his party faded into
the night.
“Martin,” whispered Mr. Wicks presently,
when they had penetrated a little way into
a narrow street, wherein candlelight gleamed
dimly through the wet diamond panes of
closed casements, and dark eaves overhung
the glistening cobble-stones shining dully in
infrequent patches where the light filtered
across them—“ Martin, do ye take the men
and beat up the inns—all except the Goat.
I’ll go there myself and see hAw the land
lies. Be round the place, back and front, by
ten o’clock, and when ye hear my whistle —
one short and one long—break in. I think
I’ll have a likely lad for ye.”
"Aye, aye, sir,” growled the petty-officer.
Mr. Wicks left them at a corner and turned
down a cross street.
It is sad to say that Mr. Wicks contem-
plated a measure of treachery that was
entirely out of keeping with his frank and
open nature. It may be urged in extenua-
tion that he was in love, and that his age
was seventeen years. No man ever falls in
love so fiercely as he is apt to'do at seven-
teen, no man is ever more buoyant or more
despairing or more unscrupulous than he can
be in the affairs of the heart that beset him
before he comes of age. Nothing can daunt
him, nothing soften his ruthlessness, nothing
temper his rashness. There is nothing in the
world but himself, his adored one, and the
passion that rends him.
Down at the next corner, red blinds and
a flickering oil-lamp in an outstanding
bracket marked the position of The Goat
and Compasses—a tavern sign which, as has
often been explained, has no Masonic signi-
ficance. From the doorway under the lamp
—which opened on to the leeside of the
house, as the wind blew that night—a broad
band of light made a swathe of brightness
across the muddy street. Mr. Wicks came
to" the door, and went in.
There was no one in the low-ceiled bar-
room when he entered, and he paused a
moment —a handsome stripling he looked, in
his midshipman’s uniform, with its strange
little glazed pot-hat—before he knocked on
the- counter. Sounds of low voices were in
the room behind, and he knocked again before
the door was opened and a girl came in.
“Oh, you darling,” she cried softly, as she
passed before the shelves of sparkling bottles
and toddy and punch glasses. “’Tis you,
dear —oh, I am glad !”
She was a fair girl, and a handsome one.
She had a jolly, smiling face, and a wealth
of flaxen hair, and red, full lips, and her
eyes sparkled.
“Come here,” she said, and when Mr.
Wicks advanced shyly to the counter, she
leaned across it and kissed him on the mouth.
“Dear boy!” she murmured as she drew her
face away.
“Pansy,” he said —“is he here?”
She laughed prettily.
“No, then —he’s not, you jealous baby. But
he was here. He’s gone now. Come inside.
Do. I’m all alone. He sails at daylight—
thank goodness!—but he’s coming in later to
say good-bye. But we’ll —we’ll ha’ done
before he comes,” she whispered, blushing. I
“We’ll ha’ done our love-making, Greg dear.”
“You’re sure he’s coming again?”
“Yes," yes. You know what a fool he is
about me. But we don’t want him now, do
we? You only want me, and I only want you
— sweetheart. Ye’ve torn y’r pantaloons,
Greg. I’ll mend ’em for ye.”
She kissed him again, as they went through
the door, his arm about her slender waist.
There was a warm fire in the little room—
red coals in an iron grate—and the girl took
a conical tin implement with a handle, filled
it with old ale from a little barrel, and set
it amidst the coals. Also, she emptied, unseen,
of Mr. Wicks, a small wliite paper of greyish
powder into the brew. Then she sat herself
upon the knee of the blushing youth, and
smiled into his eyes, and pressed her red lips
to his, and hugged him with her soft arms to
her soft bosom. And sighed. And languished.
And Mr. Wicks felt that he was the Very
Devil.
“And what is my boy about to-night, with
his pistols and his sword? I declare he
looks as though he meant murder!”
“Listen, Pansy, love,” said Mr. Wicks, hesi-
tatingly. “You wish to be rid of this fellow,
and your father objects to him? This Car-
michael, who persecutes you with his atten-
tion?”
She sighed, and glanced over Mr. Wicks’s
shoulder in the direction of a glass-topped
door. A red face was pressed to the glass,
and the grin upon it was almost* audible.
“Dear one —yes. How can you doubt it?
How happy I could be if ’twas but you and
me.” she whispered. “Oh, would that I were
rid of him!”
“You shall be rid of the fellow!” de-
clared Mr. Wicks valiantly. “This very
night. I mean to press him. He’ll ’ not
trouble ye again, my Pansy. Damme if he
will!” he swore loudly.
The grinning face behind the glass scowled
for a moment —then grinned again, as the
girl- nodded towards it with a smile.
“The d—d coal-porter, the impudent black-
guard ! He called me Sonny. We’ll teach
him better than that aboard his Majesty’s
ship Victory. Better than that. He’ll find
the skipper of a Leith collier doesn’t count
for much in his Majesty’s sarvice. I’ll show
him who’s who!”
She slipped from his knee, ran to the
grate, and lifted the ale from the glowing
coals. She poured it into a pewter mug, and
set it on the round table at his elbow.
“Drink it while ’tis hot, dearie. And
ye’ll really take James Carmichael away?”
“ ’Tis easy, love. He’ll be here again, you
say? At ten o’clock my men will be around
the house. I’ve but to blow my whistle. A
short and a long blast,”
“Oh —la. la, la!” she trilled. “He’ll make
a pretty* Jack Tar to fight the Frenchies,
won’t he! Come, drink our healths, yours
and mine ! Oh, you dear thing ! Don’t —you
mustn’t do that!”
Mr. Wicks took a pull at the warm ale,
and then another. Ten minutes later he was
fast asleep, bending over the table, his head
pillowed upon his arms. At ten o’clock, from
a room upstairs, a whistle sounded, and Tom
Martin, with the Victory’s men, broke ob-
streperously into the tavern —about the time
(Continued on next page.)

Most sprained joints take a long' time to
get well, which probably accounts for the
saying referred to above; but proper first-
aid measures, and treatment with Zam-Buk,
ensure the speediest possible recovery.
In serious cases foment the ankle (or
wrist, as the case may be) in very hot water
for some time, keeping up the heat of the
bath by constant additions. This will reduce
the swelling and lessen the pain. Then wrap
up in cotton wool and bandage firmly but
not too tightly. Later on cold water bathing
may be commenced, and Zam-Buk should be
gently applied to allay the inflammation.
This point accomplished, massage with Zam-
Buk is advisable. Rub the balm well in
with a circular motion, gently at first and
more vigorously as the patient can stand it.
These measures (and proper rest, of
course) will cure the worst sprain or strain.
Zam-Buk soothes the pain, and makes the
muscles and ligaments supple and strong
again. A pot should be kept handy in,
every home.

that James Carmichael and his wife, laugh-
ing and giggling, shoved a dinghy out on to
the harbor, and rowed cautiously towar-ds the
riding light of the Four Maries, of Leith.
That dingy craft, slipping her cable, drifted
down on the tide towards Spithead—and this
is what the boatswain’s mate and the press-
gang found in the little room behind the bar
of The Goat and Compasses.
Mr. Wicks, snoring and past waking, sat
in a straight-backed wooden armchair, his
legs sprawled out, and his glazed hat tilted
over his eyes. On the table beside him, a
bunch of artificial pansies and blue forget-
me-nots, .fashioned cunningly out of silk,
rested on a sheet of paper, on which was
written in a crabbed hand: “With love from
Pansy Carmichael.”
“D—n my heyes!” swore Tom Martin,
gasping. “Look at our young cockchafer—
oh, look at him! O my Gawd!”
The slumbering midshipman’s trousers
were missing, and, instead of them, he wore
white feminine garments, flounced and frilled.
* * * *
Ensign Calder grumbled exceedingly when
he was roused at four o’clock in the morn-
ing, on a bright dawn in January, 181 G, by
the adjutant of the 46th —the genial Cap-
tain Blake —and informed peremptorily that
he must proceed down the harbor in charge
of an armed guard, to go aboard the convict
transport Dreamland, which had anchored in
Watson’s Bay the previous evening, with a
tale of trouble and mutiny, and some prison-
ers, who were to be landed immediately. But
before the morning was over he was ready
td vow that no duty had ever before afforded
him such keen enjoyment.
At the gangway of the Dreamland he was
met by the Naval Officer, Captain John
Piper, who had half a dozen heavily-ironed
men awaiting, his coming. He explained that
the ship was bound to the Derwent Settle-
ment in Van Diemen’s Land, but had b«en
driven past the island and up the coast of
New South Wales by a succession of heavy
southerly gales. Her captain had called at
Port Jackson for water and provisions, and
to land six men who stood charged with
mutiny and the murder of the chief-officer
and a soldier. Also, that Captain Carmichael
would be glad if Mr. Calder would step into
the cabin and partake of breakfast. Mr.
Calder was nothing loth, and he and Captain
Piper walked aft, whilst the soldiers weye
handed down some refreshment in the boat.
Three-quarters of an hour afterwards Mr.
Calder came out on deck grinning. The
prisoners were already in the boat, and h 6
shoved off hastily. When he was a little
the ship, to the great scandal of
the coxswain, he roared with laughter, beat
his fist upon the gunwale, and, rocking to
and fro, in an ecstasy of pent-up mirth,
murmured chokingly, at intervals :
“O Pansy— O Pansy!”
H.M.S. Emu, of which ship Mr. Wicks
was First Lieutenant, lay at anchor in the
Cove, and ordering the coxswain to run
alongside her, Mr. Calder climbed the ladder
and enquired for his friend. With much
difficulty he composed his features and waited
on the quarter-deck. Presently Mr. Wicks
came forth.
“Pansy, old boy, I’m in the Devil’s own
hurry, but there’s a ship at the Heads, the
Dreamland, and a lady on board who knows
you. Wants you to run down without delay.
Ta, ta—must be off. Got some birds to put
in their little cages.”
“Knows me? Who is she, Patrick?”
“I —er —didn’t catch her name quite, but
she’s very charming. Better run down. See
you this evening. Come to my quarters.”
Mr. Calder’s grin almost startled the sen-
try on the King’s Wharf as the boat drew
alongside. Having delivered his prisoners at
the gaol in George-street, he hastened to
the mess, to acquaint other appreciative souls
with the joke against Mr. Wicks, R.N.
Mr. Wicks arrived at Watson’s Bay about
noon. Boarding the Dreamland he asked
for the master, and in a minute or two that
worthy Scot made his appearance.
Mr. Wicks started as he saw him. In
some vague fashion he seemed to recollect
his features, and was uneasily conscious of
the fact that such recollection afforded him
little pleasure—though he could not tell
himself why.
“Good mornin’ to ye, sir; an’ what may I
hae th’ pleasure o’ doin’ for ye?” inquired
the big, red-faced man with humorous eyes
and a perpetual smile that hovered about his
kindly features.
“Good morning, sir,” answered Mr.
Wicks. “My friend Ensign Calder, who
has been here this morning, brought me a
message that you had a passenger—a lady.
I think —who desired to see me. He did
not remember her name. Might I beg you to
acquaint the lady, whoever she may be, that
I am at her service?” he said, with a polite
bow.
“Week there’s but one leddy aboar-rd, an’
she’s Mistress Carmichael, ma guid wife.
’Twill be hersel’, nae doot?”
Mr. Wicks looked at him in a puzzled
fashion. The conviction grew upon him
that he had some unpleasant recollection of
the master of the Dreamland, but he could
define the sensation no further. He followed
the captain into the cabin.
Seated on a settee,' engaged in needle-
work. was a very plump and very fair lady,
who had blue eyes and a pretty red mouth
and who smiled and nodded, as Mr. Wicks
halted in amazement a few paces from her.
“Well, Gregory,” she laughed, “do ye not
remember me?”
“My God!” muttered Mr. Wicks. “You —
you —and him !”
He flushed angrily and turned to Captain
Carmichael. “Sir!” he rapped out.
“Nae, nae, young gentleman- -dinna fash
yersel’. Pansy, here, and I—we but desire
for tae make amends. Never before hae we
had th’ chance. When we came again to
PoT-rtsmouth in th’ Four Maries—twal’ year
agone—ye were at sea wi’ Lor-rd Nelson —
Heaven bless his brave name —an’ never till
th’ mor-rn hae we had wor-rd o’ ye. Not till
Cap’n Piper spoke yeer name at breakfast
did we ken ye Avere i’ New Holland. He
spiered o’ Mr. Calder —-a bonny young soger,
yon—hoo ye were, an’ th’ guid wife here
wheespers it mus’ be yersel’. Sit ye doon,
sir. Will ye no tak’ a wi’ drappie' wi’ us
and forgie us? ’Twas all fair. I was in
love, an’ ye was in love. Ye’d ha’ pressed
me, gin I hadna stolen a mar-rch upon ye.
Noo, wouldn’t ye?”
Mechanically Mr. Wicks laid his hand
upon the back of the chair that Captain
Carmichael pushed towards him. He was
dazed, he could not catalogue his emotions.
It came to him that he ought to be very
angry. He was very angry, outragedly
angry, with Pat Calder. He would fight
Pat Calder, d —n him if he wouldn’t —the
scurvy rascal, to land him into this! But
somehow, he could feel no real wrath with
this kindly couple. And then Mrs. Car-
michael laughed merrily.
“Gregoty,” she cried, “come and kiss me !’*
“Aye, mon—kiss an’ be frien’s!” laughed
her husband. “Faith ye’ve done it afore —
I’ve seen ye.”
He laid his big hand on Mr. Wicks’s
shoulder and pushed him gently towards his
wife. That buxom lady stood up and threw
her plump, sleeveless arms about his neck.
She hugged him shamelessly. When she
released him the big sailor gripped his hand
and wrung it.
“And see here, Gregory,” laughed Mrs.
Carmichael, “here’s yeer pants —I’ve just
mended ’em. What ha’ ye done wi’ what I
lent ye? We couldn’t leave you barelegged!
Why, I believe ye’d forgotten all about me
—even forgotten my name.”
Suddenly the humor of it struck Mr.
Wicks. He threw back his head and laughed
—a boyish, merry laugh.
“Forgotten your name, dear Pansy ! No,
by heavens I haven’t —I promise ye. I’ve
never been permitted to!”
“Will ye no help yersel’?” beamed Captain
Carmichael, pulling the cork from a squat
black bottle, and placing glasses on the
round table. “ ’Tis real Mountain Dew. Our
boy’s named after ye—he’s at schule tae
Edinbro’.”
And so—but it was fortunate for Mr. Cal-
der that duty took him to Parramatta early
that afternoon.
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
Big Medicine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BERT MAYHEW might swank for ten
years round the show-ring at War-
field with his bonny mare, harness
bought yesterday and sulky delivered that
very morning, and win enough blue ribbons
to tie up the whole art gallery ; but Bob and
Bill had had enough of the Show. So they
only turned up their peeling noses when the
turnout came bouncing round to the cheers
of the grandstand. As a matter of fact,
before Bert’s caravan danced into the ring at
all the boys had turned down the whole
Show in three-ply weariness and extra-gal-
vanised disgust.
Fed up? They were choking! Perhaps
they had started too enthusiastically, touch-
ing admiringly every bull’s fat rump, pinch-
ing every prize sheep’s cushioned fleece, and
almost singing at the high art in the pigs.
Now they had rejected without a vote the
whole of the produce exhibits as bragging
bundles and bags of rubbish.
But the train was three hours yet, and
they had to live on meanwhile.
They turned their backs on the ring finally
and dropped under a big gum. A pip that
threatened to become permanent crumpled up
their brown, seamed faces.
Neither spoke, for neither had anything to
say. Half an hour of this didn’t improve
them. So they crawled on their feet and
wandered towards the crowd.
They got among the sideshows. “Good
Lord, here’s the last thing on earth —a round-
about !” said Bob. They tried it at three-
pence apiece. Afterwards they got over
their giddiness leaning against a platform
where a stale and faded lady invited the
world to witness a free exhibition of con-
juring with an egg and a black bag. “Could
do it better,” Bill snarled, “with a poddy
and the separator.” The lady heard him.
“Clear out and camp a bit,” she hissed.
They saw a fellow playing soft games
with a paralysed snake. The appearance at
his side of a pretty half-caste girl with bare
legs promised better. But they were too far
gone for a lady’s glamor.
“Let’s try a cup o’ tea. It’ll fill up time,”
said Bob. But they did not smile back once
at the pleasant Red Cross lady, because the
jam sandwich didn’t make' up for the thin
tea.
“Can’t smoke here, I suppose,” grumbled
Bill.
“No hope,” returned Bob; “come on—l’m
sick o’ sleeping here.”
Outside they fell in with a shooting gal-
lery. “Try a shot, gentlemen,” shouted the
bright boss of the war zone.
“Keep it,” muttered Bill.
And the funeral passed on.
They lay up against the outer skin of a
crowd, and waited. After a few jerks they
found themselves on a spinning-jenny. The
few bob they tried went the way of all flesh.
It was a bit of a tonic, but not strong
enough. “Try again, gentlemen,” gurgled
the jenny man. They left the hospital as
bad as ever.
“Never again!” wailed Bill. “No more
shows for me. Only run a bloke in for a
seven-quid rig-out to boil in.” He mopped
his face of sweat and dust.
They sank down and down a hundred
fathoms of dark-blue misery.
Through their underworld a shout, sharp
as a shot, rang out from the platform of a
show twenty yards away. “Just goin’ to
begin. Two bob, and ’ere y’are. Bill Lucas
will chop against a local champion.”
The spirit moved a bit. “Cripes, a wood-
chop !”
“After the wood-chop five rounds be-
tween” —
Before he could name the gladiators, two
radiant spirits had floated into the big booth
without touching the ground once, and were
arguing at the ringside with a fat man who
seemed to know too much.
A t a quarter-past twelve, as Bob was try-
ing to get out of his new pants sg> as not to
discourage the beautiful crease down each
leg, he remarked. “Best ever.”
“Dinkum,” replied Bill.
W. PARION.
